Two Reasons for Spirits' Non-Attachment to Bodies
Al-Razi enumerates two causes for spirits' non-attachment to bodies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two possible reasons for spirits not being attached to bodies for management, and how al-Razi grounds his discussion in logic and revelation.

Core Idea
Spirits may avoid bodies either from supreme perfection or from utter weakness.

Al-Razi presents a logical division: when spirits are not managing bodies, it can be for two opposite reasons. One is exalted perfection, the other is debilitating weakness. He then acknowledges the limits of human reason and points to a Quranic verse as an indication. This lesson unpacks his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's rhythm come through.
Notice that al-Razi uses a very compressed style here.
2
Identify the two possibilities
The passage presents two opposite causes for non-attachment. Find each one and notice how al-Razi describes them.
The first cause is perfection and exaltation; the second is weakness and inability.
3
Reflect on the Quranic reference
Al-Razi cites Quran 53:26. Consider how this verse relates to the division he just outlined.
The verse mentions angels whose intercession avails nothing, which may point to the weak category.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وههنا بحث . فإن عدم تعلقها بالأجسام على سبيل التدبير والتصرف ، يحتمل أن يكون لكمالات ذواتها ، واستعلائها عن الالتفات إلى الأجسام ، ويحتمل أن يكون لضعفها في ذواتها وعدم اقتدارها على التصرف في شيء من الأجسام . فهذا بحسب القسمة محتمل . ومن الذي أحاط عقله الله وملكوته ؟ وإلى هذا القسم الإشارة في الكتاب الإلهي ، حيث قال : ( وكم من ملك في السموات ، لا تغني شفاعتهم شيئاً )
And here is an investigation. For the non-attachment of these [spirits] to bodies by way of management and disposition, it is possible that it is due to the perfections of their essences and their being exalted from attending to bodies, and it is possible that it is due to their weakness in their essences and their inability to dispose of anything among bodies. So this is, according to division, possible. And who encompasses with his intellect the kingdom of God? And to this division is the indication in the Divine Scripture, where He said: 'And how many an angel in the heavens! Their intercession avails nothing.'
Source pages p. 9

Commentary
This passage presents an investigation regarding spirits that are not attached to bodies for management. Al-Razi states that such non-attachment could be due to two possible reasons: either because these spirits are so perfect and exalted that they do not need to attend to bodies, or because they are too weak and lack the power to manage any body. Both possibilities are logically conceivable. He then remarks that human intellect cannot fully grasp the divine realm, and he cites the Quranic verse (53:26) which mentions that many angels' intercession is of no avail, alluding to the first category of spirits that are weak? Actually the verse is about angels whose intercession is not accepted, which might indicate that some angels are weak? Al-Razi does not specify which possibility the verse refers to; he just says the verse alludes to this division. The commentary should follow the text: first the two possibilities, then the statement of human ignorance, then the Quranic reference.

Key Terms
عدم التعلق
التدبير والتصرف
كمالات الذوات
ضعف الذات
الملك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All spirits that are not managing bodies are weak or inferior.
Correction: Al-Razi shows that non-attachment could be due to supreme perfection, not necessarily weakness.

Checkpoint
What are the two possibilities al-Razi mentions for why some spirits are not attached to bodies for management?
A) Weakness or unwillingness
B) Perfection or weakness
C) Strength or weakness
D) Divine command or prohibition

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi identifies two opposite reasons for spirits not being attached to bodies—perfection or weakness—and reminds us of the limits of human knowledge while pointing to scriptural indication.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a responsibility you are currently avoiding. Honestly ask yourself: is your avoidance because you feel too weak and incapable, or because you consider yourself too 'exalted' for that task? Which is more dangerous for your spiritual growth, and what will you do about it this week?
?
Al-Razi humbly says, 'Who encompasses with his intellect the kingdom of God?' In which area of your life are you most likely to assume you fully understand God's plan or wisdom? Where do you need to surrender your intellect and trust divine decree today?
?
Have you ever judged someone else for not managing something, assuming laziness or weakness? Could it be that they are actually engaged in something higher that you cannot see? How can you change your heart and words toward others who seem inactive or withdrawn?

The Second Category: Spirits Governing Bodies
Al-Razi defines the second category of spirits and its subdivision into celestial and elemental.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the nature of the second category of spirits in Al-Razi's classification and its two subdivisions based on the type of body managed.

Core Idea
Immaterial spirits are not detached; they govern bodies celestial and elemental.

Al-Razi continues his classification of spirits by describing the second category: immaterial substances that are not incarnated in bodies but are attached to them through management and disposition. He further divides this category into spirits governing celestial bodies and those governing elemental bodies, and notes the epistemic limits of human inquiry into possible other worlds.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read Al-Razi's definition of the second category and its subdivision. Notice the characteristic 'attached by management and disposition.'
Pay attention to the bracketed text, which may be a textual variant.
2
Identify the two subcategories
Find where Al-Razi divides the second category into two parts based on the type of body managed.
The division is between celestial and elemental bodies.
3
Consider the metaphysical note
Al-Razi mentions the possibility of multiple worlds and unknown spirits. Why does he bring this up? (Hint: to demarcate the scope of inquiry.)
This shows his awareness of broader possibilities beyond human knowledge.
4
Reflect on the epistemic limit
Al-Razi says investigation of other worlds is not possible. What implication does this have for the study of spirits?
It focuses the investigation on spirits that manage this world's celestial and elemental bodies.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما القسم الثاني . فهو الجواهر التي تكون مجردة في ذواتها عن الجسمية . والحلول في الجسمية . إلا أنها متعلقة بالأجسام على سبيل التدبير والتصرف . وهذا القسم ينقسم إلى قسمين لأنها إما أن تكون مدبرة للأجسام الفلكية ، أو الأجسام العنصرية . ولما ثبت بالدليل [ أنه حصل خارج العالم خلاء لا نهاية له ، ولم يثبت بالدليل ] أن العالم واحد ، بل ثبت أنه لا يمتنع وجود عوالم غير هذا العالم . وبتقدير ثبوتها ، فيحصل هناك من زمر الأرواح المقدسة ، ما لا يعلمها إلا الله تعالى ، ولا يعلمها البشر البتة . إلا أن البحث عن تلك الأحوال غير ممكن. فلهذا السبب اقتصرت العقول على البحث عن الأرواح المدبرة للأجسام الفلكية ، أو الأرواح المدبرة للأجسام العنصرية .
As for the second category: they are the substances that are immaterial in their essences and [free from] indwelling in corporeality, but they are attached to bodies by way of management and disposition. And this category is divided into two divisions: because they are either managers of the celestial bodies or the elemental bodies. And since it has been established by proof [that there occurred outside the world an infinite void, and it has not been established by proof] that the world is one, but rather it has been established that the existence of worlds other than this world is not impossible. And supposing their existence, there would be among the hosts of sacred spirits that which none knows except Allah Almighty, and humans do not know at all. But investigation of those states is not possible. For this reason, intellects have confined themselves to investigating the spirits that manage the celestial bodies or the spirits that manage the elemental bodies.
Source pages p. 9

Commentary
This passage defines the second category of spirits: they are immaterial substances (jawahir mujarrada) that are free from indwelling in bodies, yet they are attached to bodies for management and disposition. Al-Razi then divides this category into two types based on what they manage: celestial bodies (al-ajsam al-falakiyya) and elemental bodies (al-ajsam al-unsuriyya). He adds a metaphysical remark: it has been proven that there is an infinite void outside the world, and it has not been proven that the world is unique; rather, the existence of multiple worlds is possible. If such worlds exist, they would contain hosts of sacred spirits unknown to humans. However, since investigation of those states is not possible, human intellects limit themselves to studying the spirits that manage the celestial and elemental bodies only. Thus Al-Razi establishes the scope of the inquiry.

Key Terms
الجواهر المجردة
الجسمية
التدبير والتصرف
الأجسام الفلكية
الأجسام العنصرية
الخلاء
العوالم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All immaterial spirits are completely detached from bodies and have no relation to them.
Correction: Al-Razi clarifies that some immaterial spirits manage bodies without being incarnated in them.

Checkpoint
According to Al-Razi, into what two subcategories is the second category of spirits divided?
A) Celestial and elemental
B) Good and evil
C) Human and non-human
D) Incarnated and non-incarnated

Key Takeaway
The second category of spirits are immaterial managers of bodies, divided into celestial and elemental, and human inquiry is limited to these.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Al-Razi says that there may be countless worlds with sacred spirits unknown to humans. When you think about the vastness of God's creation, does your heart feel awe or does it shrink into a comfortable box? What would change if you truly believed that what you see is only a fraction of reality?
?
You just read that some immaterial spirits manage celestial bodies and others manage elemental ones. Have you ever thanked the spirits (angels) that God has appointed to run the universe—even the sun that rises every day? If not, why do you neglect acknowledging such blessings?
?
The passage argues that human intellect is limited to investigating only what is accessible. Where in your life have you overstepped that boundary—trying to figure out mysteries God has kept hidden—while ignoring the obvious spiritual realities that you are meant to manage, like your own soul and its connection to your body?

Celestial Spirits and the Human Soul Analogy
Al-Razi explains the analogy between celestial spirits and human souls in managing bodies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Razi's analogy between the celestial spirits (angels) and human souls, which explains how a single universal soul can manage an entire sphere through particular souls.

Core Idea
Celestial spirits manage spheres like human souls manage bodies — through a hierarchy of forces.

Al-Razi presents a powerful analogy to help us conceive how the celestial spirits (angels) manage the vast celestial bodies. He compares the spheres to the human body, the planets to the heart, and the radiating rays to subtle bodily fluids. This analogy leads to the idea that just as the human soul emanates particular powers to each organ, the universal soul of the sphere emanates particular souls to each part.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the full English translation. Let the author's own words establish the analogy.
Notice how Al-Razi uses the phrase 'thumma kama' to introduce the comparison.
2
Identify the elements of the analogy
List the pairs: spheres → bodies, planets → heart, rays → subtle luminous bodies. Ask yourself why each comparison is made.
The heart is central in medieval physiology, just as planets are central in the spheres.
3
Examine the extension to souls
Al-Razi extends the analogy: the universal soul of the sphere is like the human soul, and particular souls are like the managing powers in each organ. Reflect on how this explains celestial management.
This shows a hierarchy within a single celestial spirit.
4
Consider the theological significance
Think about why Al-Razi uses this analogy. It helps reconcile the idea of one angel managing a huge sphere with the need for differentiated actions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما الأرواح المدبرة للأجسام الفلكية . فالأظهر أن الأفلاك تكون لها كالأبدان ، والكواكب لها كالقلوب ، والخطوط الشعاعية الفائضة من أجرام الكواكب [ النيرة ] جارية مجرى الأجسام اللطيفة النورانية ، الفائضة من القلب والدماغ ، الواصلة إلى سائر الأعضاء . ثم كما أن لكل بدن : نفساً واحدة ، إلا أنه يفيض عنها في كل جزء من أجزاء البدن : قوة مديرة لذلك الجزء ، فكذلك لا يبعد أن يفيض من النفس الكلية التي للفلك : نفوس مخصوصة ، كل واحد منها يكون متعلقاً بجزء معين من أجزاء الفلك ، وبجانب مخصوص من جوانبه .
As for the spirits that manage the celestial bodies: the most apparent is that the spheres are to them like bodies, and the planets are to them like hearts, and the radiating lines emanating from the bodies of the luminous planets are like the subtle luminous bodies emanating from the heart and brain, reaching the rest of the organs. Then, just as each body has a single soul, yet from it emanates in every part of the body a managing power for that part, so likewise it is not unlikely that from the universal soul of the sphere there emanate particular souls, each of which is attached to a specific part of the sphere and to a specific side of its sides.
Source pages p. 10

Commentary
This passage describes the celestial spirits that manage the celestial bodies, namely the angels that move the spheres. Al-Razi presents an analogy: the celestial spheres are like bodies, the planets like hearts, and the celestial rays like the subtle luminous bodies that emanate from the heart and brain to the rest of the organs. This draws a parallel with the human body. He then extends the analogy: just as a human body has a single soul that emanates particular powers to each organ, so the universal soul of the sphere emanates particular souls attached to specific parts of the sphere. This explains how one celestial spirit can manage an entire sphere through subordinate spirits.

Key Terms
الأرواح المدبرة للأجسام الفلكية
الأفلاك
الكواكب
النفس الكلية
النفوس المخصوصة
قوة مديرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think angels are simple, indivisible beings without internal differentiation.
Correction: Al-Razi shows that a single celestial spirit can have a hierarchical structure, with a universal soul emanating particular souls for specific parts, much like the human soul.

Checkpoint
In Al-Razi's analogy, what corresponds to the heart in the human body?
A) The spheres
B) The planets
C) The rays
D) The particular souls

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi uses the analogy of the human soul managing the body through particular powers to explain how one universal celestial spirit can manage an entire sphere through particular souls attached to its parts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If Al-Razi's analogy holds, then your very ability to read this lesson is a result of a managing power emanating from your soul. When was the last time you paused to recognize that the most basic actions of your life are sustained by a spiritual force you take for granted? Will you spend one minute today thanking the One who created that soul for you?
?
The universal soul of the sphere manages the entire cosmos through particular souls, yet remains unified. In your own life, do you experience the unity of divine purpose behind the many events that happen to you, or do you feel fragmented? What would change if you saw every trial and blessing as coming from one divine source?
?
Al-Razi describes a hierarchy of spirits: one universal soul, many particular souls. How do you treat the 'subordinate' roles in your own life—people you manage or tasks you delegate? Do you see them as extensions of your own responsibility, or as separate? How might viewing them as emanations of your own soul change your sense of accountability?

Human Souls as Immaterial Substances
Al-Razi states the majority view on human rational souls.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the majority view that human rational souls are immaterial substances attached to bodies for management, not by indwelling.

Core Idea
Human souls are immaterial managers of the body, not its inhabitants.

Al-Razi presents the prevailing position on the nature of human rational souls. He affirms their immateriality while clarifying their unique relationship with the body—one of management rather than spatial indwelling. This distinction is crucial for theological understandings of the soul's independence from the body.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once carefully, then the English translation. Let the author's own wording settle before analysis.
Notice the compactness of the phrasing.
2
Identify the key claim
The passage states that human rational souls are 'immaterial substances' (jawahir mujarrada). Understand that this means they have no physical composition or location.
3
Note what is negated
Al-Razi negates two things: corporeality and indwelling (hulul) in the body. Recognize that this avoids thinking the soul is a physical thing inside the body.
Indwelling would imply spatial containment.
4
Understand the positive relation
The souls are 'attached' (ta'alluq) to bodies for management and disposition (tadbir wa tasarruf). This attachment is functional, not spatial.
Think of a manager guiding a company without being physically inside each part.
5
Reflect on the importance
Ask yourself: why does this distinction matter for Islamic theology? It preserves the soul’s independence from the body and its survival after death.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما النفوس السفلية : فقد اتفق الأكثرون على أن النفوس الناطقة البشرية : جواهر مجردة عن الجسمية ، وعن الحلول في الجسم ، إلا أنها متعلقة بهذه الأبدان على سبيل التدبير والتصرف .
As for the lower souls: the majority have agreed that the human rational souls are substances immaterial from corporeality and from indwelling in body, but they are attached to these bodies by way of management and disposition.
Source pages p. 10

Commentary
This passage presents the majority position on human souls: they are immaterial substances (jawahir mujarrada) that are not corporeal nor do they dwell within the body, yet they have a relationship of management and disposition (tadbir wa tasarruf) with the human body. This is a key doctrine in Islamic philosophy and kalam regarding the nature of the soul.

Key Terms
النفوس السفلية
النفوس الناطقة البشرية
جواهر مجردة
الحلول في الجسم
التدبير والتصرف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the soul's attachment to the body means it dwells inside it like a body in a container.
Correction: Al-Razi distinguishes attachment (ta'alluq) from indwelling (hulul). The soul manages the body without being spatially contained within it.

Checkpoint
What do the majority of scholars hold regarding human rational souls?
A) They are material
B) They are immaterial but dwell in the body
C) They are immaterial and attached to bodies for management
D) They are identical to the body

Key Takeaway
The human rational soul is an immaterial substance attached to the body for management, not indwelling.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your soul is not inside your body but only manages it, then what should you be attending to most: the body's demands or the soul's direction? And how will you listen to your soul today, when the body's cravings shout louder?
?
Since the soul is immaterial and not located in this world, where is your true home? If you live as though this body is your permanent residence, what needs to change so that you live as a manager, not a dweller?
?
Al-Razi says souls are 'attached to bodies for management' - but if you die, the connection ends. Are you preparing your soul for that moment when the management contract is terminated? What one concrete step will you take this week to prioritize your soul over your body?

Animal Souls: Two Opinions and Subdivisions
Al-Razi classifies opinions on animal souls into two camps with subgroups.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two main opinions on animal souls according to Al-Razi and the subgroups within those who consider them immaterial.

Core Idea
Animal souls: either corporeal powers or immaterial—with two further camps among immaterialists.

This lesson examines Al-Razi's taxonomy of views on animal souls. He reports two contrasting opinions, then shows that the minority view itself splits into two factions. Understanding this classification clarifies the broader debate about the nature of souls in Islamic thought.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to get the overall classification.
2
Identify the two main opinions
Notice first that there is a majority view (corporeal powers) and a minority view (immaterial substances).
3
Find the two subgroups
Within the minority, Al-Razi distinguishes between transmigrationists and non-transmigrationists. Note the descriptions of each.
The non-transmigrationists still affirm immateriality but reject transmigration.
4
Reflect on the classification
Consider why Al-Razi might present these divisions and what it tells us about the diversity of views on animal souls.
This classification is a typical kalam method of mapping positions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما نفوس سائر الحيوانات : فالأكثرون زعموا : أنها قوى جسمانية ، وليست جواهر مجردة . ومنهم من زعم : أنها من الجواهر المجردة . وهؤلاء قسمان : الأول : القائلون بالتناسخ، وهم الذين زعموا : أن أرواح البهائم والسباع كانت أرواحاً بشرية ، إلا أنها بعد مفارقة الأبدان الإنسانية صارت متعلقة بأبدان البهائم والباع . والفرقة الثانية : الذين ينكرون التناسخ، ويزعمون مع هذا : أن نفوس السباع والبهائم ، وسائر الحيوانات : نفوس مجردة عن الجسمية ، والحلول في الجسمية . فهذا هو الكلام في نفوس الحيوانات .
As for the souls of the rest of the animals: the majority claimed that they are corporeal powers and not immaterial substances. And among them are those who claimed that they are part of the immaterial substances. And these are two groups: the first: the transmigrationists, who claimed that the souls of beasts and predators were human souls, but after departing from human bodies they became attached to the bodies of beasts and predators. And the second faction: those who deny transmigration, and they claim along with this that the souls of predators, beasts, and all animals are souls immaterial from corporeality and from indwelling in corporeality. This is the discussion on animal souls.
Source pages p. 10

Commentary
This passage addresses animal souls. Al-Razi reports two main opinions. The majority opinion is that animal souls are corporeal powers (quwa jismaniyya) and not immaterial substances. A minority, however, holds that they are immaterial substances. Among this minority, there are two groups: (1) the transmigrationists (al-qailun bi al-tanasukh), who believe that the souls of animals were once human souls that, after death, transmigrated into animal bodies. (2) Those who deny transmigration but still affirm that animal souls are immaterial, distinct from bodies. Al-Razi concludes: 'Thus this is the discussion on animal souls.'

Key Terms
نفوس الحيوانات
قوى جسمانية
جواهر مجردة
التناسخ
الباع/السباع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All who consider animal souls immaterial believe in transmigration.
Correction: Al-Razi identifies a second subgroup that affirms immateriality but denies transmigration.

Checkpoint
According to Al-Razi, what is the majority view on animal souls?
A) They are immaterial substances
B) They are corporeal powers
C) They are human souls in animal bodies
D) They are nonexistent

Key Takeaway
The nature of animal souls is debated: most see them as corporeal powers, while a minority views them as immaterial, with further disagreement on transmigration.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If animal souls are immaterial and have some form of consciousness, what does that mean for how you treat the animals you encounter daily? Are there changes you need to make in your attitude or actions starting today?
?
Al-Razi presents the majority view that animal souls are mere corporeal powers. Does your treatment of animals reflect that view (as if they are machines) or the alternative view (as beings with immaterial souls)? What does your behavior reveal about your actual belief?
?
The source lists differing opinions without a clear endorsement. How do you respond to ambiguity in Islamic theological matters? Do you seek evidence and reflect, or do you gravitate toward the majority? What would it cost you to honestly confront this uncertainty?

Jinn and Shayatin: Prophetic Affirmation and Classification of Spirits
Al-Razi notes disagreement on jinn, affirms prophets' testimony, and classifies spirits into two kinds.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Razi's stance on jinn and shayatin: his dismissal of later philosophers' opinions, his reliance on prophetic testimony, and the concluding classification of spirits into celestial and lower types.

Core Idea
Prophets affirm jinn and devils; later philosophers have no convincing argument.

In this passage, Al-Razi concludes his discussion on spirits by addressing the existence of jinn and shayatin. He notes that later philosophers offer no persuasive arguments, but the majority of prophets were certain of their reality. He then provides a final summary classification of spirits into two categories: celestial and lower. This passage ties together the preceding discussion and grounds belief in jinn on prophetic authority.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation to get the overall flow.
2
Identify Al-Razi's main claim about philosophers
Notice that Al-Razi dismisses the later philosophers ('laysa...kalam muqni').
3
Understand the classification of spirits
The passage ends with the division of spirits into celestial (falakiyya) and lower (sufliyya). Consider how jinn and shayatin fit into this.
Al-Razi does not elaborate here, but this echoes earlier classifications.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واختلف الناس في الجن والشياطين. وليس للمتأخرين من الفلاسفة في هذا الباب : كلام مقنع ، إلا أن الأكثرين من الأنبياء والرسل - عليهم السلام - أثبتوها ، وجزموا بوجودها . وحاصل القول فيها : أنهم زعموا : أن الأرواح على قسمين : أرواح فلكية . وأرواح سفلية .
And people differed regarding jinn and devils. There is no convincing speech from the later philosophers on this topic, except that the majority of the prophets and messengers - peace be upon them - affirmed them and were certain of their existence. And the conclusion of the statement regarding them is: that they claimed that the spirits are of two kinds: celestial spirits and lower spirits.
Source pages p. 10

Commentary
This final passage deals with the reality of jinn and shayatin. Al-Razi notes that later philosophers have no convincing argument on this matter, but the majority of prophets affirmed their existence with certainty. He then gives the conclusion (hasil al-qawl) regarding these beings: they claimed that spirits are of two kinds: celestial (falakiyya) and lower (sufliyya). This conclusion echoes the earlier division of spirits. Thus, jinn and shayatin likely fall under the category of lower spirits, but Al-Razi does not elaborate further here.

Key Terms
الجن
الشياطين
الأنبياء والرسل
الأرواح الفلكية
الأرواح السفلية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Later philosophers have definitive views or arguments on the existence of jinn and devils.
Correction: Al-Razi explicitly says that later philosophers have no convincing speech ('kalam muqni') on this matter.

Checkpoint

A) They have compelling arguments
B) They have no convincing speech
C) They deny their existence
D) They affirm them through reason

Al-Razi relies on prophetic testimony to affirm jinn and shayatin, and concludes that spirits are of two kinds: celestial and lower.

Celestial and Terrestrial Souls: Angels, Believing Jinn, Iblis, and the Magian Error
Defines celestial souls (angels) and two types of terrestrial souls, and criticizes the Magian view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's classification of souls into celestial (angels) and terrestrial (believing jinn and malicious jinn with Iblis), and the refutation of the Magian dualistic exaggeration.

Core Idea
Souls are divided into pure celestial angels and two earthly kinds, rejecting any equal power to Allah.

In this passage, the author presents a clear classification of souls based on their nature and role. He begins with the celestial souls—angels who are infallible—then moves to terrestrial souls, which include both good jinn and evil jinn led by Iblis. Finally, he criticizes the Magian claim that Iblis is an opposing force to God, affirming that evil is not from a separate creator.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation, noting the three categories: celestial souls (angels), pure terrestrial souls (believing jinn), impure terrestrial souls (Iblis and his followers), and the Magian view.
Pay attention to the contrast between the pure and impure groups.
2
Identify the attributes
Notice that angels are described as infallible, while believing jinn are pure and good, and Iblis is the leader of the evil jinn.
3
Understand the Magian error
The Magians exaggerate by positing Iblis as a counterpart to Allah and angels, attributing all evil to him. The author presents this as an overstatement.
Consider why this dualism is problematic from an Islamic perspective.
4
Reflect on the theological point
The author implicitly rejects dualism by not giving Iblis any creative power; Allah alone is the creator of all.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما الأرواح الفلكية : فهم الملائكة ، وهم معصومون عن الشرور والنقائص وأما الأرواح السفلية ، فهم فريقان : أحدهما : الأرواح الطاهرة الخيرة ، النقية عن العقائد الذميمة ، والأفعال الرديئة . وهم مؤمنو الجن والثاني : الأرواح الخبيثة الشريرة المؤذية . قالوا : ولهم رئيس ، هو أقواهم قوة ، وأتممهم قدرة . وهو المسمى ( إبليس ، والباقون أتباعه ، وهو مع جنده ، في مقابلة [ جند الملائكة . وأما المجوس . فقد بالغوا ، وزعموا : أنه . أعني ( إبليس ، في مقابلة (1) ] ( الله : تعالى وملائكته (۲) فكل ما يحصل في العالم من الخيرات والراحات، فهم من الله تعالى ، والملائكة . وكل ما يحصل في العالم من الآلام والآفات والمكروهات فهو من إبليس والشياطين .
As for the celestial souls: they are the angels, and they are infallible against evils and deficiencies. As for the terrestrial souls, they are two groups: one of them: the pure, good souls, purified from reprehensible beliefs and evil deeds. They are the believing jinn. The second: the malicious, harmful, injurious souls. They said: they have a leader, who is the strongest of them in power and the most complete in ability. He is named Iblis, and the rest are his followers, and he with his army is opposed to the army of the angels. As for the Magians, they have exaggerated, claiming that he—I mean Iblis—is opposed to God Almighty and His angels. So every good and comfort that occurs in the world is from God Almighty and the angels, and every pain, affliction, and disliked thing that occurs in the world is from Iblis and the devils.
Source pages p. 11

Commentary
This passage defines the categories of souls according to the author's framework. It begins by identifying celestial souls as angels, noting their key attribute of being free from evil and deficiency. Then it turns to terrestrial souls, which are divided into two types: the first comprises pure and good souls, free from bad beliefs and actions—these are the believing jinn. The second type consists of malicious souls that cause harm; they have a leader named Iblis, who is the most powerful and capable among them, and his followers. Together they oppose the angelic host. The passage then critiques the Magian (Zoroastrian) view, which oversteps by positing Iblis as an opposing power to God and His angels, attributing all good to God and all evil to Iblis and the devils. The author implies this dualism is excessive.

Correction: The text distinguishes believing jinn as pure and good, free from reprehensible beliefs and evil deeds.

The author classifies souls into three types: angels (infallible), believing jinn (pure), and devilish jinn led by Iblis, and rejects the Magian dualistic exaggeration.

?

Two Views on the Nature of Administrative Spirits
Presents two opinions on spirits that administer parts of the world.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two classical views on spirits that administer parts of the world and their subdivisions.

Core Idea
Administrative spirits are either celestial administrators or attached to the ether sphere.

This passage from al-Matalib al-'Aliyah examines the nature of spirits that manage various parts of the world. Thinkers who accept their existence divide into two groups: one sees them as celestial souls assigned to specific domains, the other sees their primary attachment to the ether sphere. Understanding these views clarifies how spiritual beings interact with the physical world.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note the two groups and the subdivisions mentioned.
2
Identify the first group's view
The first group says these spirits are celestial souls that administer specific earthly domains. List the domains and subdivisions.
Notice how they specialize further.
3
Contrast with the second group
The second group holds that these spirits' primary attachment is to a part of the ether sphere, not directly to earthly things.
This suggests a more remote relationship.
4
Reflect on theological significance
Consider why these distinctions matter for understanding divine governance and the hierarchy of creation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم القائلون [ بوجود هذا (۳) ] النوع من الأرواح ، فريقان : منهم من زعم : أنها أرواح فلكية ، إلا أن كل واحد منها يدبر لقسم من أقسام هذا العالم ، فههنا (٤) أرواح مديرة [ للجبال ، وأرواح مدبرة () ] للبحار ، وأرواح مديرة للمفاوز ، وأرواح مديرة للعمرانات . وهذا القسم أيضاً : أقسام . فبعضها مدبرة لأحوال الأمطار ، وبعضها للزروع ، وبعضها للحروب . وقال الآخرون : بل هذه الأرواح ، المدبرة لهذه الأشياء ، لا يبعد أن يكون تعلقها الأول ، بجزء من أجزاء كرة الأثير
Then those who affirm the existence of this type of spirits are two groups: some of them claimed that they are celestial souls, except that each one of them administers a division of the divisions of this world. So here are souls managing the mountains, souls managing the seas, souls managing the deserts, and souls managing the cultivated lands. And this division also has subdivisions: some of them manage the conditions of rains, some for crops, and some for wars. And others said: Rather these spirits that administer these things—it is not unlikely that their first attachment is to a part of the parts of the sphere of the ether.
Source pages p. 11

Commentary
This passage classifies the proponents of this type of spirits into two groups. The first group holds that these spirits are originally celestial but are assigned to administer specific domains of the earthly world—each has a particular province like mountains, seas, deserts, or cultivated areas. Moreover, within these domains, there are further specializations: some manage rains, some crops, some wars. The second group holds that rather than being directly attached to earthly things, these spirits' primary attachment is to a part of the ether sphere, implying they are more remote from the physical world. This illustrates the theological and philosophical diversity regarding how spiritual entities interact with the material world.

Key Terms
أرواح مديرة
كرة الأثير
أرواح فلكية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that all spirits are either purely celestial or purely terrestrial.
Correction: This passage shows a middle view: administrative spirits can be celestial in origin but assigned to earthly tasks, or their primary attachment is to the ether sphere.

Checkpoint

A) To the mountains and seas directly.
B) To a part of the sphere of the ether.
C) To the cultivated lands.
D) To the human heart.

Key Takeaway
Administrative spirits are understood in two ways: as celestial souls assigned to earthly domains or as spirits attached to the ether sphere.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage describes spirits assigned to manage mountains, seas, deserts, and cultivated lands. Consider your own surroundings: do you ever reflect on the unseen administrators behind the natural phenomena you experience daily? What would change in your awareness if you truly believed these tasks are assigned to specific spirits? Commit to noticing one natural element today and thanking Allah for its unseen management.
?
The two groups differ on where these spirits are primarily attached—directly to earthly domains or to the ether sphere. How does your understanding of the unseen affect your sense of divine order? Are you comfortable with uncertainty regarding such matters, or do you feel the need for certainty? Ponder whether your faith allows for these differences of opinion without disturbance.
?
If you were to classify yourself, which view resonates more? How does that affect your daily gratitude or awe towards the natural world? Write down one specific action you will take this week to acknowledge the unseen management of your environment—for example, pausing before a meal to reflect on the spirits assigned to crops and rains.

Another Type of Spirits: Prophetic and Talismanic Affirmation
Introduces spirits affirmed by prophets and talisman practitioners

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that there is a category of spirits affirmed by prophets and talisman practitioners, and why the author considers this a legitimate topic for investigation.

Core Idea
Spirits affirmed by prophets deserve serious investigation, not dismissal.

In this passage, the author introduces another type of spirits—one accepted by prophets and talisman practitioners alike. He grounds this claim in a prophetic hadith that mentions angels of seas, mountains, rains, provisions, and war. The passage invites us to examine the existence of such beings through both transmitted and rational inquiry.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation

Since the Prophet affirmed this, and since many people affirm it, the author concludes that one must investigate the reality of this type—i.e., it is a legitimate topic for rational and transmitted inquiry.

The Four Ranks of Immaterial Entities
Summary of the four ranks of immaterial entities per later philosophers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the hierarchy of immaterial entities according to later philosophers and how it differs from religious beliefs.

Core Idea
Later philosophers recognized four ranks of immaterial beings, excluding jinn and devils.

This concluding passage from al-Matalib al-Aliyah summarizes the discussion on immaterial entities. It presents the hierarchy accepted by later philosophers like al-Farabi and Ibn Sina, and contrasts it with the broader affirmation of religious communities. Understanding this hierarchy helps clarify the philosophical worldview regarding non-material existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to get an overview of the content.
2
Identify the four ranks
List the four categories in order of nobility as stated by the philosophers.
Notice that the first is the Necessary Existent in itself.
3
Compare with religious view
Note what philosophers deny that religious groups affirm, and why this distinction matters.
Jinn and devils are absent in the philosophical list.
4
Reflect on the ending
Consider the phrase 'And God knows best' as a sign of humility and deference to divine knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والحاصل : أن مراتب المفارقات عند الفلاسفة المتأخرين كـه أبي نصر الفارابي ) و ( أبي علي بن سينا ) أربعة . فأعلاها وأشرفها : واجب الوجود لذاته ، ثم العقول ، ثم النفوس السماوية ، ثم النفوس البشرية . ولا يعترفون بالجن والشياطين . وأما أرباب الملل والنحل ، وأصحاب الطلسمات . فإنهم يثبتون الجن والشياطين ، وسائر الأقسام . والله أعلم .
The summary: the ranks of the immaterial entities according to the later philosophers, such as Abu Nasr al-Farabi and Abu Ali ibn Sina, are four. The highest and noblest of them is the Necessary Existent in itself, then the intellects, then the celestial souls, then the human souls. And they do not acknowledge the jinn and the devils. As for the people of religious communities and sects, and the practitioners of talismans, they affirm the jinn and the devils and all the other divisions. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 11

Commentary
This concluding passage summarizes the discussion by presenting the hierarchy of immaterial entities according to later philosophers, specifically al-Farabi and Ibn Sina. They recognize four levels: (1) the Necessary Existent in itself (God), (2) the intellects (celestial intelligences), (3) the celestial souls (souls of the spheres), and (4) human souls. Importantly, these philosophers do not accept the existence of jinn or devils. In contrast, the people of religious communities (Muslims, Jews, Christians, etc.) and practitioners of talismans do affirm the existence of jinn, devils, and all the other types discussed in the previous sections. The author ends with 'And God knows best,' acknowledging that ultimate knowledge belongs to God.

النفوس السماوية
النفوس البشرية

الشياطين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that all philosophers deny the existence of jinn and devils, but the passage specifies that it is the later philosophers (like al-Farabi and Ibn Sina) who hold this view.
Correction: The passage does not claim that all philosophers deny jinn; it only attributes this denial to the later philosophers mentioned.

Checkpoint

B) The celestial souls.
C) The Necessary Existent in itself.
D) The human souls.

Key Takeaway
Later philosophers recognize four ranks of immaterial entities, with God as the highest, and do not affirm jinn or devils.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Is there any unseen reality you struggle to accept? What would it take for your heart to fully submit to what Allah has told us, even when it challenges your intellect?
?

The Infinite Degrees of Spirits and the Objection about Their Activity
The author explains the infinite degrees, the designation 'pure intellects', and answers an objection about passivity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the highest spirits are described as 'pure intellects' with infinite degrees, and how their complete absorption in divine knowledge does not negate their active emanation.

Core Idea
Total absorption in God is the highest form of activity, not passivity.

In this passage, the author delves into the nature of the highest spiritual beings, explaining why they are called 'pure intellects'. He then addresses a potential objection: if they are entirely absorbed in contemplation, how can they have any active effect? The answer reveals a profound synthesis of contemplation and effusion.

Guided Path
1
Understand the basis for infinite degrees
Read the first part where the author establishes that the degrees of these spirits are infinite because the divine lights are infinite. Notice how this connects perfection to the infinity of God.
2
Grasp the reason for the name 'pure intellects'
The author explains that these spirits are called 'pure intellects' because their intense intellection makes them as if identical to their intellections. Identify the key phrase: صاروا كأنهم عين تلك التعقلات.
3
Consider the objection
Focus on the objection: if these spirits only receive, where is their action? The objector claims total passivity. Notice the terms انفعال وتأثر (passivity and being affected).
This is a common critique of mystical absorption.
4
Learn the response
The author answers that despite their immersion, they emanate great effects upon celestial spirits and bodies, using the analogy of light from the sun. See how the response reframes absorption as the source of emanation.
The text cuts off at the analogy; think about how the sun gives light without losing its essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم إن لهذا القسم من الروحانيات : درجات لا نهاية لها في الكمال والنقصان وذلك لأن أنوار جلال الله سبحانه وتعالى ، غير متناهية . فدرجات العارفين في العرفان أيضاً : غير متناهية . ثم إنه لما كان لا صفة لهذا القسم من الروحانيات ، إلا الاستغراق في المعارف الإلهية ، والفناء في تلك الجلالة الصمدية ، لا جرم سمى الحكماء الإلهيون هذا القسم من الأرواح : بالعقول المحضة ، لأنها وإن كانت جواهر قائمة بأنفسها ، إلا أنها لكثرة تعقلاتها ، وقوة معارفها ، صاروا كأنهم عين تلك التعقلات ، ونفس تلك الإدراكات. فإن قال قائل : فعلى ما تقولونه أنتم ، لم يبق لهذا القسم من الروحانيات ، إلا قبول الوجود عن الحق ، وقبول الأضواء القدسية عن إشراق جلال الحق ، وكل ذلك انفعال وتأثر ، فأين التأثير والفعل ؟ فنقول في الجواب : إنها وإن كانت مستغرقة في تلك التعقلات الإلهية ، والتعقلات القدسية ، إلا أنه لا يبعد أن يفيض عنها : آثار عظيمة ، إما على الأرواح الفلكية ، أو على الأجرام الفلكية فيضان النور عن الشمس ، والحياة عن
Then, for this category of spiritual beings, there are infinite degrees in perfection and deficiency, because the lights of the majesty of Allah are infinite. So the degrees of the knowers (al-ʿārifīn) in knowledge are also infinite. Then, since there is no attribute for this category of spiritual beings except immersion in divine gnoses and annihilation in that majestic self-subsistence, the divine philosophers called this category of spirits 'pure intellects' (al-ʿuqūl al-maḥḍa), because even though they are substances subsisting by themselves, due to the abundance of their intellections and the strength of their knowledge, they become as if they are the very intellections and the very perceptions. If someone says: According to your statement, nothing remains for this category of spiritual beings except receiving existence from the Truth and receiving holy lights from the radiance of the majesty of the Truth; all of that is passivity and being affected. So where is the affecting and action? We say in response: Even if they are immersed in those divine intellections and holy intellections, it is not unlikely that great effects emanate from them, either upon the celestial spirits or upon the celestial bodies, like the emanation of light from the sun and life from
Source pages p. 19

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion of spiritual beings by establishing that their degrees are infinite, corresponding to the infinite lights of divine majesty. The highest of them, who are completely absorbed in divine knowledge, are called by philosophers 'pure intellects' (al-ʿuqūl al-maḥḍa). Although they are self-subsistent substances, their intense intellection makes them as if identical to those intellections. The author then presents an objection: if these spirits are entirely absorbed in contemplation, they seem to be purely passive recipients of existence and light, with no active effect. The author responds that their immersion does not prevent them from emanating great effects, either on celestial spirits or on celestial bodies, using the analogy of light emanating from the sun and life from... (the text cuts off). The point is that absorption in divine contemplation does not preclude active effusion; indeed, their overflowing bounty is a natural consequence of their perfection.

Key Terms
عقول محضة (ʿuqūl maḥḍa)
تعقلات (taʿaqqulāt)
فيضان (fayḍān)
انفعال (infiʿāl)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That total absorption in divine contemplation renders a being entirely passive and inactive.
Correction: The author shows that such absorption is compatible with active emanation; indeed, the emanated effects are a natural overflow of their perfection.

Checkpoint
Why do divine philosophers call the highest spirits 'pure intellects' (عقول محضة)?
A) Because they have no bodies.
B) Because they are absorbed in intellection to the extent that they become as if identical to their intellections.
C) Because they only contemplate themselves.
D) Because they are separate from matter.

Key Takeaway
The highest spirits, though completely absorbed in divine knowledge, are not passive; rather, they actively emanate great effects upon the cosmos, analogous to the sun's light.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage says that the highest spirits become 'as if they are the very intellections'. Is there any knowledge or truth that has so consumed you that it defines who you are? If not, what stands in the way of that kind of total immersion?
?
The objection suggests that total absorption makes one passive and ineffective. Do you avoid deep spiritual practices because you fear they will distract you from your duties? What would you have to let go of to embrace the irony that the deepest absorption yields the greatest activity?
?
The author compares the emanation of the pure intellects to the sun giving light without effort. When you perform good deeds, are you striving in your own strength, or are you so filled with God's presence that goodness flows from you naturally, like light? What would need to change for you to become that kind of vessel?

The First Category of Spiritual Beings: The Mugrabun
Al-Razi describes the first category of spiritual beings wholly absorbed in divine contemplation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first category of spiritual beings—the angels brought near (al-muqarrabun)—and their complete absorption in divine contemplation, as well as why the divine philosophers call them 'pure intellects.'

Core Idea
The highest angels are so absorbed in Allah that they become pure intellects, identical with their knowledge.

Al-Razi begins his classification of spiritual beings by describing the highest rank: those entirely absorbed in the light of divine majesty. These beings have no other occupation than contemplating Allah, and their very sustenance is tawhid, taqdis, and tanzih. This lesson unpacks their state, their scriptural basis, and the philosophical term 'pure intellects' that captures their nature.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the vivid imagery of absorption and the key terms sink in before interpreting.
Notice the contrast between their absorption and worldly concerns.
2
Identify the three metaphors of sustenance
Al-Razi uses a beautiful metaphorical description: their food is tawhid, their drink is taqdis, and their intimacy is tanzih. Reflect on what each term signifies about their relationship with Allah.
These are not literal but spiritual nourishment.
3
Connect to the Quranic proof
Al-Razi cites Quran 21:19-20 to support that these beings are constantly in worship. Read the verse and see how it aligns with the description.
4
Understand the infinite degrees
The passage states that the lights of divine majesty are infinite, so the degrees of these beings are also infinite. Consider why this matters for understanding spiritual hierarchy.
This implies that no two are exactly alike in perfection.
5
Grasp the philosophical term 'pure intellects'
Al-Razi explains why divine philosophers call these beings 'pure intellects': they are so immersed in intellections that they become essentially identical with their perceptions. Ponder this claim.
This sets up a potential theological discussion about their nature.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مستغرقين في نور جلال الله تعالى، استغراقاً تاماً ، بحيث لا يتفرغون مع ذلك الاستغراق ، لتدبير عالم الأجسام ، فطعامهم التوحيد ، وشرابهم التقديس ، وأنسهم التنزيه . استغرقوا في نور جلال الله تعالى ، ولم يتفرغوا إلى شيء سوى الله تعالى . وهؤلاء هم الملائكة المقربون . وقد عبر الكتاب الإلهي عن هذه المرتبة بقوله : ( ومن عنده لا يستكبرون عن عبادته ولا يستحرون ، يسبحون الليل والنهار ، لا يفترون (۷) ) ثم إن لهذا القسم من الروحانيات : درجات لا نهاية لها في الكمال والنقصان وذلك لأن أنوار جلال الله سبحانه وتعالى ، غير متناهية . فدرجات العارفين في العرفان أيضاً : غير متناهية . ثم إنه لما كان لا صفة لهذا القسم من الروحانيات ، إلا الاستغراق في المعارف الإلهية ، والفناء في تلك الجلالة الصمدية ، لا جرم سمى الحكماء الإلهيون هذا القسم من الأرواح : بالعقول المحضة ، لأنها [ وإن كانت جواهر قائمة بأنفسها، إلا أنها (۳) ] لكثرة تعقلاتها ، وقوة معارفها ، صاروا كأنهم [ عين (۳) ] تلك التعقلات ، ونفس تلك الإدراكات
Absorbed in the light of the majesty of Allah Almighty, completely absorbed, such that they do not become free from that absorption to manage the world of bodies. Their food is tawḥīd (divine unity), their drink is taqdīs (sanctification), and their intimacy is tanzīh (transcendence). They are absorbed in the light of the majesty of Allah Almighty and do not occupy themselves with anything other than Allah Almighty. These are the angels brought near (al-muqarrabūn). And the divine Book expressed this station by His saying: 'And those who are with Him are not too proud to worship Him, nor do they grow weary. They glorify (Him) night and day, never slackening' (21:19-20). Then indeed, this category of spiritual beings has infinite degrees in perfection and deficiency because the lights of the majesty of Allah, glorified and exalted, are infinite. So the degrees of knowers (ʿārifīn) in knowledge are also infinite. Then, since this category of spiritual beings has no attribute except absorption in divine knowledge and annihilation in that majestic Self-sufficiency, the divine philosophers (al-ḥukamāʾ al-ilāhiyyūn) have called this category of spirits 'pure intellects' (ʿuqūl maḥḍa), because even though they are substances subsisting by themselves, they, due to the abundance of their intellections and the strength of their knowledge, become as if they are the very essence of those intellections and the very self of those perceptions.
Source pages p. 19

Commentary
This passage classifies and describes the first category of spiritual beings—the angels brought near (al-muqarrabūn). Al-Razi begins by depicting their state: they are completely absorbed in the light of divine majesty, to the extent that they have no room to manage the physical world. Their sustenance is tawḥīd, taqdīs, and tanzīh—key concepts of divine unity, sanctification, and transcendence. He then identifies them as the muqarrabūn and cites Qurʾān 21:19-20 to support this, emphasizing their constant worship without weariness. Next, he notes that this category has infinite degrees of perfection because the divine lights are infinite, so the levels of knowledge among the knowers are also infinite. Since their sole attribute is absorption in divine knowledge and annihilation in the divine, the divine philosophers (presumably Peripatetic philosophers like al-Fārābī and Ibn Sīnā) call them 'pure intellects' (ʿuqūl maḥḍa). Al-Razi explains the reasoning: although they are self-subsistent substances, their intense intellectual activity makes them identical with their intellections. The passage ends with a textual variant note (indicated by brackets and footnote markers) that they are 'as if the essence of those intellections and the very self of those perceptions.' This sets the stage for a potential objection about their passivity.

?

Is it the oneness of Allah and reflection on His perfection, or is it the distractions of this world? Can you identify one worldly craving you can replace today with meditation on Allah's attributes?

Practical Angels and Their Degrees
Al-Razi enumerates the second category of spirits (practical angels) and their degrees.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Razi's classification of the second category of spirits as practical angels (souls) that manage the physical world, and their hierarchical degrees starting with the Throne and Footstool.

Core Idea
The practical angels are the souls that manage the cosmos, with the Throne spirit at the peak.

Al-Razi continues his classification of spiritual beings, now turning to those who engage with the physical realm. These are the 'practical angels,' termed 'souls' by philosophers. They have a hierarchy based on the bodies they manage, beginning with the Throne and the Footstool. This lesson unpacks their nature and degrees.

Guided Path

The Throne spirit is also called Supreme Spirit and Universal Soul.
3
Connect to the Quranic citation
Al-Razi cites Ayat al-Kursi to link the Footstool with the eighth sphere. Reflect on how he integrates Quranic and philosophical terminology.
The text cuts off; we only have the beginning of the explanation.

وأما القسم الثاني من أقسام الأرواح : فهم الذين التفتوا إلى تدبير عالم الأجسام . وما بالغوا في الاستغراق في خدمة الجانب الأعلى إلى حيث يمنعهم عن التعلق بالجانب الأسفل ، وهؤلاء هم الملائكة العملية . وفي مصطلحات الفلاسفة هم المسمون بالنفوس . ثم إن هذا القسم متفاوتون أيضاً في درجات الشرف والكمال . فكل من أشرف وأعلى ، كان هو في ذاته أشرف (۱) وأعلى . ولما كان أعظم الأجسام هو العرش ، لا جرم كان أعظم الأرواح المتعلقة بعالم الأجسام هو الروح المتصرف في العرش، ولا يبعد أن يكون المسمى بالروح الأعظم هو ذلك الروح ، وهو المسمى أيضاً : بالنفس الكلية في مصطلحات الفلاسفة ، لان تلك النفس بدنها العرش (۳) ، وكل ما حصل في داخل العرش ، فكأنه جزء من أجزائه ، وشعبة من شعبه . وكذلك النفوس المدبرة لتلك الأجسام تكون شعبة من شعب تلك النفس ، ونتيجة من نتائجها . ثم المرتبة الثانية من مراتب النفوس : النفس المدبرة للكرسي ، كما قال في الكتاب الإلمي : من ذا الذي يشفع عنده إلا بإذنه ) ؟ إلى قوله تعالى : وسع كرسيه السموات والأرض (۳) ) والسبب في تسمية الفلك الثامن بالكرسي : أن الكرسي جسم حصل فيه درجات متفاوتة بالدنو والعلو ، لأن
As for the second category of spirits: they are those who turn to the management of the world of bodies. They do not go to such an extreme in absorption in the service of the higher side that it prevents them from attachment to the lower side. These are the practical angels. In the terminology of the philosophers, they are called 'souls' (nufūs). Then indeed this category also varies in degrees of honor and perfection. So whoever is more noble and higher is essentially more noble and higher. And since the greatest of bodies is the Throne (al-ʿArsh), necessarily the greatest of spirits pertaining to the world of bodies is the spirit that disposes in the Throne. It is not unlikely that the one called the 'Supreme Spirit' (al-Rūḥ al-Aʿẓam) is that spirit, and it is also called the 'Universal Soul' (al-Nafs al-Kulliyya) in the terminology of the philosophers, because that soul has the Throne as its body, and everything that occurs within the Throne is like a part of its parts and a branch of its branches. Likewise, the souls that manage those bodies are branches of that soul and results of its results. Then the second rank of souls: the soul that manages the Footstool (al-Kursī), as He said in the divine Book: 'Who is there that can intercede with Him except by His permission?' up to His saying: 'His Footstool extends over the heavens and the earth' (2:255). And the reason for naming the eighth sphere as the Footstool: that the Footstool is a body that has various degrees in nearness and elevation, because...
Source pages p. 20

Commentary
This passage defines the second category of spirits—those concerned with managing the physical world, called 'practical angels' in religious terminology and 'souls' (nufūs) in philosophical terminology. Al-Razi notes that they do not become so absorbed in the higher realm that they neglect the lower; they balance both. He then explains that this category also has degrees of nobility. The highest is the spirit of the Throne (al-ʿArsh), the greatest physical body, which Al-Razi identifies with the 'Supreme Spirit' and the 'Universal Soul' (al-Nafs al-Kulliyya). He clarifies that the Throne serves as its body, and all other celestial bodies within it are branches of this soul. Next, he describes the second rank: the soul of the Footstool (al-Kursī), citing the Ayat al-Kursī (2:255) to link it with the eighth sphere. He begins to explain why this sphere is called the Footstool—because it has degrees of nearness and elevation—but the text cuts off at the page break. Al-Razi uses the philosophical framework of emanation but adapts it to Islamic terms. Note that 'al-ilmī' in the text is likely a scribal error for 'al-ilāhī' (the divine Book).

روح أعظم

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All angels are only engaged in worship and have no role in managing the physical world.
Correction: Al-Razi identifies a second category of spirits, the practical angels (souls), who manage the physical world, including the Throne and Footstool.

The Second Division of Spirits and the Principle of Hierarchy
Razi defines the second division of spirits and states their hierarchical principle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of the second division of spirits (the practical angels/souls) and the principle that their rank corresponds to the body they manage.

Core Idea
Spirits vary in honor according to the bodies they manage.

Razi begins by distinguishing a second category of spirits: those who turn to manage the material world without being absorbed in the divine. These are the practical angels, also called souls. He then introduces the key principle that determines their hierarchy: the nobler the body they manage, the nobler the spirit itself. This lesson establishes the foundation for the cosmic chain of spirits that follows.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how Razi defines the second division and then immediately states the principle of variation.
2
Identify the key terms
Find the terms 'practical angels' and 'souls' and note their equivalence.
3
Reflect on the principle
Think about why the rank of a spirit depends on the body it manages. This is a recurring theme in the hierarchy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما القسم الثاني من أقسام الأرواح : فهم الذين التفتوا إلى تدبير عالم الأجسام . وما بالغوا في الاستغراق في خدمة الجانب الأعلى إلى حيث يمنعهم عن التعلق بالجانب الأسفل ، وهؤلاء هم الملائكة العملية . وفي مصطلحات الفلاسفة هم المسمون بالنفوس .
كان تعلقه بجسم
ثم إن هذا القسم متفاوتون أيضاً في درجات الشرف والكمال . فكل من أشرف وأعلى ، كان هو في ذاته أشرف وأعلى . ولما كان أعظم الأجسام هو العرش ، لا جرم كان أعظم الأرواح المتعلقة بعالم الأجسام هو الروح المتصرف في العرش، ولا يبعد أن يكون المسمى بالروح الأعظم هو ذلك الروح ، وهو المسمى أيضاً : بالنفس الكلية في مصطلحات الفلاسفة ، لان تلك النفس بدنها العرش ، وكل ما حصل في داخل العرش ، فكأنه جزء من أجزائه ، وشعبة من شعبه . وكذلك النفوس المدبرة لتلك الأجسام تكون شعبة من شعب تلك النفس ، ونتيجة من نتائجها .
ثم المرتبة الثانية من مراتب النفوس : النفس المدبرة للكرسي ، كما قال في الكتاب الإلمي : من ذا الذي يشفع عنده إلا بإذنه ) ؟ إلى قوله تعالى : وسع كرسيه السموات والأرض ) والسبب في تسمية الفلك الثامن بالكرسي : أن الكرسي جسم حصل فيه درجات متفاوتة بالدنو والعلو ، لأن الكواكب المركوزة في الفلك الثامن : أجرام مختلفة بالصغر والكبر ، والتي أدركتها أبصار الخلق من تلك الكواكب مرتبة على سبع مراتب في العظم ، فأصغرها جرماً ، ما يكون في العظم السابع، وهو يكون في أدنى الدرجات ، وفوقها ما يكون في العظم السادس . وهكذا تترقى مرتبة فمرتبة ، ودرجة فدرجة ، إلى أن تصل إلى العظم الأول . ولهذا [ السبب ] حصل في ثخن الفلك الثامن : درجات متفاوتة . ولهذا سمي بالكرسي . ولما كانت الشعرى اليمانية ، أكبرها قدراً، وجب أن يكون مكانه في الدرجة الأعلى ، والمرتبة الأسمى والأسنى . وأقول : لا شك أن لكل واحد من تلك الكواكب خواص وآثار ، لا يعلمها إلا الله - سبحانه وتعالى .
والمرتبة الثالثة من النفوس : الأرواح المدبرة لفلك زحل وهكذا القول في سائر أطباق السموات ، وأجرام الكواكب على اختلاف درجاتها ، وتباعد مراتبها، حتى تنتهي إلى الروح المدبرة لكرة القمر .
ثم بعد هذه المراتب : الأرواح المدبرة لكرة الأثير [ ثم لكرة الهواء ] ثم الأرواح المدبرة لأقسام هذا العالم ، وذلك لأن كرة الأرض مقسومة بأربعة أقسام ، وأعظم الأقسام الأربعة : البحار والقسم الثاني : المفاوز ، والبيانات . والقسم الثالث : الجبال . والقسم الرابع : العمرانات ، ولا يبعد في العقل أن يحصل لكل قسم من هذه الأقسام : روح واحدة ، أو أرواح كثيرة مديرة لها . وكل ما ذكرناه ، مما نطق به أصحاب الوحي والتنزيل فإنه [ عليه السلام كان يقول ] : «جاءني ملك البحار ، فقال : كذا وكذا . وجاءني ملك الجبال ، فقال : كذا وكذا ، وجاءني ملك الأمطار ، فقال : كذا وكذا . وجاءني ملك الحروب ) . و ( خازن الجنة فلان ، وخازن النار فلان ، وإذا كان كل واحد من هذه الأقسام أمراً محتملاً في العقل ، ولم يوجد دليل على نفيه،
As for the second division of spirits: they are those who turned to the management of the world of bodies and did not go to extremes in absorption in the service of the higher aspect to the point that it would prevent them from attachment to the lower aspect. These are the practical angels. In the terminology of the philosophers, they are called souls.  Its attachment to a body  Then this division also differ in degrees of honor and perfection. For whoever is more honorable and higher, he is in himself more honorable and higher. And since the greatest of bodies is the Throne, inevitably the greatest of spirits related to the world of bodies is the spirit that manages the Throne. It is not unlikely that what is called the Greatest Spirit is that spirit, and it is also called the Universal Soul in the terminology of the philosophers, because that soul's body is the Throne, and everything that occurs within the Throne is as if part of its parts and a branch of its branches. Likewise, the souls managing those bodies are a branch of that soul and a result of its results. Then the second rank of the ranks of souls: the soul managing the Kursi, as it is said in the Divine Book: 'Who is there that can intercede with Him except by His permission?' up to His saying: 'His Kursi extends over the heavens and the earth.' And the reason for naming the eighth sphere the Kursi is that the Kursi is a body in which there are varying degrees of proximity and elevation, because the stars fixed in the eighth sphere are bodies differing in smallness and bigness, and those of those stars which the sight of creation perceives are arranged in seven ranks of magnitude. The smallest in body is that which is in the seventh magnitude, and it is in the lowest degree, and above it is that which is in the sixth magnitude. Thus it ascends rank by rank and degree by degree until it reaches the first magnitude. For this reason, in the thickness of the eighth sphere there occur varying degrees, and for this reason it is called the Kursi. And since Sirius (al-Shi'ra al-Yamaniyya) is the greatest in magnitude, its place must be in the highest degree and the most exalted rank. And I say: there is no doubt that each of those stars has properties and effects which none knows except Allah – glorified and exalted is He. The third rank of souls: the spirits managing the sphere of Saturn, and similarly for the rest of the layers of the heavens and the bodies of the planets according to their different degrees and the distances of their ranks, until it ends with the spirit managing the sphere of the moon. Then after these ranks: the spirits managing the sphere of the ether, then the sphere of the air, then the spirits managing the divisions of this world. That is because the sphere of the earth is divided into four divisions: the greatest of the four divisions are the seas; the second division is the deserts and wildernesses; the third division is the mountains; the fourth division is the inhabited areas. And it is not far-fetched in reason that for each of these divisions there should be one spirit or many spirits managing them. And all that we have mentioned is something spoken by the people of revelation and inspiration, for he (peace be upon him) used to say: 'The angel of the seas came to me and said such-and-such; the angel of the mountains came to me and said such-and-such; the angel of the rains came to me and said such-and-such; the angel of wars came to me.' And 'the keeper of Paradise is so-and-so, and the keeper of Hell is so-and-so.' And since each of these divisions is something possible in reason, and there is no evidence for its negation...
Source pages p. 20

Commentary
This passage classifies the second division of spirits and then delineates a hierarchical order of souls (nufus) managing the cosmos. The author begins by defining the second division: these spirits are not completely absorbed in the higher aspect but instead engage in managing the material world; they are the 'practical angels' (al-mala'ika al-'amaliyya), equivalent to the 'souls' (al-nufus) in philosophical terminology. He then states that these spirits vary in degrees of honor and perfection corresponding to the bodies they manage. Since the greatest body is the Throne ('arsh), the greatest spirit is the one managing the Throne, called the Greatest Spirit (al-ruh al-a'zam) and in philosophy the Universal Soul (al-nafs al-kulliyya). Its body is the Throne, and all other bodies within the Throne are parts or branches of it. The second rank is the soul managing the Kursi (the eighth sphere, or the 'Seat'). The author explains why the eighth sphere is called Kursi: the stars within it are arranged in seven degrees of magnitude, giving the sphere thickness and thus a 'seat' structure. The largest star, Sirius (al-Shi'ra al-Yamaniyya), occupies the highest degree. The third rank consists of spirits managing the sphere of Saturn, and similarly for all seven heavens down to the moon. Subsequently, the spirits managing the ether sphere, then the air sphere, then the four divisions of the earth (seas, deserts, mountains, and inhabited areas) are listed. The author supports the possibility of such spirits with prophetic traditions mentioning angels of seas, mountains, rains, wars, and the keepers of Paradise and Hell. The passage concludes that since each of these is rationally possible and there is no evidence to negate it, the classification is valid.

نفس
الملائكة العملية

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what are the practical angels also called in philosophical terminology?
A) Spirits
B) Souls
C) Throne spirits
D) Kursi spirits

Key Takeaway
The second division of spirits are the practical angels/souls, and their rank depends on the body they manage.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your soul manages your body. If the honor of your soul is tied to the body it manages, what does the state of your body—your actions, your health, your purity—reveal about your soul's rank? Are you content with that, or does it compel you to change how you treat your physical vessel?
?
The practical angels are not completely absorbed in the higher realm; they engage with the lower world. Are you, like them, balancing spiritual devotion with worldly responsibilities? Or have you tipped entirely into one side? What would it take for you to be a true 'practical angel'?
?
This lesson teaches that the spirit's worth is proportional to the body it manages. If your body is a trust from Allah, what are you doing to make it noble enough for a noble soul? Will you let this truth transform how you use your limbs and faculties today?
