The Greatest Spirit: The Spirit of the Throne
Razi identifies the Throne spirit as the greatest and equates it with the Universal Soul.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the spirit managing the Throne is the greatest among the spirits of the material world, and its equivalence to the Universal Soul in philosophical terms.

Core Idea
The Throne spirit is the greatest because the Throne is the greatest body.

Having established the principle of hierarchy, Razi applies it to the highest body: the Throne. Since the Throne is the greatest of all bodies, its managing spirit must be the greatest. This spirit is called the Greatest Spirit and is identified with the Universal Soul of the philosophers, with the Throne as its body. This lesson explores this highest rank and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read and locate the reasoning
Read the Arabic passage with translation. Notice how Razi uses the principle from the previous lesson to deduce the rank of the Throne spirit.
2
Understand the Universal Soul
Reflect on the idea that the Throne is the body of this soul, and all lower bodies are its parts or branches.
3
Connect to the cosmic hierarchy
This spirit is the source for all lower souls, which are branches of it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما القسم الثاني من أقسام الأرواح : فهم الذين التفتوا إلى تدبير عالم الأجسام . وما بالغوا في الاستغراق في خدمة الجانب الأعلى إلى حيث يمنعهم عن التعلق بالجانب الأسفل ، وهؤلاء هم الملائكة العملية . وفي مصطلحات الفلاسفة هم المسمون بالنفوس .
كان تعلقه بجسم
ثم إن هذا القسم متفاوتون أيضاً في درجات الشرف والكمال . فكل من أشرف وأعلى ، كان هو في ذاته أشرف وأعلى . ولما كان أعظم الأجسام هو العرش ، لا جرم كان أعظم الأرواح المتعلقة بعالم الأجسام هو الروح المتصرف في العرش، ولا يبعد أن يكون المسمى بالروح الأعظم هو ذلك الروح ، وهو المسمى أيضاً : بالنفس الكلية في مصطلحات الفلاسفة ، لان تلك النفس بدنها العرش ، وكل ما حصل في داخل العرش ، فكأنه جزء من أجزائه ، وشعبة من شعبه . وكذلك النفوس المدبرة لتلك الأجسام تكون شعبة من شعب تلك النفس ، ونتيجة من نتائجها .
ثم المرتبة الثانية من مراتب النفوس : النفس المدبرة للكرسي ، كما قال في الكتاب الإلمي : من ذا الذي يشفع عنده إلا بإذنه ) ؟ إلى قوله تعالى : وسع كرسيه السموات والأرض ) والسبب في تسمية الفلك الثامن بالكرسي : أن الكرسي جسم حصل فيه درجات متفاوتة بالدنو والعلو ، لأن الكواكب المركوزة في الفلك الثامن : أجرام مختلفة بالصغر والكبر ، والتي أدركتها أبصار الخلق من تلك الكواكب مرتبة على سبع مراتب في العظم ، فأصغرها جرماً ، ما يكون في العظم السابع، وهو يكون في أدنى الدرجات ، وفوقها ما يكون في العظم السادس . وهكذا تترقى مرتبة فمرتبة ، ودرجة فدرجة ، إلى أن تصل إلى العظم الأول . ولهذا [ السبب ] حصل في ثخن الفلك الثامن : درجات متفاوتة . ولهذا سمي بالكرسي . ولما كانت الشعرى اليمانية ، أكبرها قدراً، وجب أن يكون مكانه في الدرجة الأعلى ، والمرتبة الأسمى والأسنى . وأقول : لا شك أن لكل واحد من تلك الكواكب خواص وآثار ، لا يعلمها إلا الله - سبحانه وتعالى .
والمرتبة الثالثة من النفوس : الأرواح المدبرة لفلك زحل وهكذا القول في سائر أطباق السموات ، وأجرام الكواكب على اختلاف درجاتها ، وتباعد مراتبها، حتى تنتهي إلى الروح المدبرة لكرة القمر .
ثم بعد هذه المراتب : الأرواح المدبرة لكرة الأثير [ ثم لكرة الهواء ] ثم الأرواح المدبرة لأقسام هذا العالم ، وذلك لأن كرة الأرض مقسومة بأربعة أقسام ، وأعظم الأقسام الأربعة : البحار والقسم الثاني : المفاوز ، والبيانات . والقسم الثالث : الجبال . والقسم الرابع : العمرانات ، ولا يبعد في العقل أن يحصل لكل قسم من هذه الأقسام : روح واحدة ، أو أرواح كثيرة مديرة لها . وكل ما ذكرناه ، مما نطق به أصحاب الوحي والتنزيل فإنه [ عليه السلام كان يقول ] : «جاءني ملك البحار ، فقال : كذا وكذا . وجاءني ملك الجبال ، فقال : كذا وكذا ، وجاءني ملك الأمطار ، فقال : كذا وكذا . وجاءني ملك الحروب ) . و ( خازن الجنة فلان ، وخازن النار فلان ، وإذا كان كل واحد من هذه الأقسام أمراً محتملاً في العقل ، ولم يوجد دليل على نفيه،
As for the second division of spirits: they are those who turned to the management of the world of bodies and did not go to extremes in absorption in the service of the higher aspect to the point that it would prevent them from attachment to the lower aspect. These are the practical angels. In the terminology of the philosophers, they are called souls.  Its attachment to a body  Then this division also differ in degrees of honor and perfection. For whoever is more honorable and higher, he is in himself more honorable and higher. And since the greatest of bodies is the Throne, inevitably the greatest of spirits related to the world of bodies is the spirit that manages the Throne. It is not unlikely that what is called the Greatest Spirit is that spirit, and it is also called the Universal Soul in the terminology of the philosophers, because that soul's body is the Throne, and everything that occurs within the Throne is as if part of its parts and a branch of its branches. Likewise, the souls managing those bodies are a branch of that soul and a result of its results. Then the second rank of the ranks of souls: the soul managing the Kursi, as it is said in the Divine Book: 'Who is there that can intercede with Him except by His permission?' up to His saying: 'His Kursi extends over the heavens and the earth.' And the reason for naming the eighth sphere the Kursi is that the Kursi is a body in which there are varying degrees of proximity and elevation, because the stars fixed in the eighth sphere are bodies differing in smallness and bigness, and those of those stars which the sight of creation perceives are arranged in seven ranks of magnitude. The smallest in body is that which is in the seventh magnitude, and it is in the lowest degree, and above it is that which is in the sixth magnitude. Thus it ascends rank by rank and degree by degree until it reaches the first magnitude. For this reason, in the thickness of the eighth sphere there occur varying degrees, and for this reason it is called the Kursi. And since Sirius (al-Shi'ra al-Yamaniyya) is the greatest in magnitude, its place must be in the highest degree and the most exalted rank. And I say: there is no doubt that each of those stars has properties and effects which none knows except Allah – glorified and exalted is He. The third rank of souls: the spirits managing the sphere of Saturn, and similarly for the rest of the layers of the heavens and the bodies of the planets according to their different degrees and the distances of their ranks, until it ends with the spirit managing the sphere of the moon. Then after these ranks: the spirits managing the sphere of the ether, then the sphere of the air, then the spirits managing the divisions of this world. That is because the sphere of the earth is divided into four divisions: the greatest of the four divisions are the seas; the second division is the deserts and wildernesses; the third division is the mountains; the fourth division is the inhabited areas. And it is not far-fetched in reason that for each of these divisions there should be one spirit or many spirits managing them. And all that we have mentioned is something spoken by the people of revelation and inspiration, for he (peace be upon him) used to say: 'The angel of the seas came to me and said such-and-such; the angel of the mountains came to me and said such-and-such; the angel of the rains came to me and said such-and-such; the angel of wars came to me.' And 'the keeper of Paradise is so-and-so, and the keeper of Hell is so-and-so.' And since each of these divisions is something possible in reason, and there is no evidence for its negation...

The Second Rank: The Spirit of the Kursi (Eighth Sphere)
Razi describes the second rank soul managing the Kursi and explains its name.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the reason for calling the eighth sphere the Kursi and the rank of its managing soul, including the significance of Sirius.

Core Idea
The Kursi is named for the varying degrees of stars within it, like a seat with steps.

After the Throne spirit, Razi turns to the second rank: the soul managing the Kursi. He explains why the eighth sphere is called Kursi (Seat): its thickness contains stars arranged in seven magnitudes, creating degrees like a seat. The largest star, Sirius (al-Shi'ra al-Yamaniyya), occupies the highest degree. This lesson details this intermediate celestial sphere and its spirit.

Guided Path
1
Read and note the Quranic reference
Razi quotes the verse about the Kursi. Reflect on the connection.
2
Understand the astronomical reasoning
Follow the explanation of the seven magnitudes of stars and why the sphere is called Kursi.
3
Note the special status of Sirius
Grasp why Sirius is placed at the highest degree.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

How does this awareness change the way you view the universe and your place in it? What practical step can you take today to cultivate deeper awe of Allah's creation?
?
Razi mentions that each star has unique properties and effects known only to Allah. How does this truth challenge your tendency to rely on your own knowledge or to think you have fully understood something? What area of your life do you need to humbly admit that only Allah knows the full reality?
?
The Kursi is named for its varying degrees, like steps. In your spiritual journey, are you content to stay at a lower 'magnitude' of faith, or are you striving to ascend to higher degrees of closeness to Allah? What specific effort will you make today to elevate your spiritual rank?

From Saturn to the Air: The Descent of Souls
Razi enumerates spirits from Saturn to the moon, then ether and air.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the continuation of the hierarchy from the spirit of Saturn through the planets down to the moon, and then the spirits of the ether and air spheres.

Core Idea
The hierarchy descends from Saturn through all planets to the moon, then to ether and air.

After the Throne and Kursi, Razi lists the third rank: the spirits managing the planets, starting with Saturn and continuing through all celestial spheres down to the moon. Then, beyond the moon, he mentions the spirits of the ether sphere and the air sphere. This lesson traces this downward progression.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and note the enumeration
The passage lists the third rank and then says the same applies to all heavens down to the moon.
2
Identify the spheres after the moon
Note the ether sphere and air sphere are mentioned next.
3
Observe the pattern
Each sphere has a managing spirit, following the same principle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما القسم الثاني من أقسام الأرواح : فهم الذين التفتوا إلى تدبير عالم الأجسام . وما بالغوا في الاستغراق في خدمة الجانب الأعلى إلى حيث يمنعهم عن التعلق بالجانب الأسفل ، وهؤلاء هم الملائكة العملية . وفي مصطلحات الفلاسفة هم المسمون بالنفوس .
كان تعلقه بجسم
ثم إن هذا القسم متفاوتون أيضاً في درجات الشرف والكمال . فكل من أشرف وأعلى ، كان هو في ذاته أشرف وأعلى . ولما كان أعظم الأجسام هو العرش ، لا جرم كان أعظم الأرواح المتعلقة بعالم الأجسام هو الروح المتصرف في العرش، ولا يبعد أن يكون المسمى بالروح الأعظم هو ذلك الروح ، وهو المسمى أيضاً : بالنفس الكلية في مصطلحات الفلاسفة ، لان تلك النفس بدنها العرش ، وكل ما حصل في داخل العرش ، فكأنه جزء من أجزائه ، وشعبة من شعبه . وكذلك النفوس المدبرة لتلك الأجسام تكون شعبة من شعب تلك النفس ، ونتيجة من نتائجها .
ثم المرتبة الثانية من مراتب النفوس : النفس المدبرة للكرسي ، كما قال في الكتاب الإلمي : من ذا الذي يشفع عنده إلا بإذنه ) ؟ إلى قوله تعالى : وسع كرسيه السموات والأرض ) والسبب في تسمية الفلك الثامن بالكرسي : أن الكرسي جسم حصل فيه درجات متفاوتة بالدنو والعلو ، لأن الكواكب المركوزة في الفلك الثامن : أجرام مختلفة بالصغر والكبر ، والتي أدركتها أبصار الخلق من تلك الكواكب مرتبة على سبع مراتب في العظم ، فأصغرها جرماً ، ما يكون في العظم السابع، وهو يكون في أدنى الدرجات ، وفوقها ما يكون في العظم السادس . وهكذا تترقى مرتبة فمرتبة ، ودرجة فدرجة ، إلى أن تصل إلى العظم الأول . ولهذا [ السبب ] حصل في ثخن الفلك الثامن : درجات متفاوتة . ولهذا سمي بالكرسي . ولما كانت الشعرى اليمانية ، أكبرها قدراً، وجب أن يكون مكانه في الدرجة الأعلى ، والمرتبة الأسمى والأسنى . وأقول : لا شك أن لكل واحد من تلك الكواكب خواص وآثار ، لا يعلمها إلا الله - سبحانه وتعالى .
والمرتبة الثالثة من النفوس : الأرواح المدبرة لفلك زحل وهكذا القول في سائر أطباق السموات ، وأجرام الكواكب على اختلاف درجاتها ، وتباعد مراتبها، حتى تنتهي إلى الروح المدبرة لكرة القمر .
ثم بعد هذه المراتب : الأرواح المدبرة لكرة الأثير [ ثم لكرة الهواء ] ثم الأرواح المدبرة لأقسام هذا العالم ، وذلك لأن كرة الأرض مقسومة بأربعة أقسام ، وأعظم الأقسام الأربعة : البحار والقسم الثاني : المفاوز ، والبيانات . والقسم الثالث : الجبال . والقسم الرابع : العمرانات ، ولا يبعد في العقل أن يحصل لكل قسم من هذه الأقسام : روح واحدة ، أو أرواح كثيرة مديرة لها . وكل ما ذكرناه ، مما نطق به أصحاب الوحي والتنزيل فإنه [ عليه السلام كان يقول ] : «جاءني ملك البحار ، فقال : كذا وكذا . وجاءني ملك الجبال ، فقال : كذا وكذا ، وجاءني ملك الأمطار ، فقال : كذا وكذا . وجاءني ملك الحروب ) . و ( خازن الجنة فلان ، وخازن النار فلان ، وإذا كان كل واحد من هذه الأقسام أمراً محتملاً في العقل ، ولم يوجد دليل على نفيه،
As for the second division of spirits: they are those who turned to the management of the world of bodies and did not go to extremes in absorption in the service of the higher aspect to the point that it would prevent them from attachment to the lower aspect. These are the practical angels. In the terminology of the philosophers, they are called souls.  Its attachment to a body  Then this division also differ in degrees of honor and perfection. For whoever is more honorable and higher, he is in himself more honorable and higher. And since the greatest of bodies is the Throne, inevitably the greatest of spirits related to the world of bodies is the spirit that manages the Throne. It is not unlikely that what is called the Greatest Spirit is that spirit, and it is also called the Universal Soul in the terminology of the philosophers, because that soul's body is the Throne, and everything that occurs within the Throne is as if part of its parts and a branch of its branches. Likewise, the souls managing those bodies are a branch of that soul and a result of its results. Then the second rank of the ranks of souls: the soul managing the Kursi, as it is said in the Divine Book: 'Who is there that can intercede with Him except by His permission?' up to His saying: 'His Kursi extends over the heavens and the earth.' And the reason for naming the eighth sphere the Kursi is that the Kursi is a body in which there are varying degrees of proximity and elevation, because the stars fixed in the eighth sphere are bodies differing in smallness and bigness, and those of those stars which the sight of creation perceives are arranged in seven ranks of magnitude. The smallest in body is that which is in the seventh magnitude, and it is in the lowest degree, and above it is that which is in the sixth magnitude. Thus it ascends rank by rank and degree by degree until it reaches the first magnitude. For this reason, in the thickness of the eighth sphere there occur varying degrees, and for this reason it is called the Kursi. And since Sirius (al-Shi'ra al-Yamaniyya) is the greatest in magnitude, its place must be in the highest degree and the most exalted rank. And I say: there is no doubt that each of those stars has properties and effects which none knows except Allah – glorified and exalted is He. The third rank of souls: the spirits managing the sphere of Saturn, and similarly for the rest of the layers of the heavens and the bodies of the planets according to their different degrees and the distances of their ranks, until it ends with the spirit managing the sphere of the moon. Then after these ranks: the spirits managing the sphere of the ether, then the sphere of the air, then the spirits managing the divisions of this world. That is because the sphere of the earth is divided into four divisions: the greatest of the four divisions are the seas; the second division is the deserts and wildernesses; the third division is the mountains; the fourth division is the inhabited areas. And it is not far-fetched in reason that for each of these divisions there should be one spirit or many spirits managing them. And all that we have mentioned is something spoken by the people of revelation and inspiration, for he (peace be upon him) used to say: 'The angel of the seas came to me and said such-and-such; the angel of the mountains came to me and said such-and-such; the angel of the rains came to me and said such-and-such; the angel of wars came to me.' And 'the keeper of Paradise is so-and-so, and the keeper of Hell is so-and-so.' And since each of these divisions is something possible in reason, and there is no evidence for its negation...
Source pages p. 20

Commentary
This passage classifies the second division of spirits and then delineates a hierarchical order of souls (nufus) managing the cosmos. The author begins by defining the second division: these spirits are not completely absorbed in the higher aspect but instead engage in managing the material world; they are the 'practical angels' (al-mala'ika al-'amaliyya), equivalent to the 'souls' (al-nufus) in philosophical terminology. He then states that these spirits vary in degrees of honor and perfection corresponding to the bodies they manage. Since the greatest body is the Throne ('arsh), the greatest spirit is the one managing the Throne, called the Greatest Spirit (al-ruh al-a'zam) and in philosophy the Universal Soul (al-nafs al-kulliyya). Its body is the Throne, and all other bodies within the Throne are parts or branches of it. The second rank is the soul managing the Kursi (the eighth sphere, or the 'Seat'). The author explains why the eighth sphere is called Kursi: the stars within it are arranged in seven degrees of magnitude, giving the sphere thickness and thus a 'seat' structure. The largest star, Sirius (al-Shi'ra al-Yamaniyya), occupies the highest degree. The third rank consists of spirits managing the sphere of Saturn, and similarly for all seven heavens down to the moon. Subsequently, the spirits managing the ether sphere, then the air sphere, then the four divisions of the earth (seas, deserts, mountains, and inhabited areas) are listed. The author supports the possibility of such spirits with prophetic traditions mentioning angels of seas, mountains, rains, wars, and the keepers of Paradise and Hell. The passage concludes that since each of these is rationally possible and there is no evidence to negate it, the classification is valid.

Key Terms
نفس
فلك زحل
كرة القمر
الأثير
الهواء

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Earth and water
B) Ether and air
C) Throne and Kursi
D) Paradise and Hell

Key Takeaway
The hierarchy continues through all planetary spheres down to the moon, then to ether and air.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at the night sky and remember that each celestial body from Saturn to the moon has a spirit assigned to manage it, does your heart feel the awe of God's precise order? What specific time today can you set aside to contemplate that order and let it deepen your gratitude?
?
If the spirits are ranked by nearness to God, with the highest being closest to the divine light, where would you place your own soul right now? Are you content with being at the 'air' level, or will you take one concrete action today to purify your intentions and rise spiritually?
?
Razi describes a descending hierarchy from the highest heavens down to the air around us. In your life, have you allowed your own spiritual state to descend lower than your God-given potential? Write down one area where you have settled for less and commit to one step to reverse that decline today.

The Spirits of the Earth and Prophetic Confirmation
Razi lists the four earth divisions and supports them with prophetic traditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four divisions of the earth managed by spirits and the prophetic evidence supporting this classification.

Core Idea
The earth is divided into four regions, each with its own managing spirit, confirmed by prophetic traditions.

Razi completes the hierarchy by descending to the terrestrial realm. He divides the earth into four major divisions: seas, deserts, mountains, and inhabited areas, each with one or more spirits. He then supports this classification by citing prophetic traditions where angels of seas, mountains, rains, wars, and keepers of Paradise and Hell are mentioned. This lesson connects the rational hierarchy with scriptural evidence.

Guided Path
1
Read the four divisions
Identify the four divisions of the earth: seas, deserts, mountains, inhabited areas.
2
Note the rational possibility
Razi says it is not far-fetched that each division has a spirit.
3
Examine the prophetic support
He quotes traditions about various angels. Reflect on how this supports the classification.

Third Proof: The Self Endures While the Body Perishes
The author argues from memory continuity that the self is a lasting substance distinct from the body.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the continuity of memory proves that the self is a lasting entity distinct from the perishable body.

Core Idea
Memory continuity shows the self is not the body.

This passage presents a powerful argument from self-evident experience. If you have ever remembered something from the past, you have direct evidence that you are not merely your physical body which constantly changes. The author uses this simple observation to establish a central claim of Islamic theology: the soul (or self) is a permanent substance separate from the body.

Guided Path
1
Understand the hypothetical scenario
Read the conditional premise: if the first state perishes and a second state comes, the second state has no knowledge of the first. This sets up the logical consequence.
Notice the word 'البتة' which emphasizes absolute lack of connection.
2
Recognize the consequence for knowledge
The consequence is that if the body completely perishes and a new body comes, a person would have no memory of the past – only knowledge of the present. Consider what that would mean for your own experience.
3
Observe the reality and draw conclusions
Since we do have memories of the past, the hypothetical is false. The 'I' that we refer to must be something lasting from beginning to end. From this, the author derives three conclusions about the body, man (self), and their relationship.
Note the use of 'علوم بديهية' (self-evident sciences) to assert that the lasting cannot be identical to the non-lasting.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والوجه الثالث : أنه إذا فني الأول وجاء الثاني فهذا الثاني غير الأول ولا خبر له من حال الأول البتة فوجب أن لا يحصل للإنسان علم بأحواله الماضية ، وأن لا يحصل عنده من العلوم إلا العلوم بالأحوال الحاضرة . وحيث لم يكن الأمر كذلك ، علمنا أن المشار إليه لكل أحد بقوله : « أنا شيء باق من أول العمر إلى آخره » فثبت بهذه البيانات : أن الجسد غير باق ، لا بمجموعه ولا بشيء من أجزائه ، وثبت : أن الإنسان باق. ومن العلوم البديهية : أن الباقي لا يكون عين ما هو غير باق . وهذا يقتضي أن الإنسان شيء مغاير لهذا البدن ، وشيء مغاير لهذه الأعضاء المحسوسة التي منها يتولد هذا البدن . وهو المطلوب
And the third proof: that if the first [state] perishes and the second comes, then this second is not the first and has no knowledge of the circumstances of the first at all. So it is necessary that man does not acquire knowledge of his past circumstances, and that he does not acquire from sciences except knowledge of present circumstances. But since it is not so, we know that that which is pointed to by every person when he says, 'I am something lasting from the beginning of life to its end.' So it is established by these evidences that the body is not lasting, neither in its entirety nor in any of its parts, and it is established that man is lasting. And from self-evident sciences: that the lasting cannot be identical to that which is not lasting. And this requires that man is something other than this body, and something other than these sensible limbs from which this body is generated. And that is what is sought.
Source pages p. 104

Commentary
This passage presents the third proof that the self (anā) is a permanent entity distinct from the physical body. The author first posits a conditional: if the first state perishes and a second state arrives, the second state has no access to the first. Consequently, a person would have no memory of past experiences and only perceive the present. Yet obviously humans do remember the past, so the reference of 'I' must be a constant element persisting throughout life. From this, the author draws three conclusions: (1) the body is not permanent, neither wholly nor partially; (2) man (the self) is permanent; and (3) since the permanent cannot be identical to the perishable, man is therefore distinct from the body and its parts. The argument uses self-evident premises about memory and identity to establish the incorporeal nature of the soul.

Key Terms
أنا
جسد
باقٍ
علوم بديهية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the proof relies on assuming the soul's existence from the start.
Correction: Rather, it starts from the observable fact of memory continuity, which is a self-evident premise.

A) The body is permanent and the soul is perishable.
B) Man is a combination of body and soul that perishes together.
C) Man is a lasting entity distinct from the perishable body.
D) The self is identical to the physical body.

Key Takeaway
The continuity of memory provides self-evident proof that the self is a lasting substance distinct from the perishable body.

What might change if you truly saw yourself as a lasting substance, not just a collection of changing cells?
?
This proof shows that the self is distinct from the body. If your body will perish but your self endures, what weight does that place on your choices today? Are you living as if your 'I' is heading somewhere eternal, or are you consumed by the temporary?
?
You have knowledge of your past actions—both good and bad. That memory is evidence that the same self that committed those deeds still exists. How will you face that self at the end of your life? What accountability does this proof demand of you right now?

Bodily Discipline and the Soul-Body Distinction
The author contrasts bodily indulgence and asceticism to show soul and body are distinct.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the contrasting effects of bodily indulgence and asceticism demonstrate that the soul is distinct from the body.

Core Idea
What benefits the soul harms the body, proving they are distinct.

The author presents a compelling thought experiment: observe how excessive eating and amusement dull the mind, while fasting and sensory deprivation sharpen the soul. This inverse relationship reveals that soul and body are fundamentally distinct entities.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the contrast between the two scenarios: bodily indulgence vs. ascetic discipline.
Pay attention to the proverb cited.
2
Identify the two contrasting states
Identify the first scenario (constant eating, drinking, amusement) and its effect on the intellectual faculty. Then identify the second scenario (fasting, sensory deprivation) and its effect on the soul and body.
3
Understand the logical conclusion
The author concludes that what causes happiness for the soul causes misery for the body, and vice versa. This shows that soul and body are distinct substances.
The conclusion is that soul is something other than body and its parts.
4
Reflect on the proof's strength
Consider how this empirical observation supports the earlier argument about the soul's distinctness. Why does this contrast serve as proof?

واعلم: أن الاعتبار المذكور يختص بالقوة النظرية، وهو أيضاً قائم في رياضة الإنسان إذا كان موظباً على الأكل والشرب والتفرج والتنزه بالجسمانيات، فإنه تختل قوته العقلية، وتفسد، ولا يبقى معه منها شيء يلتفت إليه، أو يعتبر به. فإن من المشهور في المثل: (البطنة تذهب بالفطنة). أما إذا راض الإنسان نفسه بالامتناع عن المأكولات، والانقطاع عن اللذات الجسمانية، وجلس في بيت مظلم بحيث لا يبصر شيئاً ولا يسمع شيئاً، وتكلف إزالة الصور المحسوسة والمثل الجسمانية عن الفكر والخيال، وواظب على هذه الحالة مدة مديدة فإنه تصير نفسه في غاية الكمال، وبدنه في غاية الضعف والنحافة. وهذا الاعتبار يدل على أن كل ما كان سبباً لسعادة النفس، فهو بعينه يصير سبباً لشقاوة البدن. وكل ما كان سبباً لشقاوة النفس فهو بعينه سبب لسعادة البدن. وذلك يدل على أن النفس شيء غير البدن، وغير أجزاء هذا البدن وأعضائه.
Know that the aforementioned consideration pertains specifically to the theoretical faculty, and it is also present in the discipline (رياضة) of a person: if he is constant in eating, drinking, amusement, and recreation with bodily things, then his intellectual faculty becomes disordered and corrupted, and nothing of it remains that is regarded or considered. For it is well-known in the proverb: 'Gluttony destroys intelligence.' But if a person disciplines himself by refraining from foods and cutting off from bodily pleasures, sits in a dark room where he sees nothing and hears nothing, and takes upon himself to remove sensible images and bodily forms from thought and imagination, and perseveres in this state for a long period, then his soul becomes in utmost perfection and his body in utmost weakness and thinness. This consideration indicates that everything that is a cause of happiness (سعادة) of the soul, the very same becomes a cause of misery (شقاوة) of the body. And everything that is a cause of misery of the soul is the very same cause of happiness of the body. And that indicates that the soul is something other than the body and other than the parts of this body and its organs.

Commentary
This passage extends the previous proof by considering the effects of bodily indulgence vs. asceticism. It shows that the same thing (e.g., fasting) cannot benefit both soul and body; it benefits one and harms the other. Therefore, soul and body are distinct substances.

The Third Proof: Sleep as Death for the Body, Life for the Soul
The author gives a third proof from the nature of sleep and dreams.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the phenomenon of sleep and true dreams provides evidence for the immateriality and independent existence of the soul.

Core Idea
Sleep reveals the soul's wakefulness when the body sleeps.

The author presents a third argument based on sleep. During wakefulness, the soul is heedless of the unseen; during sleep, the soul awakens to that world. This contrast shows the soul's distinct nature and its ability to exist independently of the body.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation carefully. Notice the key terms: نوم (sleep), يقظة (wakefulness), عالم الغيب (world of the unseen), and منامات صادقة (true dreams).
The passage ends mid-sentence; the conclusion likely continues on the next page.
2
Identify the key analogy
Identify the analogy the author draws between sleep and death/wakefulness. Sleep is like death for the body but like wakefulness for the soul.
3
Trace the logical progression
Follow the argument: in wakefulness, the soul is asleep to the unseen. When the body sleeps, the soul awakens and connects to the unseen, giving true dreams. This shows the soul's different nature.
The conclusion is that the soul's life, happiness, and wakefulness depend on separation from the body.

Then when he wakes up, wakefulness occurs for the body, and the soul becomes asleep, cut off from the world of the unseen. So it is established by these three faces that the life of the soul, its happiness, and its wakefulness

If Soul Were Body, Resurrection Impossible: The First Premise
Razi argues the first premise: if soul = body, resurrection impossible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's first premise that if the soul were the body, resurrection would be impossible, and how he defends it against the objection of bodily continuity.

Core Idea
If the soul were the body, the person would be lost when the body decays, making resurrection impossible.

Razi presents a compelling argument: if the soul were nothing but the body, then when the body disintegrates and perishes, the individual would cease to exist entirely. Resurrection would require recreating an identical being, which reason deems impossible. He then addresses a potential objection that only accidents perish while matter persists, showing that this fails to preserve the individual's identity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how Razi structures the argument: conditional statement, observation of decay, impossibility of returning exactly after annihilation.
2
Understand the objection raised
After the initial argument, Razi presents an objection: perhaps man is just the enduring particles, and death removes only accidents. Identify the objection and its reasoning.
The objector claims that the same particles remain, so the same person can return when accidents return.
3
Follow Razi's refutation
Razi refutes by showing that the individual is defined by specific attributes (knowledge, power, life) attached to the body. When these perish, a constituent part of the individual's quiddity is lost, and reviving a non-existent accident is impossible.
The key point: the person is not just matter but matter with specific essential accidents.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة العاشرة : لو كانت النفس هذا البدن ، أو عضواً من أعضاء هذا البدن ، لامتنع الحشر والنشر والثواب والعقاب ، ولو كان الأمر كذلك ، لكان الإنسان أخس الحيوانات ولكان تكوينه وإيجاده عبثاً محضاً . وهذه اللوازم باطلة ، فكان القول بأن النفس عين هذا البدن ، أو عضو من أعضائه : باطلاً . فنفتقر في تقرير هذا الدليل إلى تقرير مقدمات . المقدمة الأولى : في بيان أن النفس ، لو كانت عبارة عن هذا البدن ، أو عن عضو من أعضائه ، لكان القول بالبعث والقيامة باطلا . فتقريره : إنا نشاهد أن هذا البدن مع جميع أعضائه وأجزائه يتفرق ويتمزق ، ويصير فانياً بالكلية . والشيء إذا فني وعدم فإعادته بعينه بعد فنائه : محال في بديهة العقل . فإن قالوا : لم لا يجوز أن يقال : الإنسان عبارة عن أجزاء مخصوصة ، فإذا مات الإنسان وتفرقت تلك الأجزاء والأعضاء ، فالزائل إنما هو الصفات والأعراض ، وأما تلك الأجزاء فهي بأعيانها باقية . فلا جرم كان ذلك الإنسان باقياً بعينه . فإذا عادت تلك الأعراض إليه ، فإن هذا الإنسان الموجود في هذه الحالة ، هو عين الإنسان الذي كان موجوداً قبل ذلك . فنقول : هذا باطل ، لأن بتقدير أن يكون الإنسان عبارة عن جسم مخصوص ، فإنه يمتنع أن يقال : ذلك الإنسان عبارة عن مجرد تلك الأجسام ، لأن تلك الأجسام ، قد كانت موجودة قبل أن تركبت واتصفت بتلك الصفات، وما كان هذا الإنسان : موجوداً . وإذا مات ذلك الإنسان بعد حياته ، وتفرقت تلك الأجزاء . فتلك الأجزاء باقية ، مع أن ذلك الإنسان غير باق . فعلمنا : أن بتقدير أن يكون الإنسان جسما ، فإنه لا يكون الإنسان المخصوص عبارة عن مجرد الجسمية ، بل يجب أن يكون عبارة عن جسم موصوف بصفات مخصوصة . وهي : العلم والقدرة والحياة. وإذا كان الإنسان عبارة عن مجموع تلك الذوات مع هذه الصفات ، فعند فناء هذه الصفات ، قد عدم أحد الأجزاء المعتبرة في تحقق هذا الإنسان ، من حيث إنه هذا الإنسان . ولما ثبت بحكم بديهة العقل : أن إعادة المعدوم : محال ، ثبت : أن عود ذلك العرض الذي هو أحد أجزاء ماهية ذلك الإنسان : محال . فيلزم : أن يكون عود ذلك الإنسان بعينه محالاً . فثبت بما ذكرناه : أنه لو كان النفس عبارة عن هذا البدن ، أو عن عضو من أعضاء هذا البدن لكان القول بالبعث والقيامة : باطلاً ، ولكان بقاء النفس بعد موت البدن : باطلا . وهذا برهان قوي في إثبات هذا المطلوب . وأما بيان المقدمة الثانية : وهي قولنا : لو كان القول بالبعث باطلاً
The tenth argument: If the soul were this body, or an organ of this body, then the gathering, dispersal, reward, and punishment would be impossible, and if that were the case, then man would be the basest of animals, and his formation and existence would be pure futility. These consequences are false, so the statement that the soul is identical to this body, or an organ of its organs, is false. So we need in establishing this proof to establish premises. The first premise: Explaining that if the soul were this body, or an organ of its organs, then the statement of resurrection and standing would be false. Its explanation: We observe that this body with all its organs and parts disintegrates, is torn apart, and becomes completely annihilated. And if a thing perishes and is non-existent, then its return exactly after its perishing is impossible in the intuition of the intellect. If they say: Why is it not permissible to say that man consists of specific parts, and when man dies and those parts and organs become separated, what perishes are only the qualities and accidents, but those parts themselves remain, so necessarily that man remains identical; so if those accidents return to him, then this man existing in this state is the identical man who existed before? We say: This is false because, supposing that man is a specific body, it is impossible to say that that man consists of merely those bodies, because those bodies existed before they combined and were characterized by those qualities, and this man did not exist. And when that man dies after his life, and those parts separate, those parts remain, while that man does not remain. So we know that, supposing man is a body, the specific man is not merely corporeality, but must be a body described with specific qualities: knowledge, power, and life. And if man is the sum of those essences with these qualities, then upon the annihilation of these qualities, one of the parts considered in the realization of this man, in respect that he is this man, has ceased to exist. And since it is established by intuition of the intellect that reviving the non-existent is impossible, it is established that the return of that accident which is one of the parts of the quiddity of that man is impossible. Consequently, the return of that identical man is impossible. So it is established by what we have mentioned that if the soul were this body, or an organ of the organs of this body, then the statement of resurrection and standing would be false, and the survival of the soul after the death of the body would be false. And this is a strong proof in establishing this desired conclusion. As for the explanation of the second premise: namely our statement: if the statement of resurrection is false...
Source pages p. 124

Commentary
This passage presents the tenth argument for the soul's non-physical nature by examining the implications for bodily resurrection. The author begins by stating the conditional: if the soul were the body or a bodily organ, then resurrection, reward and punishment would be impossible, leading to the absurd conclusion that man is the basest creature and creation is pointless. Since these consequences are false, the premise (soul = body) is false. To solidify the proof, he introduces premises. The first premise argues that if the soul were the body, then resurrection is impossible. The proof: the body disintegrates to nothing; reviving something exactly after annihilation is intuitively impossible. An objection is raised: perhaps man is just the enduring particles, and death only removes accidents; those particles remain, so the same man can return when accidents return. The author refutes this: those particles existed before which were not that man; after death, the particles remain but the individual man is gone. The individual man is not mere corpuscles but a body with specific essential attributes (knowledge, power, life). Once these attributes perish, a constituent part of that individual's quiddity is lost, and since reviving the non-existent is impossible, the identical man cannot return. Thus the conclusion is reaffirmed: if the soul were the body, resurrection and the soul's survival after death would be impossible. The passage ends as it begins to introduce the second premise, which is cut off.

Key Terms
إعادة المعدوم (iʿādat al-maʿdūm)
العرض (al-ʿaraḍ)
الماهية (al-māhiyya)
الحشر (al-ḥashr)
النشر (al-nashr)
البعث (al-baʿth)
القيامة (al-qiyāma)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if the body's atoms persist, the same person can be resurrected exactly as before.
Correction: Razi argues that the individual person is not just the matter but a body with specific essential attributes (knowledge, power, life). When these attributes perish, a constituent part of the person's quiddity is lost, and reviving a non-existent accident is impossible. Thus mere material continuity is insufficient for personal identity.

Checkpoint
According to Razi's first premise, why is resurrection impossible if the soul were the body?
A) Because the soul would be trapped in the body forever.
B) Because the body never truly perishes, only changes form.
C) Because the body becomes completely annihilated and reviving the identical non-existent thing is impossible.
D) Because resurrection is only for non-physical souls.

Key Takeaway
Razi's first premise establishes that if the soul were the body, resurrection would be impossible because the body perishes entirely, and the individual is defined by perishable attributes that cannot be restored.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You believe in resurrection, but have you ever truly confronted the fact that your body will one day be dust? If your soul were nothing more than this decaying body, what hope would you have of being raised again? How does Razi's premise force you to reconsider the necessity of an immaterial soul for your own personal survival after death? What would you lose if you could not claim 'I am not my body'?
?
Razi identifies three essential accidents that define a man: knowledge, power, and life. Which of these three do you most deeply attach to your sense of self? If at death these were simply gone, would you still be you? And if not, what are you doing today to ensure that these qualities—especially knowledge and life—are not merely accidental but rooted in an eternal soul?
?
The objection that 'man is just particles' is scientifically tempting, but Razi dismantles it by showing that the person is not the same before and after composition. You have changed greatly since you were a child—your cells replaced, your knowledge increased. What part of you remains constant through all change? If you cannot point to an immaterial soul, are you prepared to accept that your own identity is an illusion? How does that affect your fear of death?

Razi's Proof of the Soul's Survival: The Argument from Intellect
Razi presents a reductio argument for the soul's survival.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's rational argument that the soul must survive death, based on the purpose of intellect.

Core Idea
If intellect harms in this life, it must benefit after death.

Razi uses a clever reductio ad absurdum to show that the soul cannot perish with the body. He argues that intellect, humanity's distinguishing trait, would be a net harm without an afterlife. Since we know humans are superior, the soul must survive. This passage is a classic rational proof for the immortality of the soul.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation once to grasp the overall logical flow. Notice the conditional structure and the appeal to intuition.
Pay attention to the phrase 'بديهة العقل' (rational intuition) as it is key to the argument.
2
Identify the reductio structure
Razi assumes the opposite of the conclusion (the soul perishes) and derives a contradiction: humans would be the lowest animals, which is false. Trace how he arrives at this absurdity.
3
Understand the role of intuition
The argument relies on the intuitive truth that humans are the noblest animals. Reflect on why this premise is taken as self-evident in the argument.
This shows that rational proofs can start from obvious truths.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إذا ثبت هذا ، فنقول : إن كون العقل موجباً للكمال والسعادة ، إنما يحصل بعد الموت. فإذا كان الغرض هو أن النفس لا تبقى البتة بعد موت البدن ، لم يحصل من العقل منفعة فيما بعد الموت ، وقد بينا أنه من أقوى الأسباب الموجبة لتنغيص هذه اللذات الجسدانية ولتكديرها ، وإبطال الانتفاع بها . وحينئذ يظهر [ عن هذا (۱) ] أنه لو لم تكن النفس باقية بعد موت البدن (٢) لكان الإنسان أخس الحيوانات وأرذلها ، وأدناها قدراً وقيمة . فإن امتيازها عن غيرها، ليس إلا بالعقل الذي لا فائدة فيه. إلا تنقيص [ الخيرات (۳) ] وتنغيص اللذات والطيبات. ولما شهدت بديهة العقل بأن هذا اللازم باطل ، وأن الإنسان أشرف الحيوانات وأعلاها قدراً وقيمة ، بسبب حصول هذا العقل له ، وظهر أن العقل سبب نقصان الخيرات الجسمانية العاجلة ، وجب القطع بأن كمال العقل [ إنما حصل (1) ] وجلالته ورفعته ، إنما حصل بسبب أنه سبب الحصول الكمالات فيما بعد موت (٥) البدن . وهذا يدل على أن النفس غير البدن ، وعلى أنها باقية بعد موت البدن
If this is established, we say: The intellect being a cause of perfection and happiness only occurs after death. If the intent is that the soul does not remain at all after the death of the body, then no benefit from intellect is obtained after death, and we have shown that it is among the strongest causes that spoil these bodily pleasures, disturb them, and nullify enjoyment of them. Then it becomes apparent from this that if the soul were not remaining after the death of the body, man would be the worst of animals, the most contemptible, and the lowest in rank and value. For his distinction from others is only by intellect, which has no benefit except diminishing goods and spoiling pleasures and delights. And since the intuition of the intellect testifies that this necessary consequence is false, and that man is the noblest of animals and the highest in rank and value due to his possession of this intellect, and it has become clear that intellect is a cause of decrease in immediate bodily goods, it must be concluded that the perfection of intellect, its majesty and loftiness, only occur because it is a cause of obtaining perfections after the death of the body. And this indicates that the soul is other than the body, and that it remains after the death of the body.
Source pages p. 127

Commentary
This passage is a rational proof by Razi for the survival of the soul after bodily death, using a reductio ad absurdum. The argument proceeds in steps: (1) Intellect (al-'aql) is supposed to bring perfection and happiness, but this only happens after death. (2) If the soul does not survive death, then intellect offers no post-mortem benefit. (3) Moreover, in this life, intellect actually diminishes bodily pleasures and causes distress. (4) Therefore, if the soul perishes, intellect becomes a harm, making humans the lowest of animals since their distinguishing trait causes only loss. (5) But common sense and rational intuition (badīhat al-'aql) affirm that humans are the noblest of animals due to intellect. (6) Hence the premise that the soul does not survive must be false. (7) Therefore, the perfection of intellect is only meaningful if it leads to perfections after death, proving the soul is distinct from the body and survives.

Key Terms
العقل
النفس
البدن
الكمال
السعادة
بديهة العقل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think Razi argues that intellect is purely harmful in this life.
Correction: Razi only shows that intellect diminishes worldly pleasures; its true benefit is in the afterlife, which is why the soul must survive.

Checkpoint
According to Razi's argument, if the soul does not survive death, what would be the consequence for human status?
A) Humans would be the noblest of animals
B) Humans would be equal to other animals
C) Humans would be the lowest and most contemptible of animals
D) Humans would cease to exist entirely

Key Takeaway
Razi's argument proves the soul's survival by showing that intellect, humanity's distinguishing trait, would otherwise be a harm, contradicting human nobility.

Proof of the Soul's Survival: The Argument from Futility
Razi enumerates a trilemma to prove the soul's survival.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's argument that if the soul did not survive death, human creation would be futile, thus proving the soul's distinctness and persistence after the body.

Core Idea
If creation has purpose, the soul must survive death.

This passage presents a key argument for the soul's survival: without it, human creation would be futile (عبثاً). Razi uses a trilemma to show that humans are created for good, and that good can only be attained in an afterlife. This argument establishes both that the soul is distinct from the body and that it continues after death.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its English translation to get the full argument. Notice the logical structure of the trilemma.
Pay attention to the three possibilities Razi lists.
2
Analyze the trilemma
Razi presents three possibilities for why humans are created: for good, for evil, or for neither. He rejects the second and third, leaving only the first.
Consider why the third possibility is equivalent to remaining in original non-existence.
3
Connect to the conclusion
Since the good must be attained after death (this world is full of tribulation), the soul must survive the body and be distinct from it.
Razi concludes that the soul remains either in happiness or misery.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما بيان المقدمة الثالثة : وهي قولنا : لو لم تكن النفس باقية بعد موت البدن ، لكان خلق الإنسان عبثاً. فالدليل عليه : أن هذا الإنسان إما أن يكون مخلوقاً للخير والمصلحة والراحة ، أو يكون مخلوقاً للضرر والبلاء ، أو لا يعتبر أحد هذين القسمين. أما القسم الثاني ، فهو بعيد لما ثبت : أن الخير غالب ، والشر مغلوب . وأما القسم الثالث ، فباطل . لأن هذا المعنى ، كان حاصلا عند البقاء على العدم الأصلي. فلم يبق إلا أن الحق هو القسم الأول ، وهو أنه خلق للفوز بالجنة ، والراحة ، والحكمة ، والمصلحة . فنقول : محل الفوز بتلك الخيرات ، إما في هذه الحياة الجسمانية وإما بعدها. والأول باطل . لأن دار الدنيا ، دار البلاء والعناء والشقاء . وسيأتي تقرير هذه المقدمات في كتاب الأخلاق عند مذمة هذه الحياة الجسمانية على سبيل الاستقصاء . ولما بطل هذا القسم ، ثبت : أن المقصود من تكوين هذه النفوس البشرية : فوزها بالخيرات والسعادات فيها بعد موت البدن. وهذا يدل على أن النفس غير البدن ، وغير جملة أجزائه وأبعاضه ، وعلى أن النفس باقية بعد موت البدن إما في السعادة والسلامة ، أو في الشقاوة والعذاب. وليكن ههنا آخر كلامنا في إثبات النفس . والله أعلم
As for the exposition of the third premise: our statement: 'If the soul were not remaining after the death of the body, the creation of man would be futile.' The proof for it is: This man is either created for good, benefit, and comfort, or created for harm and affliction, or neither of these two categories is considered. As for the second category, it is unlikely because it has been established that good predominates and evil is overcome. As for the third category, it is invalid because this meaning was already obtained by remaining in original non-existence. So nothing remains except that the truth is the first category: that he is created for attaining Paradise, comfort, wisdom, and benefit. We say: The place of attaining those goods is either in this bodily life or after it. The first is invalid because the abode of this world is the abode of tribulation, toil, and misery. The detailed exposition of these premises will come in the Book of Ethics on the dispraise of this bodily life exhaustively. And when this category is invalidated, it is established that the purpose of the formation of these human souls is their attainment of goods and happinesses after the death of the body. And this indicates that the soul is other than the body and other than all its parts and limbs, and that the soul remains after the death of the body, either in happiness and safety or in misery and torment. Let this be the end of our discourse on proving the soul. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 127

Commentary
This passage presents the third premise in a larger proof for the soul's survival: that without survival, creation would be futile (عبثاً). Razi offers a trilemma: humans are created for good, for evil, or for neither. The second is rejected because good predominates in the world. The third is rejected because it would be equivalent to remaining in original non-existence, which is absurd. Thus humans must be created for good, specifically to attain Paradise and wisdom. The next question is where this attainment occurs. It cannot be in this worldly life because this world is full of tribulation and misery. Therefore it must occur after death. Hence the soul must survive the body, and it is distinct from the body. The passage concludes with a note that this will be further elaborated in his Book of Ethics, and declares this the end of the proof of the soul.

Key Terms
العبث
الخلق
الفوز
الجنة
البلاء
العدم الأصلي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument assumes all humans go to Paradise.
Correction: Razi explicitly states that the soul survives either in happiness (safety) or misery (torment), so both outcomes are possible.

Checkpoint
According to Razi's argument, why is the possibility that humans are created for neither good nor evil invalid?

B) Because that state already existed in original non-existence
C) Because humans are inherently good
D) Because it contradicts revelation

Key Takeaway
Razi argues that the soul must survive death because otherwise human creation would be futile, and the ultimate good intended for humans cannot be fully realized in this world.

When you feel that your efforts in this world are wasted, do you remember that the real reward is after death? How can you cultivate that awareness in your daily struggles?
?
This passage concludes that the soul either ends in happiness or misery. Are you confident that your current path leads to the former? What concrete evidence from your life supports that confidence, and what would you need to change if you are unsure?

Proof of the Soul's Immateriality and Immortality
Al-Razi argues from the purpose of creation that the soul is immaterial and immortal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Razi's teleological argument for the soul's immateriality and immortality based on the purpose of creation.

Core Idea
The soul is not the body; it survives death to attain true happiness.

Al-Razi presents a concise but powerful argument for the existence of the soul as a distinct, immortal substance. He invites us to reflect on the purpose of our creation: if this world is full of suffering, then the goods we seek must be attained after death. This reasoning not only establishes the soul's distinctness from the body but also its survival after death. Let us examine his argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read al-Razi's argument
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation carefully. Try to follow the logical flow from the premise about goods to the conclusion about the soul.
Notice that al-Razi starts by setting up a dichotomy between worldly and afterlife goods.
2
Identify the two possibilities
Al-Razi divides goods into two categories: in this bodily life or after it. Understand why he rejects the first category.
He rejects worldly goods because this world is a place of trial and misery.
3
Trace the conclusion
See how the rejection leads to the conclusion that the purpose of creating souls is attainment of goods after death. Then note what this implies about the soul.
The conclusion has two parts: the soul is not the body, and it survives death.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider how this argument proves both that the soul is distinct from the body and that it continues after death. This is a key proof in Islamic theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخيرات ، إما في هذه الحياة الجسمانية وإما بعدها. والأول باطل . لأن دار الدنيا ، دار البلاء والعناء والشقاء . وسيأتي تقرير هذه المقدمات في كتاب الأخلاق عند مذمة هذه الحياة الجسمانية على سبيل الاستقصاء . ولما بطل هذا القسم ، ثبت : أن المقصود من تكوين هذه النفوس البشرية : فوزها بالخيرات والسعادات فيها بعد موت البدن. وهذا يدل على أن النفس غير البدن ، وغير جملة أجزائه وأبعاضه ، وعلى أن النفس باقية بعد موت البدن إما في السعادة والسلامة ، أو في الشقاوة والعذاب. وليكن ههنا آخر كلامنا في إثبات النفس . والله أعلم
The goods are either in this bodily life or after it. And the first is false, because the abode of this world is the abode of trial, hardship, and misery. And the confirmation of these premises will come in the Book of Ethics at the dispraise of this bodily life in an exhaustive manner. And when this part is invalidated, it is established that the purpose of creating these human souls is their attainment of goods and happiness after the death of the body. And this indicates that the soul is not the body, nor any of its parts and limbs, and that the soul remains after the death of the body, either in happiness and safety or in misery and punishment. And let this be the end of our speech in proving the soul. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 128

Commentary
This passage is a deductive proof for the existence of the soul as an immaterial substance that survives bodily death. Al-Razi begins by dividing the possible objects of human good (al-khayrat) into two categories: those attainable in this bodily life and those after it. He argues that the first category is invalid because this world is a place of trial (bala'), hardship ('ana'), and misery (shaqa'), not a place of ultimate fulfillment. Therefore, the true purpose for which human souls are created must be the attainment of goods and happiness after the death of the body. From this purpose, he draws two important conclusions: (1) the soul is not identical to the body or any of its parts or limbs, and (2) the soul continues to exist after death, experiencing either happiness and safety or misery and punishment. The argument thus establishes both the immateriality and immortality of the soul through teleological reasoning. He concludes by marking this as the end of his discussion on proving the soul.

Key Terms
النفس (al-nafs)
البدن (al-badan)
الخيرات (al-khayrat)
دار الدنيا (dar al-dunya)
السعادة (al-sa'ada)
الشقاوة (al-shaqawa)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think this argument is circular because it assumes an afterlife purpose to prove the soul's survival.
Correction: Al-Razi does not assume an afterlife; he reasons from the shared premise that humans seek goods. He then argues that since worldly goods are unattainable (this world is trial), the only remaining possibility is afterlife goods, which logically requires a surviving soul.

Checkpoint
What does al-Razi's argument conclude about the relationship between the soul and the body?
A) The soul is a part of the body and dies with it.
B) The soul is distinct from the body and survives after death.
C) The soul is identical to the body but experiences happiness after death.
D) The soul is created for worldly goods only.

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi's argument shows that the soul must be distinct from the body and immortal because true goods are unattainable in this world, implying an afterlife purpose.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believed that this world is only a place of trial and not where you will find your ultimate good, what would you change about your priorities starting today?
?
Al-Razi says the soul remains after death either in happiness or misery. Which outcome does your current life most resemble preparing for, and what one concrete step can you take to align yourself with the purpose for which you were created?
?
The purpose of your soul's creation is to attain goods after death. If you were to die tonight, would you be confident that you have invested in that goal? If not, what is holding you back from beginning that investment right now?

The First Quranic Proof that the Soul is Not the Body
Razi introduces and presents the first Quranic argument for the soul's independence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first Quranic proof that the soul is not the body, as presented by Fakhr al-Din al-Razi.

Core Idea
The Quran's description of the dead as alive proves the soul's independent existence.

Razi opens this chapter by countering critics who claim the soul-body distinction contradicts revelation. He then presents the first Quranic proof: the Quran shows the blessed and wretched are alive after death. This indicates the soul continues to exist separately from the body.

Guided Path
1
Understand the context
Read the opening where Razi notes that some people with shallow knowledge condemn the view that the soul is not the body as being against the Book and Sunnah. He responds by bringing this chapter to show the Quran and Sunnah are full of proofs.
2
Read the statement of the proof
The first Quranic proof is that the Quran indicates the blessed (e.g., martyrs) and the wretched are alive after death. This implies the soul is not the body and remains after the body dies.
The proof is based on a general statement from the Quran about life after death.
3
Examine the proof for the blessed
Razi quotes Quran 3:169-170 about martyrs being alive and receiving provision. The inference is that necessary knowledge tells us the body is dead, so the life must be of the soul.
The text is cut off here; the explanation for the wretched is not provided.
4
Reflect on the argument's force
Consider why this proof is compelling: the Quran is the primary source, and if it speaks of the dead as alive, then the soul must survive bodily death.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل السابع في الدلائل السمعية على أن النفس غير البدن

Chapter Seven: On the scriptural proofs that the soul is not the body. Know that a group of those whose understanding of real sciences is deficient have taken to condemning the statement that the soul is not the body as a statement contrary to the Book and the Sunnah, so it is necessary to be certain of its falsehood. For this reason, we have brought this chapter in this book as a reminder that the Book and the Sunnah are filled with proofs indicating this sought conclusion. We say: As for the Quranic proofs, they are from several aspects: The first proof: The Quran indicates that the blessed are alive after death, and that the wretched are alive after death. That indicates that the soul is not the body and that it remains after the death of the body. As for the clarification of that regarding the blessed: it is His statement, exalted: 'And do not think that those killed in the way of Allah are dead; rather, they are alive with their Lord, receiving provision, rejoicing in what Allah has given them of His bounty.' And the aspect of the inference: that necessary knowledge is obtained that this body...

Commentary
This passage opens a chapter on scriptural proofs for the soul's distinction from the body. The author begins by noting that some people with shallow knowledge claim that the view that the soul is not the body contradicts revelation. He responds by asserting that the Quran and Sunnah are full of proofs for this. Then he presents the first Quranic proof: the Quran speaks of the blessed as alive after death (martyrs) and the wretched as alive after death (presumably the punishment of the grave). This indicates that the soul survives the body. He quotes Quran 3:169-170 about martyrs. The author then begins to explain the inference: that it is a necessary knowledge that this body… (the text is cut off, but the argument is that the body is dead, so the life must be of the soul).

Quranic Proof for the Soul's Distinction from the Body
Razi presents a Quranic proof for the soul's survival after death.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi uses Quranic verses about martyrs and the punishment of the grave to prove that the soul is distinct from the body and survives after death.

Core Idea
The Quran proves the soul's survival by showing people are alive after bodily death.

Razi begins by addressing those who claim that the doctrine of the soul's distinction from the body contradicts scripture. He insists that the Quran and Sunnah are filled with proofs for it. The first proof he presents is based on the clear Quranic statements that the blessed (martyrs) are alive with their Lord, and the wretched are punished after death. By logical analysis, this forces the conclusion that the human person is not the physical body.

Guided Path
1
Understand the context
Razi introduces the chapter in response to critics who say the soul-body distinction is un-Islamic. He asserts that the Quran and Sunnah are full of proofs for it.
This sets the stage for the scriptural argument.
2
Examine the proof for the blessed
Read the verse about martyrs being alive (3:169-170). Razi argues that the body is necessarily dead after death, but the Quran says the person is alive, so the person cannot be the body.
Uses the second figure of syllogism.
3
Examine the proof for the wretched
Read verses about punishment after death (40:46, 71:25). Punishment requires life and consciousness, so the person is alive after bodily death.
Same logical structure applies.
4
Consider the additional proof from grave reward/punishment
Razi points to Quranic and hadith mentions of reward and punishment in the grave, which again imply the person is alive and distinct from the body.
This reinforces the conclusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل السابع في الدلائل السمعية على أن النفس غير البدن. اعلم أن جماعة من الذين قلت بضاعتهم في العلوم الحقيقية أخذوا يشنعون بأن القول بأن النفس غير البدن قول مخالف للكتاب والسنة فوجب الجزم بإبطاله. فلهذا السبب أوردنا هذا الفصل في هذا الكتاب تنبيهاً على أن الكتاب والسنة مملوءان من الدلائل الدالة على هذا المطلوب. فنقول: أما الدلائل القرآنية فمن وجوه: الحجة الأولى: إن القرآن دل على أن السعداء أحياء بعد الموت، وعلى أن الأشقياء أحياء بعد الموت. وذلك يدل على أن النفس غير البدن وعلى أنها باقية بعد موت البدن. أما بيان ذلك في حق السعداء: فهو قوله تعالى: ( ولا تحسبن الذين قتلوا في سبيل الله أمواتاً بل أحياء عند ربهم يرزقون، فرحين بما أتاهم الله فضله ) ووجه الاستدلال: أن العلم الضروري حاصل بأن هذا البدن بعد موته ميت، ولو جاز الشك في موته لجاز أن يقال إن جميع الجمادات أحياء عاقلة ناطقة، وذلك يوجب القول بالسفسطة وزوال العقل. فثبت بمقتضى بديهة العقل: أن بعد موت البدن يكون الجسد ميتاً. والله تعالى نص على أن ذلك الإنسان حي بعد هذا الموت. وحينئذ نقول: الإنسان ليس ميتاً بعد موت البدن، وأما البدن وجميع أجزائه فإنه ميت بعد موته. ينتج من الشكل الثاني أن الإنسان شيء مغاير للبدن ولجميع أجزائه وأبعاضه. وهذا كلام في غاية القوة. وأما تقرير هذا المعنى في حق الأشقياء: فهو قوله تعالى: ( النار يعرضون عليها غدواً وعشياً ) وقوله: ( أغرقوا فأدخلوا ناراً ) والمراد: أن شرح تعذيب أولئك الكفار والفساق وتعذيبهم لا يتم إلا إذا كانوا أحياء عقلاء يميزون بين العذاب وبين الراحة. فهذا يقتضي كون أولئك الكفار والفساق أحياء بعد موت البدن، وحينئذ يرجع القياس المنعقد من الشكل الثاني. وأيضاً: فالقرآن والأخبار ناطقة بحصول ثواب القبر تارة وبحصول عذاب القبر. وذلك يدل على أن المكلف حي بعد موت هذا البدن، وهذا يدل على القطع بأن المكلف شيء مغاير لهذا البدن.
The seventh chapter on scriptural proofs that the soul is not the body. Know that a group of those whose merchandise in the true sciences is scanty have begun to decry the statement that the soul is not the body as a saying contrary to the Book and the Sunnah, so it is necessary to decisively invalidate it. For this reason we have presented this chapter in this book as a notification that the Book and the Sunnah are filled with proofs indicating this sought matter. So we say: As for the Qur'anic proofs, they are from several aspects: The first proof: The Qur'an indicated that the blessed are alive after death, and that the wretched are alive after death. And that indicates that the soul is not the body and that it remains after the death of the body. As for the clarification of that concerning the blessed: it is His saying, the Exalted: (And do not think those who are killed in the way of Allah are dead; rather they are alive with their Lord, being provided for, rejoicing in what Allah has given them of His bounty). And the aspect of inference is that necessary knowledge is obtained that this body after its death is dead, and if doubt were allowed in its death, it would be permissible to say that all inanimate objects are alive, rational, speaking, and that would necessitate sophistry and the loss of intellect. So it is established by the dictate of the self-evident intellect that after the death of the body, the corpse is dead. And Allah, the Exalted, has textually stated that that human is alive after this death. Then we say: The human is not dead after the death of the body, but the body and all its parts are dead after its death. It results from the second figure that the human is something different from the body and all its parts and limbs. And this speech is of extreme strength. As for the establishment of this meaning concerning the wretched: it is His saying, the Exalted: (The Fire; they are exposed to it morning and evening) and His saying: (They were drowned and then made to enter a Fire). And the intent is that the explanation of the torment of those disbelievers and sinners and their punishment is not complete unless they are alive, rational, distinguishing between torment and comfort. So this necessitates that those disbelievers and sinners are alive after the death of the body, and then the syllogism constructed from the second figure returns. Also, the Qur'an and reports speak of the occurrence of reward of the grave sometimes and punishment of the grave sometimes. And that indicates that the accountable person is alive after the death of this body, and this indicates the certainty that the accountable person is something different from this body.
Source pages p. 130

Commentary
This passage is a scriptural proof from the Quran that the soul is distinct from the body and survives bodily death. The author begins by noting that some people object to this doctrine as contrary to revelation. In response, he argues that the Quran is full of proofs for it. He presents the first proof: the Quran explicitly states that both the blessed (martyrs) and the wretched (disbelievers) are alive after death. For the blessed, he cites verse 3:169-170, arguing that while the body is necessarily dead after death (by necessary knowledge), God declares that the person is alive. Therefore, using the second figure of syllogism, the human is not the body. For the wretched, he cites verses 40:46 and 71:25, which describe ongoing punishment. Punishment requires life and rationality, so they must be alive after bodily death, leading again to the same conclusion. Finally, he adds that the Quran and reports mention reward and punishment in the grave, further proving that the accountable person (al-mukallaf) is something other than the body.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think verses about martyrs being alive are metaphorical.
Correction: Razi argues they are literal because the body is necessarily dead; the only way to be alive is as a soul distinct from the body.

Checkpoint
What fundamental premise does the author rely on to argue that the blessed are not dead after bodily death?
A) That the soul is immortal by nature
B) That the body is necessarily dead after death, while the Quran states the person is alive
C) That martyrs appear alive to their loved ones
D) That the body can be resurrected immediately

Key Takeaway
The Quranic verses about martyrs and punishment in the grave demonstrate that the human soul continues to exist after the death of the body, proving that the soul is distinct from the body.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believed that the soul of a disbeliever is being tormented right now in the grave, would it change how you view your own sins? In what way does the comfort of your life today distance you from the fear of that punishment?
?
The Quran says martyrs are alive and rejoicing. Is your own longing for Paradise so strong that you would be willing to sacrifice your body for it? Or do you cling to this life as if it is all there is?
?
You know with necessary knowledge that your body will die. But do you live as if you are only a body, or as if you are a soul accountable to its Creator? What concrete change would prove that you believe in the soul's survival?
