Two Views on the Nature of Souls: Essence Equality vs. Distinction
Chapter opening presenting two opposing views on soul essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two competing philosophical positions: the view of ancient philosophers that all souls are equal in essence, and Ibn Sina's view that animal souls are physical while human souls are immaterial yet all human souls are equal in essence.

Core Idea
Ancient philosophers equate all souls; Ibn Sina distinguishes animal from human souls but equates all human souls.

This passage opens a chapter by presenting the central debate: are souls all the same in essence, or are there fundamental differences? The author sets up two contrasting views from ancient philosophers and from Ibn Sina before proceeding to his own analysis. Pay attention to how each view explains observed differences among living beings.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage to identify the two views
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice that the author first states the question, then presents the ancient philosophers' view, then Ibn Sina's view.
The passage is clearly structured: first view, then second view.
2
Analyze the ancient philosophers' argument
Ancient philosophers claim all souls (human and animal) are equal in essence. Differences in actions are due to bodily instruments. Note the hypothetical examples: if an animal had a human brain, it would think like a human; if it had a human tongue, it would speak.
This view denies any essential difference between animal and human souls.
3
Examine Ibn Sina's distinction
Ibn Sina separates animal souls (physical powers) from human rational souls (immaterial substances). However, he then claims all human souls are equal in essence.
This is a crucial nuance: Ibn Sina does not equate animal and human souls.
4
Understand Ibn Sina's explanation for human differences
Ibn Sina attributes differences in intelligence, dullness, and morals among humans to bodily temperaments (e.g., choleric temperament leads to joy).
Thus, for Ibn Sina, essential equality does not contradict observable diversity.
5
Reflect on the core disagreement
The key issue is whether differences among living beings stem from essence or from external factors (instruments or temperaments). Ancient philosophers say all differences are instrumental; Ibn Sina says some differences (animal vs. human) are essential, but human differences are temperamental.
This sets the stage for the author's own position in the chapter.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الأول في أن النفوس كل كي متحدة في الحقيقة والمادية أم لا؟ لقد عظم اختلاف الناس في هذا الباب : ذهب جمع عظيم من قدماء الفلاسفة : إلى أن النفوس الإنسانية والحيوانية : متساوية في تمام الماهية ، وأن اختلاف أفعالها وإدراكاتها إنما حصل بسبب اختلاف آلاتها وأدواتها ، فلو كان دماغ سائر الحيوانات مساوياً لدماغ الإنسان ، لكانت نفوس سائر الحيوانات مساوية للنفوس الإنسانية في التعقلات والتفكرات ، ولو كان لسان سائر الحيوانات مساوياً للسان الإنسان لكان مساوياً له في قوة النطق . فهذا مذهب قال به بعض الناس وأما الشيخ الرئيس (أبو علي بن سينا) : فإنه زعم أن نفوس سائر الحيوانات قوى جسمانية ، وليست جواهر مجردة . وأما النفوس الناطقة البشرية فإنها جواهر مجردة في ذواتها . ثم زعم : أنها بأسرها متساوية في تمام الماهية والحقيقة ، ولما شاهدنا اختلاف الناس في الذكاء والبلادة ، والأخلاق الفاضلة والمذمومة . زعم : أن ذلك الاختلاف إنما حصل بسبب اختلاف الأمزجة البدنية . فمن كان صفراوي المزاج ، كان الفرح وحسن الاخلاق غالباً
The First Chapter on whether souls are all [؟] united in reality and materiality or not? Indeed, the disagreement among people in this matter became great. A great multitude of ancient philosophers went to the view that human and animal souls are equal in the entirety of essence, and that the difference in their actions and perceptions only occurs due to the difference of their instruments and tools. For if the brain of all animals were equal to the human brain, then the souls of all animals would be equal to human souls in intellectual reflections and thoughts. And if the tongue of all animals were equal to the human tongue, it would be equal to it in the power of speech. So this is a view held by some people. As for al-Shaykh al-Ra'is (Abu Ali ibn Sina), he claimed that the souls of all animals are physical powers, not immaterial substances. As for the human rational souls, they are immaterial substances in their essences. Then he claimed that all of them are equal in the entirety of essence and reality. And when we observe the difference among people in intelligence and dullness, and in virtuous and blameworthy morals, he claimed that this difference only occurs due to the difference of bodily temperaments. So whoever has a choleric temperament, joy and good morals are predominant.
Source pages p. 141

Commentary
This passage is a chapter opening that presents two opposing views on the nature of souls. The author first states the question: whether souls are all united in reality and materiality or not. He then notes that disagreement on this matter is great. The first view is attributed to a large group of ancient philosophers. They assert that human and animal souls are equal in essence (al-mahiyya), and that any differences in their actions and perceptions are entirely due to differences in their bodily instruments (ālāt) and tools (adawāt). To illustrate, they give hypothetical examples: if an animal's brain equaled the human brain, its soul would also be equal in intellectual capabilities; if an animal's tongue equaled the human tongue, it would have equal speech. Thus, they deny any essential difference between animal and human souls. The second view is that of Ibn Sīnā (Avicenna). He distinguishes animal souls from human rational souls: animal souls are physical powers (quwā jismāniyya) and not immaterial substances (jawāhir mujarrada), whereas human rational souls are immaterial substances in their very essences. However, Ibn Sīnā then claims that all human rational souls are equal in the entirety of essence and reality (tamām al-māhiyya wa al-ḥaqīqa). To account for observed differences among humans in intelligence, dullness, and morals, he attributes these variations solely to differences in bodily temperaments (amzija al-badaniyya). For example, a person with a choleric temperament (ṣafrāwī al-mizāj) tends to predominate in joy and good morals. Thus, the passage sets up the debate for the chapter by contrasting two positions: one that equates all souls (ancient philosophers) and another that distinguishes animal from human souls but equates all human souls (Ibn Sīnā). The author leaves the question open for further discussion.

Key Terms
النفوس
الماهية
الجواهر المجردة
الأمزجة البدنية
صفراوي المزاج
قوى جسمانية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ibn Sina's view is identical to that of the ancient philosophers.
Correction: Ibn Sina crucially distinguishes animal souls as physical powers from human souls as immaterial substances, while ancient philosophers equate all souls. However, both agree that all human souls are equal in essence.

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Sina, what is the cause of differences in intelligence and morals among humans?
A) Differences in the essences of their souls
B) Differences in bodily temperaments
C) Differences in the souls' origins
D) Differences in divine decree

Key Takeaway
The central debate is whether souls differ in essence or only due to bodily instruments, with Ibn Sina uniquely restricting essential equality to human rational souls while distinguishing them from animal souls.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If all human souls are essentially equal, why do you sometimes look down on others for being less intelligent or less moral? Does this lesson challenge you to see your own accomplishments as products of your body's temperament rather than your soul's superiority?
?
Ibn Sina attributes differences in character to bodily temperaments. If your bad habits are due to your temperament, are you excused from striving to improve? What does this mean for your personal accountability before Allah?
?
The ancient philosophers reduced the difference between humans and animals to mere bodily instruments. Are you comfortable with such a view? How does your belief about the soul affect the way you treat animals and appreciate your own humanity?

Quranic Evidence for the Heart as Seat of Understanding
Cites Quranic verses showing revelation and remembrance are sent to the heart.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand three Quranic proofs that locate understanding and feeling in the heart as the primary organ of spiritual cognition.

Core Idea
The Quran explicitly states that revelation and remembrance are sent to the heart.

In this passage, the author presents scriptural evidence from the Quran to demonstrate that the heart—not the brain—is the center of understanding and inner perception. The verses quoted explicitly mention the heart as the recipient of revelation and the locus of remembrance. Let us examine these proofs step by step.

Ayah-Based Proof: The Heart Earns Recompense
Author presents three Quranic verses as the third proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third Quranic proof that accountability is based on the heart's intention.

Core Idea
The heart's intention is what earns reward or punishment.

The author now presents the third scriptural argument: a selection of verses that explicitly tie divine accountability to what the heart 'earns' or 'strives' for. This proof anchors the principle that external actions are secondary to internal resolve.

Guided Path
1
Read the third proof passage
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Note the verses cited and how the author interprets them.
Verses about oaths and sacrifice show the heart's role.
2
Identify the key verses

The first two verses are direct; the third locates piety in the heart.
3
Understand the logic
Why does the author conclude that entitlement to recompense is only based on what is in the heart?
The verses contrast external actions (oaths, meat) with internal states.

The third proof: The verses indicating that entitlement to recompense is only based on what is in the heart of striving. Allah Almighty said: 'Allah does not hold you accountable for what is inadvertent in your oaths, but He holds you accountable for what your hearts have earned.' He said: 'Those are the ones whose hearts Allah has tested for piety.' The fourth proof: His saying (Exalted): 'Indeed the hearing, the sight, and the heart - all of those will be questioned.' And it is known that the hearing and sight have no benefit except in what they convey to the heart, so the questioning about them is in reality a questioning about the heart.
Source pages p. 171

Commentary
This passage presents two additional Quranic proofs that emphasize the centrality of the heart in moral accountability. The third proof cites two verses: one about oaths showing that accountability is for what the heart earns, and another about sacrifice showing that piety (which resides in the heart) is what reaches Allah. A third verse locates piety specifically in the hearts of the believers. The fourth proof uses the verse about hearing, sight, and heart being questioned, arguing that since hearing and sight only benefit by conveying to the heart, the ultimate questioning is about the heart. Thus, the heart is the true locus of responsibility and intention.

Correction: These verses show that Allah looks at what the heart earns and the piety within, not merely external acts.

Key Takeaway
The heart's intention is the basis for divine accountability, as shown by three Quranic verses.

The Heart as the True Subject of Questioning
Proof from Quran with two verses linking sensory actions to heart

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Quranic questioning of hearing, sight, and heart ultimately points to the heart as the center of moral accountability.

Core Idea
The senses are merely channels; the heart bears full responsibility.

This lesson examines the fourth proof from the Quran, in which the author argues that hearing and sight are only meaningful in what they convey to the heart. Therefore, the questioning mentioned in the verse is essentially about the heart. A second verse reinforces this by linking outward 'treachery of the eyes' to inner concealment in the heart.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to absorb its flow, then read the translation. Notice the two Quranic verses cited.
The verses are from Surah al-Isra and Surah Ghafir.
2
Identify the logical structure
The passage first quotes a verse listing three things questioned (hearing, sight, heart). Then it reasons that hearing and sight have no independent benefit—they serve only the heart. So questioning them is really questioning the heart.
This is a simple cause-effect chain.
3
Examine the supporting verse
The second verse says Allah knows the treachery of the eyes and what hearts conceal. The author notes that this treachery originates from what hearts conceal, again pointing to the heart as the source.
4
Reflect on the misconception
The passage guards against thinking that the senses themselves are blameworthy. Instead, they are tools; the real moral weight lies in the heart's intentions and beliefs.
This is a key point in Islamic theology about accountability.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الرابعة : قوله تعالى : ( إن السمع والبصر والفؤاد ، كل أولئك كان عنه مسؤولاً ) ومعلوم : أن السمع والبصر ، لا فائدة فيهما إلا بما يؤديانه إلى القلب ، فكان السؤال عنهما في الحقيقة : سؤالا عن القلب ونظيره قوله تعالى : ( يعلم خائنة الأعين وما تخفي الصدور ) ومعلوم : أن خائنة الأعين لا تكون إلا بما تضمره القلوب .
The fourth proof: His saying most high: “Indeed the hearing, the sight, and the heart – each of those will be questioned.” And it is known that hearing and sight have no benefit except in what they convey to the heart. So the questioning about them is in reality a questioning about the heart. And similar to it is His saying most high: “He knows the treachery of the eyes and what the breasts conceal.” And it is known that the treachery of the eyes is only by what the hearts conceal.
Source pages p. 171

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth proof, citing Surah al-Isra 17:36, which states that hearing, sight, and the heart will all be questioned. The author argues that hearing and sight have no independent benefit; they only serve to convey information to the heart. Therefore, the real subject of questioning is the heart. He reinforces this with another verse, Surah Ghafir 40:19, where Allah knows the 'treachery of the eyes' and what hearts conceal. Since the eyes' treachery originates from inner intentions, the heart is the core of moral responsibility.

Correction: The passage shows that the senses are merely means; the heart is the true subject of accountability.

Fifth Proof: Gratitude for Hearing, Sight, and Heart
Quranic proof for gratitude and heart's centrality

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Quranic verse about hearing, sight, and hearts serves as a proof for the heart's central role in moral responsibility and gratitude.

Core Idea
The senses are messengers; the heart is the judge.

This lesson examines the fifth proof from Al-Matalib al-Aliyah. The author cites a well-known Quranic verse where Allah reminds humanity of the gifts of hearing, sight, and hearts, yet little gratitude is shown. He argues that these faculties are singled out to establish a divine proof and demand gratitude, but their ultimate benefit lies in what they convey to the heart, which then judges and rules. This highlights the heart's pivotal role in knowledge and accountability.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Let the author's rhythm come through.
Notice the verse citation and the two-part structure.
2
Identify the proof structure
The passage has two parts: (1) the verse citation and its implication of proof and gratitude, (2) the explanation of the heart's role. Identify each.
3
Reflect on the heart's function
Ask yourself why the author emphasises that hearing and sight have no benefit without the heart's judgment. Connect this to human responsibility.
This links the physical senses to moral accountability.

Author cites verses linking punishment to three faculties.

This short passage presents the seventh proof from the Qur'an. The author cites two verses that link the sealing or malfunction of the heart, hearing, and sight to punishment. This establishes that these three faculties are crucial for guidance—when they are blocked or misused, divine punishment is inevitable. Let's explore how this proof works.

Read and understand the verses

List them: qalb, sam', basar.
3
Connect to punishment

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة السابعة : قوله تعالى : (ختم الله على قلوبهم ، وعلى سمعهم . وعلى أبصارهم : غشاوة ) فجعل العذاب لازماً لهذه الثلاثة ، ونظيره قوله تعالى : ( لهم قلوب لا يفقهون بها ولهم أعين لا يبصرون بها ولهم آذان لا يسمعون بها )

The Eighth Proof: Faith is Ascribed to the Heart
Author cites four Quranic verses to show faith is ascribed to the heart.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the Quran consistently attributes faith to the heart, establishing the heart as the locus of belief.

Core Idea
Faith in the Quran is anchored in the heart, not just words or deeds.

This lesson examines the eighth proof from 'Al-Matalib al-'Aliyah', which demonstrates that faith is consistently linked to the heart in the Quran. By analyzing four Quranic verses, the author establishes that the heart is the seat of belief. This proof reinforces the importance of inner conviction over mere outward expression.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the structure: a premise, four examples, and a conclusion.
2
Identify the four Quranic citations
Find each of the four Quranic verses the author cites. What common pattern do they share?
All four verses attribute faith (or its absence) to the heart.
3
Understand the conclusion
The author concludes that the locus of cognitions is the heart. Think about why this is significant for theology.
This conclusion supports the idea that faith is primarily an inner reality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثامنة : إنه تعالى كلما ذكر الإيمان في القرآن ، أضافه إلى القلب كقوله تعالى : ( من الذين قالوا : آمنا بأفواههم ، ولم تؤمن قلوبهم ) وقال: إلا من أكره ، وقلبه مطمئن بالإيمان ) وقال : كتب في قلوبهم الإيمان ) (۱) وقال : ( ولما يدخل الإيمان في قلوبكم ) (۲) فثبت : أن محل هذه المعارف : هو القلب
The eighth proof: Indeed, whenever He mentions faith in the Quran, He ascribes it to the heart, as His statement: 'Those who say we believe with their mouths but their hearts have not believed.' And He said: 'Except one who is forced while his heart is at rest in faith.' And He said: 'He wrote faith in their hearts.' And He said: 'And faith has not yet entered your hearts.' So it is established that the locus of these cognitions is the heart.
Source pages p. 172

Commentary
This passage presents the eighth proof. The author argues that whenever the Quran mentions faith (īmān), it attributes it to the heart (qalb). He provides four examples: (1) those who say they believe with their mouths but their hearts have not believed; (2) the exception for one forced to renounce faith while his heart remains at rest; (3) God writing faith in the hearts of believers; (4) faith not yet entering the hearts of some. The conclusion (fa-thabata) is that the locus (maḥall) of these cognitions (maʿārif) is the heart.

Key Terms
القلب
الإيمان
المعارف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Faith is merely verbal profession.
Correction: The Quran shows faith is rooted in the heart, as seen in multiple verses.

Checkpoint
According to the eighth proof, where is faith located?
A) In the tongue
B) In the mind
C) In the heart
D) In actions

Key Takeaway
The heart is the seat of faith according to the Quran.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe in your heart that there is no god but Allah and that Muhammad is His Messenger, or have you only spoken these words without them settling in your heart? What would it take for your conviction to be as firm as those whose hearts are at rest in faith even under coercion?
?
If your faith were tested tomorrow by a threat to your life or property, would your heart remain at rest in faith? How can you prepare your heart today to withstand such a test?
?
The Quran says faith has not yet entered the hearts of some. Could it be that you have knowledge in your mind but it has not reached your heart? What practical step can you take this week to allow the reality of faith to penetrate deeper into your heart?

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Quranic basis for the claim that the heart is the seat of intellect and how the verses demonstrate this.

Core Idea
The heart is not just a pump; the Quran places intellect and spiritual perception in the heart.

In this lesson, we examine the ninth proof from the author, which argues that the intellect resides in the heart. The author cites multiple Quranic verses that directly link reasoning and comprehension to the heart, as well as verses that attribute the opposites of knowledge—like blindness, disease, and sealing—to the heart. This establishes a consistent scriptural foundation for this theological position.

Guided Path
1
Read the claim and first two verses
The author states that the locus of intellect is the heart and provides two verses: one about reasoning with hearts, another about not comprehending with hearts. Notice how these directly connect heart and intellect.
2
Examine the verses ascribing opposites to the heart
The author then lists eight verses where God ascribes diseases, sealing, rust, etc., to the heart. These show that the heart is the seat of knowledge, since its absence (ignorance, disbelief) is described as afflictions of the heart.
Each verse uses a different term but all point to the heart.
3
Reflect on the cumulative evidence
The author uses a 'Quranic induction' approach, gathering many verses that all point to the heart's role. Consider how this strengthens the proof.
This method is common in kalam arguments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة التاسعة : إن محل العقل ، هو القلب لقوله تعالى : ( أفلم يسيروا في الأرض ، فتكون لهم قلوب يعقلون بها ) وقال : ( لهم قلوب لا يفقهون بها ) وأيضاً : فإنه تعالى أضاف أضداد العلم إلى القلب ، فقال : ( في قلوبهم مرض ) وقال : ( ختم الله على قلوبهم ) وقال : ( وقولهم : قلوبنا غلف بل طبع الله عليها بكفرهم ) وقال : ( يحذر المنافقون أن تنزل عليهم سورة ، تنبئهم بما قلوبهم ) وقال : ( يقولون بألسنتهم ما ليس في قلوبهم ) . وقال : ( كلا بل ران على قلوبهم ) وقال : ( أفلا يتدبرون القرآن ؟ أم على قلوب أقفالها ) وقال : ( فإنها لا تعمى الأبصار ، ولكن تعمى القلوب التي في الصدور )
The ninth proof: The locus of the intellect is the heart, as His statement: 'Have they not traveled in the land, so that they have hearts with which they reason?' And He said: 'They have hearts with which they do not comprehend.' Also: Indeed, He, exalted be He, ascribes the opposites of knowledge to the heart, so He said: 'In their hearts is a disease.' And He said: 'Allah has sealed their hearts.' And He said: 'And their saying: Our hearts are wrapped, rather Allah has set a seal upon them for their disbelief.' And He said: 'The hypocrites fear that a sura be revealed informing them of what is in their hearts.' And He said: 'They say with their tongues what is not in their hearts.' And He said: 'No, rather a rust has covered their hearts.' And He said: 'Do they not ponder the Quran, or are there locks on their hearts?' And He said: 'For indeed, it is not the eyes that become blind, but the hearts that are in the breasts become blind.'
Source pages p. 173

Commentary
This passage presents the ninth proof. The author asserts that the locus of the intellect (ʿaql) is the heart, and provides a series of Quranic verses. First, two direct verses: one states that people have hearts with which they reason (yaʿqilūn), and another says they have hearts with which they do not comprehend (lā yafqahūn). Then the author argues that God ascribes the opposites of knowledge—such as disease, sealing, wrapping, rust, and locks—to the heart, implying that the heart is the seat of knowledge and its absence. He lists eight additional verses: disease in hearts, sealing, covering, informing what is in hearts, saying what is not in hearts, rust, locks, and blindness of hearts. Each indicates that spiritual or intellectual states pertain to the heart.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: People often think the intellect resides in the brain, based on modern science.
Correction: The Quran and classical Islamic scholars hold that the heart is the seat of intellect and spiritual understanding.

Checkpoint
According to the ninth proof, what is the locus of the intellect?
A) The brain
B) The soul
C) The heart
D) The mind

Key Takeaway
Through a series of Quranic verses, the author convincingly demonstrates that the heart is the locus of intellect, as both reason and its opposites are consistently attributed to it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Allah says, 'Do they not ponder the Quran, or are there locks on their hearts?' (Q 47:24). What locks might be on your heart when you read the Quran—busyness, distraction, pride? What will you do today to ask Allah to remove one of them?
?
The hypocrites 'say with their tongues what is not in their hearts' (Q 48:11). Where in your daily interactions does your speech not match the conviction of your heart? Will you commit to align them, even if it costs you?
?
The verse warns, 'It is not the eyes that become blind, but the hearts that are in the breasts become blind' (Q 22:46). How would you know if your own heart has grown blind to truth? Name one specific step you will take this week to seek clarity and light for your heart.

The Hadith on the Heart: Seat of Understanding
Author cites three prophetic hadiths to prove the heart is the seat of understanding.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how three prophetic hadiths establish the heart as the locus of rational understanding, knowledge, and faith.

Core Idea
The heart is the absolute rational being and the seat of all spiritual knowledge.

This lesson presents the tenth proof from the prophetic tradition, using three distinct narrations to demonstrate that the heart is the center of understanding and faith. The Prophet explicitly identifies the heart as the lump of flesh whose condition determines the whole body, and he points to the heart as the true measure of belief in the incident of Usama ibn Zayd. His supplication further confirms the heart as the object of divine turning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its English translation to get an overview of the three proofs. Notice how each hadith contributes to the same conclusion.
The passage uses the term 'العاقل المطلق' (absolute rational being) for the heart.
2
Understand the first hadith about the lump of flesh
The Prophet says the whole body's soundness depends on a single lump of flesh—the heart. This shows the heart is the source of rational thought and control.
The author interprets this as an explicit declaration that the heart is the absolute rational being.
3
Analyze the incident of Usama ibn Zayd
When Usama killed a man who uttered the shahada, the Prophet questioned him and suggested that only by splitting open the heart could one know true belief. This proves the heart is the locus of knowledge (maḥall al-maʿrifa).
The heart's state determines the validity of faith, not outward words alone.
4
Reflect on the Prophet's supplication
The Prophet frequently prayed, 'O Turner of hearts, establish my heart upon Your religion.' This shows that faith resides in the heart and that it can be turned by Allah.
The heart is not only the seat of knowledge but also the object of divine guidance.
5
Synthesize the three evidences
Consider how all three hadiths together build a strong proof that the heart is the center of understanding, knowledge, and faith, making it the true locus of human responsibility before Allah.
This proof belongs to the sam'iyyat category, relying on revealed texts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة العاشرة : قوله عليه السلام : ( ألا إن في الجسد مضغة ، إذا صلحت صلح الجسد كله، وإذا فسدت فسد الجسد كله . ألا وهي القلب . بأن العاقل المطلق : هو القلب . وأن سائر الأعضاء تبع له . وروي أن « أسامة بن زيد ، لما قتل الكافر الذي قال : « لا إله إلا الله ، قال له [ النبي ] عليه السلام : « لم قتلته ؟ ، فقال : لأنه قال هذه الكلمة عن خوف . فقال عليه السلام : ( هلا شققت عن قلبه ؟ ، وهذا يدل على أن محل المعرفة : هو القلب . وكان عليه السلام يقول : « يا مقلب القلوب ، ثبت قلبي على دينك )
The tenth proof: His statement, peace be upon him: 'Verily, in the body there is a lump of flesh; if it is sound, the whole body is sound; if it is corrupt, the whole body is corrupt. Verily, it is the heart.' This is an explicit statement that the absolute rational being is the heart, and that all other limbs are subordinate to it. And it is narrated that Usama ibn Zayd, when he killed the disbeliever who said 'There is no god but Allah', the Prophet [peace be upon him] said to him: 'Why did you kill him?' He said: 'Because he said this word out of fear.' So he said, peace be upon him: 'Did you split open his heart?' This indicates that the locus of knowledge is the heart. And he, peace be upon him, used to say: 'O Turner of hearts, establish my heart upon Your religion.'
Source pages p. 173

Commentary
This passage presents the tenth proof from hadith. First, the famous hadith about the lump of flesh (muḍgha) in the body: if it is sound, the whole body is sound; if it is corrupt, the whole body is corrupt; and that is the heart. The author interprets this as an explicit declaration that the absolute rational being (al-ʿāqil al-muṭlaq) is the heart, and all other organs are subordinate (tabaʿ) to it. Second, the incident of Usama ibn Zayd killing a man who uttered the shahada out of fear; the Prophet asked why he killed him and suggested that if he had split open his heart, he would have known his true belief. This demonstrates that the locus of knowledge (maḥall al-maʿrifa) is the heart. Third, the Prophet's supplication 'O Turner of hearts, establish my heart upon Your religion' further shows that the heart is the seat of faith and conviction.

Key Terms
القلب
المضغة
المعرفة
العاقل المطلق
مقلب القلوب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that outward actions or speech alone determine a person's faith or character.
Correction: The Prophet looked beyond outward actions to the heart, as shown when he asked Usama to split open the man's heart to know his true belief.

Checkpoint
According to the hadith of Usama ibn Zayd, what does the Prophet indicate is the seat of knowledge?
A) The tongue
B) The actions
C) The heart
D) The mind

Key Takeaway
The prophetic hadiths unequivocally establish the heart as the seat of rational understanding, knowledge, and faith, making it the core of human moral and spiritual responsibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the Prophet asked Usama to split open the man's heart to know his true belief, how often do you judge others by their outward actions without considering what might be in their hearts? Will you commit to pausing before judging and leaving the heart's assessment to Allah?
?
The Prophet asked Allah to establish his heart upon the religion. When was the last time you genuinely feared that your own heart might be turned away from faith? What are you doing to seek Allah's help in keeping your heart firm?
?
The hadith says if the heart is sound, the whole body is sound. Reflect on your own heart: is it sound? Which actions in your life are not in line with a sound heart? What will you change today to align your limbs with the state of your heart?

The Sixth Proof: Arteries Are Not Nerves
Al-Razi presents the sixth proof and Galen's objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Razi's sixth argument that arteries are not nerves and Galen's objection to that view.

Core Idea
Arteries are distinguished from nerves by their unceasing pulse.

Al-Razi presents a concise argument that arteries cannot be nerves because nerves can be stopped but arteries continuously pulsate. He then introduces Galen's objection, which argues that the branching of arteries from the heart does not turn them into nerves. This lesson examines the logic of both sides.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing; then read the literal translation. Notice the concise style of the argument.
The text contains brackets indicating a possible lacuna – don't worry about that now.
2
Analyze the core proof
Identify the two premises: nerves can be restrained from action, arteries always act (pulse). How does that lead to the conclusion?
3
Examine Galen's objection
Galen argues that the arterial branch descending from the heart divides into small twigs but never becomes nerves. He says the ascending branch should be similar. What assumption is Galen making?
Think about how this objection challenges al-Razi's argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السادس : إن العصب قد يمسك عن فعله كثيراً . والشريانات أفعالها دائمة . وهي النبض . فثبت بهذه الوجوه : أن الشريانات [ ليست من جنس الأعصاب والوجه الثاني في الجواب عن ذلك الكلام : قال و جالينوس ، : إن أصل الشريان (۱) ] المتشعب من القلب ينقسم إلى قسمين : قسم يصعد إلى جانب الرأس ، وقسم ينزل إلى أسفل البدن ، والقسم النازل إلى أسفل البدن ، لا شك أنه ينقسم ويتفرق إلى الشعب الصغـــار الدقاق ، ثم إنها بعد دقتها ، ما صارت أعصاباً ، فوجب أن يكون الحال كذلك في الشريانات الصاعدة إلى أعلى البدن . هذا حاصل كلام ( جالينوس » في إبطال قول ( خروسيس ) على ما ذكره في كتابه المسمى بـ ( آراء بقراط وأفلاطون ، في أوراق كثيرة
The sixth: The nerve may be restrained from its action often. And the arteries, their actions are continuous. And that is the pulse. So it is established by these aspects: that the arteries are not of the kind of nerves. And the second aspect in the answer to that statement: Galen said: The origin of the artery branching from the heart divides into two sections: a section ascending toward the head, and a section descending toward the lower part of the body. And the descending section to the lower body, no doubt it divides and branches into small thin branches, then after their thinness, they do not become nerves. So it is necessary that the case is similar in the ascending arteries to the upper part of the body. This is the gist of Galen's statement in refuting the opinion of Erasistratus, as he mentioned in his book called 'The Opinions of Hippocrates and Plato', in many pages.
Source pages p. 180

Commentary
This passage presents the sixth argument in a series proving that arteries are not nerves. The author (Fakhr al-Din al-Razi) first states the argument: nerves can be stopped from action, but arteries always act (the pulse). Therefore arteries are not a type of nerve. Then he presents Galen's objection, which consists of two answers (though only the second is detailed here). Galen argues that the artery originates from the heart and splits into ascending and descending branches. The descending branch divides into small twigs but never becomes nerves, so the ascending branch should also not become nerves. The author will later refute this.

Key Terms
العصب
الشريانات
النبض
جالينوس
خروسيس
الروح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that the pulse is a conscious action similar to nerve movement.
Correction: Al-Razi points out that nerves can be stopped but arteries cannot, showing they are different types of entities.

Checkpoint
What is the sixth proof al-Razi gives that arteries are not nerves?
A) Nerves can be restrained from action, while arteries always have continuous pulsation.
B) Arteries originate from the heart while nerves originate from the brain.
C) Galen proved that arteries never become nerves.
D) The descending arteries do not become nerves.

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi proves arteries are not nerves by their continuous pulse, and Galen's objection attempts to show arterial branching does not produce nerves.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You rely on your pulse as a sign of life, yet you have no control over it. When was the last time you reflected on your absolute dependence on something Allah created to function without your will? What would change in your daily life if you truly internalized that your heart's beat is a continuous gift, not a personal achievement?
?
Al-Razi distinguished nerves (which can be stopped) from arteries (which cannot). Think of an area in your life where you confuse what is under your control with what is not. Are you trying to 'stop' something that only Allah can stop, or neglecting action where you can choose? How can this distinction reshape your effort and reliance on Allah?
?
Galen argued from physical continuity: because the artery branches, it might become a nerve. Al-Razi argued from essential properties: the pulse is continuous, so it cannot be a nerve. In your own beliefs about yourself, do you focus on surface-level similarities with others or on your essential nature as a servant of Allah? What is one essential quality of yours that should define your actions, regardless of outward circumstances?

Refuting Galen's First Answer: Change in Arteries
Al-Razi refutes Galen's first answer by arguing that arteries change their form after entering the brain, using analogy and a principle of natural parsimony.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Razi refutes Galen's first answer by using the analogy of the brain spirit and the philosophical principle that nature uses few instruments.

Core Idea
What changes in the brain spirit can also change in arteries — nature economizes.

Al-Razi continues his critique of Galen's theory of the brain. Here he takes on Galen's first argument about the attributes of arteries. Instead of denying Galen's observations, al-Razi argues that they are not permanent — arteries change once they reach the brain. He supports this with the analogy of the brain spirit and a philosophical principle about nature's economy.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
First read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how al-Razi structures his refutation: a declaration of weakness, a question, an analogy, and a principle.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Analyze the analogy of the brain spirit
Al-Razi compares the arteries to the brain spirit. The spirit originates in the heart, changes when it reaches the brain. Similarly, arteries may lose their original attributes and take on new forms after entering the brain.
3
Understand the principle of parsimony
The philosophers agreed that nature uses as few instruments as possible. Once arteries deliver blood and spirit to the brain, nature may repurpose them into nerves rather than creating new structures.
This principle strengthens al-Razi's case.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم : أن هذين الجوابين عندي في غاية الضعف . أما الجواب الأول : فبيان ضعفه : أن الصفات التي ذكرها ( جالينوس ) : للشريانات: صفات حاصلة لها من وقت انشعابها عن القلب ، إلى وقت تشعبها إلى الأقسام الدقيقة ، ونفوذها في جوهر الدماغ . فلم قلتم : إن هذه الصفات تبقى في تلك الشظايا الدقيقة بعد هذه الحالة ؟ والدليل عليه : أن الروح الدماغي، لا شك أنه كان متولداً في القلب ، ثم إنه تصاعد من القلب ، وبقي في النسيجة المتولدة تحت الدماغ مدة ، ثم إنه ينفذ في الدماغ، فيحدث له حال كونه في الدماغ ، أحوال وصفات ، غير الأحوال والصفات التي كانت حاصلة ، حين كان في القلب . فإذا جاز ذلك ، فلم لا يجوز أن يقال : إن الحال في الشرايين كذلك ؟ فإن تلك الصفات التي ذكرها ( جالينوس ) كانت حاصلة للشرايين من وقت انشعابها من القلب إلى وقت نفوذها في جرم الدماغ . أما بعد ذلك فقد حدثت لها صورة أخرى ، وخلقة أخرى ، بخلاف الصورة الأولى . وهذا الاحتمال لا يبطل بما ذكره ( جالينوس ، والدليل عليه : أن الفلاسفة اتفقوا على أن الطبيعة ، متى أمكنها الاكتفاء بالآلات القليلة ، لم تعدل عنه إلى إعداد الآلات الكثيرة . فههنا هذه الأجسام المسماة بالشريانات ، صورت بصور موافقة لتحصيل مطلوب معين، وهو إيصال الدم والروح إلى الدماغ ، فلما حصل هذا المقصود ، فحينئذ تركب الطبيعة تلك الأجسام على صورة أخرى ، صالحة لمقصود آخر ، وهي صبورة الأعصاب ، فثبت : أن الكلام الذي ذكره جالينوس لا يمكن جعله طعنا في مذهب ( خروسيس ) .
Know that these two answers are in my view extremely weak. As for the first answer: an exposition of its weakness: The attributes that Galen mentioned for the arteries are attributes that accrue to them from the time of their branching from the heart to the time of their splitting into the fine divisions and their penetration into the substance of the brain. So why do you say that these attributes remain in those fine fragments after this state? The proof of it: The brain spirit, no doubt it was generated in the heart, then it ascended from the heart, and remained in the tissue generated under the brain for a while, then it penetrates the brain, and while it is in the brain, states and attributes occur to it other than the states and attributes that it had when it was in the heart. If that is allowed, then why not allow that the case with the arteries is similar? For those attributes that Galen mentioned were present for the arteries from the time of their branching from the heart to the time of their penetration into the substance of the brain. As for after that, another form and another constitution has occurred for them, different from the first form. This probability is not invalidated by what Galen mentioned. The proof of it: The philosophers agreed that nature, when it can suffice with few instruments, does not turn to preparing many instruments. So here these bodies called arteries were formed in forms suitable for achieving a specific purpose, which is conveying blood and spirit to the brain. When this goal is achieved, then nature compounds those bodies in another form suitable for another purpose, which is the form of nerves. So it is established that the statement Galen mentioned cannot be made a criticism of the doctrine of Erasistratus.
Source pages p. 180

Commentary
This passage is the refutation of Galen's first answer. Al-Razi begins by declaring both answers extremely weak. He then addresses the first answer: Galen listed attributes of arteries from their origin to their entry into the brain. Al-Razi questions why these attributes should persist after the arteries reach the brain. He analogizes to the brain spirit: it originates in the heart, ascends, changes when it reaches the brain. Similarly, arteries may lose their original attributes and gain new ones (form and constitution) after entering the brain. He further supports this by the philosophical principle that nature uses as few instruments as possible: the arteries' function is to deliver blood and spirit to the brain; once that is done, nature may repurpose the same material into nerves. Thus Galen's objection does not refute Erasistratus.

Key Terms
الروح الدماغي
شظايا
الطبيعة
الآلات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Galen's physical description of arteries is definitive and unchanging.
Correction: Al-Razi argues that the attributes of arteries are not permanent; they can change after reaching the brain, just as the brain spirit changes, and nature may repurpose them into nerves.

Checkpoint
What analogy does al-Razi use to argue that arteries can change their form after reaching the brain?
A) The heart generates spirits continuously.
B) The brain spirit changes its attributes when it enters the brain.
C) Nerves are formed from the spinal cord.
D) Galen himself admitted arteries have no pulse.

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi refutes Galen by showing that the attributes of arteries are not permanent and can change, analogously to the brain spirit, and that nature economizes by repurposing arteries into nerves.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When have you assumed that a part of your life—like a habit, a relationship, or a belief—was fixed and permanent, only to later realize it could change? What would it take for you to allow God to repurpose that area, like the arteries turning into nerves, for a new purpose?
?
Al-Razi uses the principle that nature suffices with few instruments. Where in your spiritual life have you added unnecessary complexity—extra rituals, worries, or distractions—when a simpler, more direct path was available? Will you commit to paring down one thing this week to focus on what truly matters?
?
The brain spirit changed upon entering the brain. What internal transformation have you been resisting because it seems too radical or unfamiliar? What would it look like to embrace that change, trusting that it is part of a divine design?

Refutation of Galen's Second Answer on Arterial Transformation
Al-Razi refutes Galen's second answer via spirit differentiation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why al-Razi considers Galen's second answer weak, based on the different fates of ascending and descending cardiac spirits and nature's need for sensory instruments in the brain.

Core Idea
The brain needs nerves for sensation; the legs don’t.

Al-Razi continues his critique of Galen's objections to Erasistratus. In this passage, he refutes Galen's claim that arteries remain unchanged throughout the body. By comparing the cardiac spirit's journey to the brain versus the legs, al-Razi demonstrates that nature's requirement for sensation and movement in the brain justifies arterial transformation there alone.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the full Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice the key terms 'الروح القلبي' (cardiac spirit) and 'قوة الحس والحركة' (power of sensation and movement).
2
Identify the two divisions of the cardiac spirit
The passage divides the cardiac spirit into an ascending portion (to the head) and a descending portion (to the legs). Identify how each is described.
The ascending portion is said to change; the descending does not.
3
Understand the change in the ascending spirit
The ascending spirit changes because it reaches the brain substance and becomes 'brain spirit' carrying sensation and movement. Note that this change is linked to the brain's function.
4
Analyze the reasoning about nature minimizing instruments
Al-Razi invokes a principle: nature seeks to minimize instruments. In the brain, an instrument (nerve) is needed for sensation and movement, so arteries transform. In the legs, no such need exists.
This explains why the descending spirit does not change and why arteries in the legs remain as they are.
5
Connect to the refutation of Galen
Since Galen argued that arteries cannot become nerves because they are uniform, al-Razi's differential explanation shows that Galen overlooked this functional need. Conclude why Galen's answer is weak.
The passage ends by stating Galen's objection is 'extremely weak and fallen'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما جوابه الثاني : فضعيف أيضاً. وذلك لأن الروح القلبي ، كما صعد إلى الرأس في الشرايين الصغيرة ، فقد صعد أيضاً إلى أسفل البدن في الشرايين الصغيرة . ثم إن القسم الصاعد إلى الرأس ، تغير عن حالته بسبب وصوله إلى جرم الدماغ ، وصار روحاً دماغياً حاملاً لقوة الحمص والحركة . وأما القسم النازل من الأرواح القلبية ، لما وصلت إلى الرجلين ، فلم يتغير عن حالته البتة . فلم لا يجوز أن يكون الحال في أجرام الشريانات كذلك ؟ وتمام التقرير فيه : أنا ذكرنا أن الطبيعة تسعى في تقليل الآلة (۱) ففى الدماغ احتاجت الطبيعة إلى إعداد آلة حاملة لقوة الحس والحركة ، فلا يبعد تكوين تلك الآلة من تلك الشظايا الشريانية . ولم توجد هذه الحاجة (٢) في الرجلين . فظهر الفرق ، وثبت بهذا البيان الذي ذكرناه : أن الكلام الذي ذكره جالينوس » في إبطال الاحتمال ، الذي ذكره ( خروسيس ، في غاية الضعف والسقوط .
As for his second answer, it is also weak. That is because the cardiac spirit, just as it ascended to the head in the small arteries, it also ascended to the lower part of the body in the small arteries. Then the portion ascending to the head changed from its state due to its reaching the substance of the brain, and became a brain spirit carrying the power of sensation and movement. As for the descending portion of the cardiac spirits, when they reached the legs, they did not change from their state at all. So why not allow that the case with the bodies of the arteries is similar? The full exposition in it: We mentioned that nature seeks to minimize the instrument. So in the brain, nature needed to prepare an instrument carrying the power of sensation and movement, so it is not unlikely that that instrument is formed from those arterial fragments. And this need is not found in the legs. So the difference appeared, and it is established by this exposition that the statement Galen mentioned in refuting the probability that Erasistratus mentioned is extremely weak and fallen.
Source pages p. 181

Commentary
This passage refutes Galen's second answer. Al-Razi argues that the cardiac spirit changes when it reaches the brain due to the need for sensation and movement, but when it goes to the legs it does not change. Similarly, the arteries themselves may transform into nerves in the brain because nature requires an instrument for sensation and movement there, but in the legs there is no such need. Therefore, Galen's argument that arteries remain unchanged throughout is invalid. Al-Razi concludes that his exposition shows Galen's objections are very weak and refuted.

Correction: Al-Razi explains that the brain's need for a sensory instrument justifies transformation there, while the legs lack this need, so the analogy does not hold.

Al-Razi refutes Galen by showing that arterial transformation into nerves occurs only where nature needs it—in the brain for sensation and movement—and not in the legs.

What specific need will you acknowledge today?
?
The principle that nature minimizes instruments challenges us to examine waste. Where in your spiritual practices have you added unnecessary complexity or rituals that lack sincere need? What would it look like to strip away what is extraneous and focus only on what your soul truly requires to sense and move toward Allah?

The Second Galenic Argument Refuted: Cooling and Balance
Refutation of a second Galenic argument on heart-brain connection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Razi refutes the second Galenic argument for the brain being the source of spirit by using the brain's cooling effect to explain sensation and movement originating from the heart.

Core Idea
The brain cools the heart; it does not create spirit.

Al-Razi presents a second argument from Galen that is stronger than the first, requiring fewer premises. But he refutes it by accepting the physiology of cooling and showing that it actually supports the heart as the source of sensory and motor powers. In this lesson, we see how he turns the opponent's own reasoning against them.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument and response
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note that al-Razi first summarizes the Galenic argument, then gives his reply starting with 'Why is it not permissible to say...'
The word 'انقطع' may indicate a break; treat it as part of the argument's presentation.
2
Identify the key physiological claim
The Galenic argument relies on the idea that the cardiac spirit is extremely hot and needs cooling from the brain. Al-Razi accepts this but reinterprets it.
3
Understand the refutation strategy
Al-Razi argues that when the passage between heart and brain is open, cooling occurs and the heart becomes balanced and ready for sensation and movement. When blocked, cooling fails and the heart loses readiness, proving the heart is the seat of these powers.
This turns Galen's own cooling theory into evidence against his conclusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
من القلب إلى الدماغ. وهذه الحجة أحسن من الحجة الأولى ، ولا نحتاج فيها إلى المقدمات الكثيرة المذكورة في الحجة الأولى ، انقطع والجواب : لم لا يجوز أن يقال : الروح القلبي يكون في غاية الحرارة . فإذا كان بينه وبين الدماغ منفذ مفتوح ، وصل تبريد الدماغ إلى القلب ، فاعتدل واستعد لقبول قوة الحس والحركة . فأما إذا انسد ذلك المسلك عن القلب أثر الدماغ ، فلا جرم لم يبق مستعداً لقبول قوة الحس والحركة ، فبطلت هذه القوة من الجانب الذي يلي القلب ؟
From the heart to the brain. And this second proof is better than the first proof, and we do not need in it the many premises mentioned in the first proof. [It] is cut off. And the response: Why is it not permissible to say: The cardiac spirit is at an extreme heat. So if between it and the brain there is an open passage, the cooling of the brain reaches the heart, so it becomes balanced and prepared to receive the power of sensation and movement. But if that pathway is blocked from the heart, the effect of the brain [does not reach it], so inevitably it does not remain prepared to receive the power of sensation and movement, so this power ceases from the side that is next to the heart?
Source pages p. 184

Commentary
This passage presents a second argument (attributed to Galen) for the brain being the source of sensory and motor spirit, and then refutes it. The argument is summarized as a better one than the first, requiring fewer premises. The refutation (al-jawab) is given: The cardiac spirit is extremely hot. If the passage between heart and brain is open, the brain's cooling effect tempers the heart, enabling it to receive sensory and motor powers. But if that passage is blocked, the heart cannot be cooled and thus loses readiness for those powers. Therefore, the failure of sensation and movement is on the heart's side, not the brain's. The author thus turns Galen's own physiology against him.

Misconception: One might think the brain itself produces the spirit for sensation and movement.
Correction: Al-Razi shows that the brain only provides cooling; the spirit and its powers originate in the heart, which becomes prepared to exercise them when balanced by the brain's cooling.

Key Takeaway
The second Galenic argument fails because its own cooling theory actually supports the heart as the source of sensation and movement.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The second argument seemed better but was still false. Are there 'better arguments' in your own beliefs—clever, plausible reasons that keep you from submitting to the truth? What would it cost you to abandon them and let the heart be balanced by revelation alone?

Galen's Third Argument: Left Ventricle Contains Blood
Galen argues from dissection that the left ventricle has blood, not spirit; author refutes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Galen's anatomical argument that the left ventricle contains blood rather than pure spirit, and the author's refutation that preserves the heart's role as the source of animal spirits.

Core Idea
Blood in the left ventricle does not disprove spirit in the heart.

Galen, the famous physician, used dissection to argue that the left ventricle of the heart contains blood, not spirit, thereby challenging the heart's role as the source of spirit. However, the author skillfully refutes this by pointing out that Galen himself concedes that animal spirits originate in the heart and ascend to the brain. Let's examine the passage to see how the argument and counterargument unfold.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its English translation to get the full flow of Galen's argument and the author's response.
Pay attention to the dissection details.
2
Identify Galen's claim and evidence
Find the core of Galen's argument: the left ventricle, when pricked, yields blood immediately, proving it contains blood, not spirit. Also note his appeal to the brain's empty spaces.
Galen uses empirical observation.
3
Understand the author's refutation
The author responds that even if the left ventricle contains blood, Galen himself agrees that the heart is the source of animal spirits and that the cerebral spirit ascends from the heart.
The refutation does not deny the dissection but reinterprets its significance.
4
Reflect on the theological significance
Consider why this argument matters: it defends the heart's role as the origin of vital spirits against Galen's brain-centered view.
This relates to broader discussions in Islamic theology about the soul and spirit.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثالثة لـ ( جالينوس :: إن الحكماء والأطباء اتفقوا على أن الحامل لقوة الحس والحركة : جسم لطيف نافذ في الأعضاء وهو الروح. وإذا كان كذلك ، فالدماغ أن يكون مبدأ لهذه الروح ، أولى من القلب . وذلك لأنا نجد في الدماغ مواضع خالية ، وتلك المواضع الخالية تصلح لأن تتولد فيها تلك الأرواح ، وأما القلب فليس كذلك ، لأن التجويف الأيمن منه مملوء من الدم وإنما الشبهة في التجويف الأيسر ، فإنه يعتقد أنه مملوء من الروح قال ( جالينوس : : وليس الأمر كذلك فإن القلب إذا كشف عنه وأبرز من غير أن تثقب وتخرق أغشيته ، لم يمت الحيوان بهذا السبب ، بل قد يلبث مدة طويلة ، تلمسه بيدك ، وتنظر إليه بعينك ، وهو مكشوف ، فتعرف كيف أن نبضه في هذه الحالة ، يساوي نبضه قبل أن تكشف عنه ، ولكن بشرط أن يقع هذا التشريح في موضع ، لا يكون هواؤه بارداً ، لئلا يتبرد القلب . فإنه لو برد لصار النبض ضعيفاً بطيئاً متفاوتاً إذا عرفت هذا فنقول : إنا إذا غرزنا إبرة في غشاء هذا التجويف الأيسر ، سال في الحال منه : دم ، ولو كان هذا التجويف مملوءاً من الروح ، لوجب أن لا يسيل منه الدم البتة ، ولو كان هذا التجويف في بعضه روح ، وفي بعضه دم ، لوجب أن تخرج الروح أولاً ، ثم يسيل الدم بعده . وعلى هذا التقدير ، فكان يجب أن لا يسيل الدم في الحال ، ولما سال في الحال. علمنا : أن التجويف الأيسر مملوء من الدم. وأيضاً : الحيوان الذي مات تجد في التجويف الأيسر من تجويفي قلبه : علق الدم وأما الدماغ فإن جرمه مزرد ، فلا يمتنع أن يحصل في تلك الغضون أجزاء الروح فهي تدل والجواب : إن هذه الحجة في غاية الضعف لأنها إن صحت ، على أنه ليس في القلب روح أصلا ، و ( جالينوس : لا ينازع في كون القلب معدناً للأرواح الحيوانية ، ويسلم أن الروح الدماغي هو الذي صعد من القلب إلى الدماغ ، وصار هناك روحاً حاملاً لقوة الحس والحركة .
The third proof by Galen: The wise men and physicians agreed that the carrier of the power of sensation and movement is a subtle body passing through the organs, and it is the spirit. If this is so, then the brain is more appropriate to be the source of this spirit than the heart. That is because we find in the brain empty spaces, and those empty spaces are suitable for the generation of those spirits in them. As for the heart, it is not so, because its right cavity is filled with blood. The doubt is only about the left cavity, for it is believed to be filled with spirit. Galen said: The matter is not so, for if the heart is uncovered and brought out without piercing and tearing its coverings, the animal does not die from this cause; rather, it may remain for a long time, you touch it with your hand and look at it with your eye while it is uncovered, and you know how its pulse in this state equals its pulse before you uncovered it, but on condition that this dissection occurs in a place where its air is not cold, lest the heart become cold. For if it became cold, the pulse would become weak, slow, and irregular. If you know this, we say: If we stick a needle into the covering of this left cavity, blood flows from it immediately. If this cavity were filled with spirit, it would be necessary that no blood flow from it at all. If this cavity had in part spirit and in part blood, it would be necessary that the spirit come out first, then the blood after it. On this assumption, it would be necessary that blood not flow immediately, yet it flows immediately. We know that the left cavity is filled with blood. Also, in the animal that dies, you find in the left cavity of its two heart cavities clotted blood. As for the brain, its substance is grooved, so it is not impossible that parts of the spirit settle in those grooves. So these [points] indicate. And the response: This proof is extremely weak, because even if it establishes that there is no spirit in the heart at all, Galen does not dispute that the heart is a mine for animal spirits, and he concedes that the cerebral spirit is that which ascended from the heart to the brain and became there a spirit carrying the power of sensation and movement.
Source pages p. 184

Commentary
This passage presents Galen's third proof that the brain is the source of spirit, along with the author's refutation. Galen argues from anatomical dissection: The left ventricle of the heart, when pricked, yields blood immediately, proving it contains blood, not spirit. Moreover, only the brain has empty spaces suitable for spirit generation. The author responds by pointing out that even if the left ventricle contains blood rather than pure spirit, it does not disprove the heart's role as the source of animal spirits, because Galen himself acknowledges that the cerebral spirit originates from the heart and ascends to the brain. Thus, the proof is weak.

Correction: Spirit can be mixed with blood or generated elsewhere in the heart; Galen himself concedes the heart is the source of animal spirits.

Galen's Fourth Argument and the Certainty of Proofs
Galen's analogical argument for the brain as seat of reason and the author's rebuttal emphasizing certain proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author dismisses Galen's analogical argument for the brain as seat of reason as merely persuasive rather than demonstrative.

Core Idea
Rhetorical analogies cannot defeat certain proofs.

In this passage, the author presents Galen's final argument: a series of analogies claiming the brain is the noblest place for the intellect. But the author is unimpressed. He clarifies the difference between persuasive rhetoric and demonstrative certainty, reaffirming that his earlier proofs stand firm.

Guided Path
1
Read Galen's argument carefully
Read the Arabic passage to see the three analogies Galen uses: the king in the highest palace, the senses as servants around the head, and the head like the heaven for spiritual beings.
2
Identify the author's response
Notice how the author dismisses these as 'persuasive arguments' (iqna'iyyat) and asserts his own certain proofs (dala'il yaqiniyya) are stronger.
3
Reflect on the distinction
Think about why the author insists on this distinction. He is defending a methodology: only demonstrative proofs are decisive in theological matters.
This is a key methodological principle in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الرابعة لـ ( جالينوس ) : إن العقل أشرف القوى ، فوجب أن يكون مكانه أشرف الأمكنة. وأشرف الأمكنة أعلاها ، فوجب أن يكون مكان العقل هو الدماغ ، وهو بمنزلة الملك العظيم الذي يسكن القصر الأعلى . وأيضاً : الحواس محيطة بالرأس ، كأنها خدم الدماغ ، وواقفة حوله على مراتبها اللائقة بها . وأيضاً محل الرأس من البدن محل السماء من العالم ، فكما أن السماء منزل الروحانيات . فكذلك الدماغ، وجب أن يكون منزل العقل ، الذي هو روحاني هذا البدن . والجواب : إن ما ذكرناه من الدلائل اليقينية ، لا يعارضها هذه الإقناعيات . ثم إنا قد ذكرنا لنصرة ذلك القول وجوهاً إقناعية أقوى من هذا الوجه وهذا آخر الكلام في هذا المطلب .
The fourth proof by Galen: The intellect is the noblest of the faculties, so it is necessary that its place be the noblest of places. The noblest of places is the highest of them, so it is necessary that the place of the intellect be the brain, and it is like the great king who resides in the highest palace. Also, the senses surround the head, as if they are servants of the brain, standing around it in their appropriate ranks. Also, the position of the head in relation to the body is like the position of the heaven in relation to the world; just as the heaven is the abode of spiritual beings, so likewise the brain must be the abode of the intellect, which is the spiritual [faculty] of this body. And the response: What we have mentioned of certain proofs are not opposed by these persuasive arguments. Moreover, we have mentioned in support of that [other] opinion persuasive arguments stronger than this. This is the end of the discussion on this topic.
Source pages p. 185

Commentary
This passage presents Galen's fourth proof that the brain is the seat of reason, based on the nobility and position of the intellect, and analogies with a king in a palace and the head's similarity to heaven. The author dismisses these as merely persuasive (iqna'iyyat) and not demonstrative. He asserts that the certain proofs (dala'il yaqiniyya) already presented are stronger, and he himself can offer equally persuasive arguments for the other side. The passage concludes the discussion on this topic.

Key Terms
عقل
قوى
إقناعيات
دلائل يقينية
روحاني

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Galen's arguments are strong proofs for the brain being the seat of reason.
Correction: The author categorizes them as merely persuasive rhetoric, not certain proofs. He insists that only demonstrative proofs are decisive in such matters.

Checkpoint
Why does the author reject Galen's fourth argument?
A) Because it is based on weak analogies rather than certain proof.
B) Because the brain is not the noblest organ.
C) Because reason resides in the heart.
D) Because the head is not the highest part.

Key Takeaway
Galen's analogies are persuasive but not certain; the author prioritizes demonstrative proofs over rhetorical arguments.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The author says he could produce stronger persuasive arguments for the opposing view. This suggests that rhetorical skill can support any position. In what area of your life are you most vulnerable to being swayed by rhetoric rather than by certain proof? What practical step can you take today to guard against that?

Third Objection: Individuation by Essence Itself
Objection that distinction occurs by essence itself, and author's response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why distinction by essence itself is rejected and why similarity in attributes does not imply identical quiddity.

Core Idea
Sharing attributes does not prove sharing quiddity.

In this objection, the opponent concedes the need for a distinguishing factor but suggests it could be the essence itself. The author dismantles this by challenging the premise that souls are equal in quiddity and by clarifying that different quiddities can share many attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and response
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the structure: the opponent makes two claims, and the author responds to each.
Pay attention to the logical flow of the argument.
2
Identify the opponent's two attempts
First, the opponent suggests distinction by essence itself. Second, he argues that even if souls are different species, each species has at least two individuals. Identify these.
3
Analyze the author's responses
The author denies the premise that souls are equal in quiddity (first response) and denies that similarity in attributes proves identical quiddity (second response). Understand why each response undermines the objection.
The key insight is that different quiddities can share many concomitants.
4
Reflect on the key point
Consider the principle: shared attributes do not guarantee shared essence. This is a crucial distinction in kalam and philosophy.
This connects to the larger issue of individuation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاعتراض الثالث : سلمنا : أنه لا بد من معنى يفيد الامتياز . فلم لا يجوز أن يكون ذلك الامتياز بنفس الماهية ؟

قوله : ( النفوس البشرية متساوية في الماهية ) . قلنا : ليس لكم على صحة هذه المقدمة دليل . ولنا : دلائل كثيرة على إبطالها .

قوله : وهب أن النفوس الناطقة أنواع ، إلا أنه لا أقل من أن يوجد من كل نوع فردان ، وحينئذ يتم البرهان . قلنا : وأي دليل على صحة هذه المقدمة ؟ أقصى ما في الباب أن يقال : إنه لا بد من أن توجد نفس أخرى ، تشبه النفس الأولى في العلوم والأخلاق ، وسائر الصفات. إلا أن هذا لا يدل على التماثل ، لما ثبت : أن الماهيات المختلفة لا يمتنع اشتراكها في اللوازم الكثيرة .
The third objection: We concede that a meaning that provides distinction is necessary. But why is it not permissible that that distinction be by the essence itself?

His statement: 'Human souls are equal in quiddity.' We said: You have no proof for the soundness of this premise. And we have many proofs for its invalidation.

His statement: 'Grant that rational souls are species, yet there is no less than that from each species two individuals exist, and then the proof is complete.' We said: And what proof is there for the soundness of this premise? The utmost that can be said is that there must exist another soul similar to the first soul in sciences, ethics, and all attributes. But this does not indicate likeness (tamathul), because it is established that different quiddities are not prevented from sharing many concomitants.
Source pages p. 193

Commentary
This passage records the third objection and the author's response. The objector concedes that a meaning for distinction is needed, but asks why that distinction could not be by the essence itself. The author responds by attacking the opponent's example: the claim that human souls are equal in quiddity. The author denies this premise, asserting he has proofs against it. The opponent then modifies his argument: even if souls are different species, each species must have at least two individuals, which would allow the argument to proceed. The author replies that similarity in attributes does not prove sameness of quiddity, because different quiddities can share many accidental properties. Thus the objection fails.

Key Terms
الماهية
التماثل
النفوس الناطقة
اللوازم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If two entities share many attributes, they must have the same essence or quiddity.
Correction: Different quiddities can share many properties, so shared attributes do not imply identical quiddity.

Checkpoint
Why does the author reject the claim that two souls from the same species prove equal quiddity?
A) Because souls are not species
B) Because similarity in attributes does not guarantee identical quiddity
C) Because there cannot be two individuals of the same species
D) Because the opponent's premise is self-contradictory

Key Takeaway
Shared attributes do not entail shared quiddity; thus distinction by essence alone remains unproven.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you assume that because people share similar knowledge and ethics, their inner essences are the same? How does this lesson challenge your view of the uniqueness of each soul before Allah? Will you consciously avoid judging another's inner reality solely by outward attributes?
?
The author denies that there is proof for souls being equal in quiddity. If your soul has a distinct essence from others, what does that mean for your personal accountability? Why do you sometimes act as if your essence is interchangeable with others when you alone will stand before Allah?
?
This passage shows that sharing many attributes does not prove sameness of essence. Is there an area in your faith where you have mistaken similarity for identity—for example, assuming your deeds are like a companion's and thus your spiritual state is the same? Commit to examining your own soul's distinct reality today.

Objection Six: Souls Attached to Celestial Bodies
Objection using author's own view of post-death celestial attachment to argue for pre-birth attachment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the sixth objection against the author's position on the pre-existence of souls, which uses the author's own allowance of post-death celestial attachment to argue for pre-birth celestial attachment.

Core Idea
The objector turns the author's own view against him.

This lesson presents the Sixth Objection. The objector has already conceded that the soul is not attached to another earthly body before this one. However, he seizes on the author's own view that after death, imperfect souls can attach to celestial bodies as corporeal instruments. Using this, he argues that if such attachment is possible after death, it should also be possible before birth, providing an alternative explanation for the soul's distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the Arabic and translation to understand the structure of the objection.
Notice the phrase 'هب أنه قبل هذا البدن' (let it be granted before this body).
2
Identify the concession
The objector concedes that the soul is not attached to another body before this one. This removes transmigration as an option.
3
Recognize the author's view
The author himself allows that after death, imperfect souls attach to celestial bodies. The objector will use this against him.
Look for the phrase 'النفوس الناقصة إذا فارقت أبدانها فإنها تتعلق'
4
Understand the argument
The objector argues: if souls can attach to celestial bodies after death, why not before birth? This would explain distinction without a prior earthly body.
This is an argument from parity (qiyas).
5
Reflect on the challenge
This objection directly challenges the author's inference that souls must have pre-existed in a different state.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاعتراض السادس : هب أنه قبل هذا البدن، لا يجوز أن يقال : إن هذه النفس كانت متعلقة ببدن آخر .. لكن لم يلزم من هذا القدر ما ذكرتموه . وبيانه أنكم ذكرتم أنه لا يبعد أن يقال : النفوس الناقصة إذا فارقت أبدانها، فإنها تتعلق بضرب من الأجسام السماوية، وتتخذها جارية مجرى الآلات الجسمانية، فإذا جوزتم هذا، فلم لا يجوز أن يقال أيضاً : إنها كانت قبل الأبدان متعلقة بجزء من أجزاء السموات، أو بجزء من أجزاء كرة الأثير، أو كرة أخرى، وبسبب هذا القدر حصل الامتياز ؟
The Sixth Objection: Let it be granted that before this body it is not permissible to say that this soul was attached to another body. But this amount does not entail what you mentioned. Explanation: You have mentioned that it is not improbable to say that imperfect souls, when they depart their bodies, become attached to a type of celestial bodies and take them as a kind of corporeal instrument. Since you allow this, why is it not also permissible to say that they were before bodies attached to a part of the parts of the heavens, or to a part of the parts of the sphere of the ether, or another sphere, and due to this amount distinction occurred?
Source pages p. 195

Commentary
This passage presents the Sixth Objection. The objector acknowledges that the soul cannot be said to be attached to another earthly body before this one. However, the author's own view allows that after death, imperfect souls can attach to celestial bodies. The objector turns this against him: if souls can attach to celestial bodies after death, why can they not have been attached to such bodies before birth? This would provide an alternative source of distinction without requiring a previous earthly body.

Key Terms
أجسام سماوية (celestial bodies)
كرة الأثير (sphere of ether)
آلات جسمانية (corporeal instruments)
نفوس ناقصة (imperfect souls)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objection is about transmigration (reincarnation) of souls into other earthly bodies.
Correction: The objection specifically uses the author's own view that souls can attach to celestial bodies after death, not earthly bodies. It is not a transmigration argument.

Checkpoint
What do imperfect souls attach to after death according to the author's view as mentioned in the objection?
A) Another earthly body
B) Celestial bodies
C) Nothing
D) Divine essence

Key Takeaway
The Sixth Objection uses the author's own allowance of post-death celestial attachment to argue that souls could have been attached to celestial bodies before birth, challenging the need for a prior earthly body.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The objector says: 'Since you allow this, why is it not also permissible?' Have you ever accepted a premise in your own reasoning that, if applied consistently, would force you to accept a conclusion you reject? What would it take for you to examine your own beliefs for such inconsistencies?
?
If it were possible that your soul had a prior existence attached to a celestial body, how would that change your view of your current life? Would you live differently knowing that your choices might echo beyond this world?
?
The author is challenged by his own view. Are there aspects of your faith or worldview that you hold without considering how they could be turned against you? How can you strengthen your position by anticipating such objections?
