الحجة السابعة في بيان أن النفس لا تموت بموت البدن: هو أن النفس لها قوتان: نظرية: وهي القوة التي باعتبارها تقوى على استفادة العلوم التصورية والتصديقية من عالم المجردات. وقوة عملية: وهي القوة التي باعتبارها تقوى على تدبير هذا البدن، وعلى التصرف فيه. فأما القوة النظرية فهي غير مشروطة البتة بالبدن. وذلك لأن محل التصورات ليس إلا النفس. فإذا حصل في جوهر النفس استعداد لقبول تلك التصورات، فاضت تلك التصورات عن العلل العالية على جوهر النفس. وإذا حصل تصور الموضوع وتصور المحمول كان ذانك التصوران محال يلزم من مجرد حصولهما حصول التصديق. فحينئذ تحصل القضايا البديهية لا محالة. وإذا حصلت القضايا البديهية، واتفق أن حصل فيها قضيتان مشتركتان في الحد الأوسط فحينئذ يكون حصول هاتين القضيتين في العقل، موجباً لحصول العلم بتلك النتيجة. ثم إذا انضافت تلك النتيجة إلى نتيجة أخرى على الشرط المذكور، حصلت نتيجة ثانية. وظاهر أن شيئاً من هذه المراتب والدرجات لا يتوقف على اعتبار حال البدن وجوداً وعدماً. فثبت: أن النفس في قوتها النظرية غنية عن البدن. وأما قوتها العملية فهي غنية أيضاً عن البدن. وذلك لأن البدن محل لنفاذ تلك القدرة، وما يكون محلاً لنفاذ القدرة، لا يكون علة لتلك القدرة. وأيضاً: البدن قابل للتصرف عن النفس فيمتنع أن يكون فاعلاً في النفس، لأن القابل من حيث إنه قابل، لا يكون فاعلاً. فثبت: أن النفس غنية في قوتها النظرية، وفي قوتها العملية عن البدن. أما أنها غنية في ذاتها عن البدن، فلأنا سنقيم الدلالة على أن المؤثر في وجود الجوهر المجرد، يمتنع أن يكون جسماً أو حالاً في جسم. فثبت بهذا البيان
The seventh proof in explaining that the soul does not die with the death of the body: that the soul has two powers: theoretical power, which is the power by virtue of which it is able to acquire conceptual and judgmental sciences from the world of abstract beings; and practical power, which is the power by virtue of which it is able to manage this body and to act in it. As for the theoretical power, it is unconditioned by the body at all. That is because the locus of concepts is nothing but the soul. So when there occurs in the essence of the soul a preparedness to receive those concepts, those concepts emanate from the higher causes upon the essence of the soul. And when the concept of the subject and the concept of the predicate occur, those two concepts are a state from which, by the mere fact of their occurrence, the occurrence of the judgment necessarily follows. Then the self-evident propositions necessarily arise. And when the self-evident propositions arise, and it happens that among them there occur two propositions sharing a middle term, then the occurrence of these two propositions in the mind necessitates the occurrence of knowledge of that conclusion. Then if that conclusion is added to another conclusion under the mentioned condition, a second conclusion arises. And it is apparent that none of these levels and degrees depends on considering the state of the body, whether existing or non-existing. So it is established that the soul is independent of the body in its theoretical power. As for its practical power, it is also independent of the body. That is because the body is a locus for the execution of that power, and what is a locus for the execution of a power is not a cause of that power. And also: the body is receptive to the action of the soul, so it is impossible for it to be an agent in the soul, because the receptive, inasmuch as it is receptive, is not an agent. So it is established that the soul is independent in its theoretical power and in its practical power of the body. As for its being independent in its essence of the body, we will provide the evidence that the effective cause in the existence of the simple substance cannot be a body or a state in a body. So it is established by this explanation.
Source pages p. 222

Commentary
This passage presents the seventh proof for the soul's immortality. It begins by stating that the soul has two powers: the theoretical power (القوة النظرية) by which it acquires conceptual and judgmental sciences from the world of abstract beings, and the practical power (القوة العملية) by which it manages and acts in the body. The author then argues that the theoretical power is unconditioned by the body. The reason is that the locus (محل) of concepts is only the soul. When the soul's essence has a preparedness to receive those concepts, they emanate upon the soul from the higher causes. When the concept of the subject and the concept of the predicate are obtained, these two concepts together necessitate the judgment (تصديق) by their mere presence. Thus self-evident propositions (قضايا بديهية) necessarily arise. From these, if two propositions share a middle term, their presence in the mind causes knowledge of a conclusion. This conclusion can combine with another to produce further knowledge. None of these steps depend on the body's existence. Thus the soul is independent of the body in its theoretical power. As for the practical power, it is also independent. Because the body is a locus for the exercise of that power, but being a locus does not make it a cause of the power. Moreover, the body is receptive to the soul's actions, so it cannot be an agent affecting the soul, since the receptive as such is not an agent. Thus the soul is independent of the body in both powers. Finally, the author states that the soul is independent in its essence, because the cause of the simple substance cannot be a body or a state in a body. This is established from evidence to be presented.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The soul needs the body to think and reason.
Correction: The soul's theoretical power acquires concepts and forms judgments through emanation from higher causes, without any dependence on the body. Even the practical power, which acts upon the body, does not depend on the body for its existence; the body is merely the locus of its action.

Checkpoint
According to the seventh proof, why is the theoretical power independent of the body?
A) Because the body provides concepts to the soul.
B) Because concepts reside only in the soul and are received from higher causes without the body's involvement.
C) Because the body is too weak to affect the soul.
D) Because the practical power is independent, so the theoretical must be also.

Key Takeaway
The soul's theoretical and practical powers are each independent of the body, establishing its survival after death.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You just learned that your soul's theoretical power acquires knowledge directly from a higher realm, not from your brain or senses. How much of your daily mental effort is directed toward that higher source? Are you feeding your soul with concepts from the unseen, or are you letting it starve? What one change can you make today to prioritize that connection?
?
Your practical power manages your body—but who is really in charge? Examine your last 24 hours: how many of your actions were driven by your soul's intention versus your body's cravings? If your soul is independent and will survive, what are you practicing mastery over that will remain?
?
This proof shows that your soul does not need your body to think or act. Yet you often act as if your body's death is the end of you. If you truly believed that your soul is independent right now, would you fear death less? Would you live more boldly for your eternal purpose? Commit to one way you will live differently today because of this truth.

First Proof for the Soul's Survival: Consensus of the Elite
First proof for the soul's survival based on consensus of prophets, saints, and divine philosophers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the consensus of the most virtuous human groups—prophets, saints, and divine philosophers—provides a rational proof for the survival of the soul after death.

Core Idea
The consensus of the most virtuous humans, combined with their ascetic lifestyle, proves the soul survives.

This lesson presents the first proof for the soul’s survival, drawn from the agreement of humanity’s most honored groups: prophets, saints, and divine philosophers. The author argues that their unanimous belief in the soul’s survival, evidenced by their ascetic way of life, logically necessitates that the soul does not perish with the body. We will trace the steps of this argument and see why it is considered compelling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the consensus groups
Read the passage and note the three groups considered the best of humanity: prophets, saints, and divine philosophers. Understand their contrast with natural and mathematical philosophers.
2
Understand their way of life
Observe how these groups turn away from worldly affairs and focus on the afterlife. Their asceticism is key to the proof.
The Quranic verse and examples of Socrates and Plato illustrate this.
3
Trace the logical conclusion
The author argues that if the soul did not survive, the path of these elites would be false, and the path of the wicked would be true—which is absurd. Therefore, the soul must survive.
4
Reflect on the example of Aristotle
Notice how Aristotle’s worldly involvement is considered a flaw by other philosophers, reinforcing the norm of otherworldliness.
5
Grasp the force of the argument
The argument is not mere appeal to authority; it uses the consensus of the most knowledgeable to show that their lifestyle logically requires belief in the soul's survival.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الأولى : إن جمهوراً من العقلاء أطبقوا على أن أفضل أقسام البشر : الأنبياء والأولياء والحكماء الإلهيون . أما الحكماء الطبيعيون والرياضيون فالفائدة في وجودهم : لأجل مصالح الحياة العاجلة . ولما كانت هذه الحياة خسيسة ، كان الخادمون لها غير موصوفين بالشرف . وإذا ثبت هذا فنقول : أجمع الأولياء والأنبياء والحكماء الإلهيون : على أن النفس باقية بعد موت البدن ، وذلك لأن هؤلاء الفرق الثلاثة حرفتهم وطريقتهم : الإعراض عن الدنيا والإقبال على عالم الآخرة ، كما قال تعالى : والباقيات الصالحات خير عند ربك ثواباً ، وخير أملا . وما كانوا يلتفتون إلى أحوال هذه الحياة ، وما كانوا يسعون في إصلاحها ، بل كأنه لا حرفة لهم إلا تزييف هذه الطريقة وتهجينها . ومن أراد [ تحقيق ] ذلك ، فليطالع الكتب المشتملة على شرح أحوال الأنبياء ، في الزهد في الدنيا ، والإقبال على [ عبادة ] الله تعالى . وأما الحكماء . فمن أراد معرفة أحوالهم وسيرهم في هذه الطريقة ، فليطالع الكتب المشتملة على شرح أحوالهم ولا سيما طريقة سقراط و أفلاطون ، وأما أرسطاطاليس ، فلأجل أنه كان وزيراً لـ الإسكندر وأستاذاً له ، كثرت الدنيا عنده ، فجعلوه عيباً عليه . وذلك يدل على إطباق أكابر الحكماء على أن طلب الدنيا : مذموم ، والإقبال على الآخرة هو الطريق المحمود . ولو لم تكن النفس باقية بعد الموت ، لكانت طريقة الأنبياء والحكماء في تهجين حب الدنيا ، وتحسين طلب الآخرة : طريقة باطلة فاسدة ولكانت طريقة الفسقة والكفرة في طلب الدنيا هي الطريقة الحقة . ولما كان ذلك معلوم الفساد بالضرورة ، وجب القطع بأن النفس باقية بعد موت البدن .
The first proof: The majority of rational people have agreed that the best divisions of humanity are the prophets, the saints, and the divine philosophers. As for the natural and mathematical philosophers, the benefit in their existence is for the interests of the immediate life. And since this life is base, those who serve it are not described with honor. And when this is established, we say: The saints, prophets, and divine philosophers have agreed that the soul remains after the death of the body, and that is because these three groups, their profession and their way is: turning away from the world and turning towards the realm of the hereafter, as the Exalted said: 'And the enduring good deeds are better with your Lord in reward and better in hope' (18:46). And they would not pay attention to the affairs of this life, nor would they strive to reform it. Rather, it is as if they have no profession except to disparage this way and to declare it ugly. And whoever wants [to investigate] that, let him consult the books that contain descriptions of the states of the prophets, regarding asceticism in the world and turning towards [worship] of Allah Almighty. As for the philosophers, whoever wants to know their states and their conduct in this way, let him consult the books that contain descriptions of their states, especially the way of Socrates and Plato. As for Aristotle, because he was a minister for Alexander and his teacher, the world was plentiful with him, so they made it a defect against him. And that indicates the agreement of the great philosophers that seeking the world is blameworthy, and turning towards the hereafter is the praiseworthy path. And if the soul were not remaining after death, then the way of the prophets and philosophers in decrying love of the world and improving seeking the hereafter would be a false, corrupt way, and the way of the wicked and disbelievers in seeking the world would be the true way. And since that is known to be corrupt by necessity, it is necessary to be certain that the soul remains after the death of the body.
Source pages p. 226

Commentary
This passage presents the first proof for the survival of the soul after death. It is a rational argument based on the consensus (ijma') of the most honorable groups of humanity: prophets, saints, and divine philosophers. The author begins by stating that these three groups are unanimously considered the best of humanity, as opposed to natural and mathematical philosophers who serve only worldly interests. Since worldly life is base, those who serve it are not truly noble. The proof then asserts that these three elite groups all agreed that the soul survives bodily death, because their entire way of life is characterized by turning away from this world and focusing on the Hereafter. Their asceticism and disregard for worldly affairs would be meaningless if the soul perished with the body. The author supports this by citing the Quranic verse about 'enduring good deeds' and then elaborates on the attitude of the philosophers, particularly Socrates, Plato, and Aristotle. Aristotle is noted as a counterexample: his worldly involvement due to his position with Alexander is considered a flaw by other philosophers, further reinforcing the ideal of otherworldliness. The argument concludes that if the soul were not eternal, the path of prophets and philosophers would be absurd, and the path of the wicked would be correct. Since this is self-evidently false, the soul's survival is necessarily true.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This proof is merely an appeal to authority.
Correction: The proof uses the consensus of the most virtuous and knowledgeable groups to show that their lifestyle logically requires belief in the soul's survival; it is a rational argument, not a blind appeal.

Checkpoint
According to the first proof, which groups are mentioned as having agreed on the survival of the soul?

Key Takeaway
The consensus of the most virtuous humans, combined with their ascetic lifestyle, provides a logical proof that the soul survives after death.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you were to die tonight, would your life's pattern match that of the prophets and saints who turned away from the world? What is one worldly attachment you are unwilling to let go of, and what does that say about your true belief in the soul's survival?
?
The proof argues that the way of the wicked would be correct if the soul did not survive. Are there areas of your life where you live more like the wicked—seeking only this world? What would it take for you to realign your priorities with the hereafter starting today?
?
The elite 'would not pay attention to the affairs of this life, nor would they strive to reform it.' How much of your time and energy is spent on reforming worldly affairs versus preparing for the afterlife? Is your lifestyle a testimony to your belief in the soul's survival?

The Precaution Argument for the Soul's Survival
Second proof for the soul's survival using the principle of precaution (ihtiyat).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the principle of precaution (ihtiyat) is used as a rational argument for believing in the soul's survival after death.

Core Idea
Precaution favors belief in the soul's survival because the stakes are eternal.

This lesson presents the second proof for the soul's survival: the argument from precaution. Unlike a direct demonstration, this argument weighs the consequences of two beliefs. By comparing the outcomes of believing in the soul's survival versus its annihilation, the author shows that reason demands we adopt the safer belief. The passage uses a pragmatic calculus that any rational person can appreciate.

Guided Path
1
Understand the premise: the body's time is negligible
Read the opening lines: the body was non-existent for eternity before birth and will be non-existent after death. This sets up that our worldly life is a tiny sliver between two infinities.
This makes the potential loss of worldly pleasures insignificant compared to eternal outcomes.
2
Consider the two beliefs
The passage contrasts two beliefs: (A) the soul survives death and faces eternal reward or punishment, and (B) the soul perishes completely. Notice how each is described.
3
Evaluate outcomes for each belief
For each belief, consider what happens if it is true and if it is false. The passage spells out four scenarios. Identify the best and worst outcomes for each.
Belief A: best = eternal reward, worst = loss of temporary pleasures. Belief B: best = temporary pleasures, worst = eternal punishment.
4
Apply the principle of precaution
Now compare the two beliefs. Which one has a better worst-case scenario? Which offers the greater potential gain? The principle of precaution (ihtiyat) tells us to choose the safer path.
Clearly, Belief A is safer because even if wrong, the loss is trivial, while if right, the gain is infinite.
5
Conclude that precaution requires this belief
The author concludes that belief in the soul's survival is 'closer to precaution' and therefore necessary to adopt. Understand that this is a practical, not demonstrative, argument.
This argument does not prove the soul exists, but shows it is rationally preferable to believe so.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية : طريقة الاحتياط. فنقول : إن هذا البدن كان معدوماً من الأزل إلى يوم الولادة، وسيبقى معدوماً من يوم الموت إلى الأبد . ومن المعلوم : أن هذا الزمان الواقع فيما بين الأزل والأبد : قليل قليل ، بالنسبة إلى الكثير الكثير . فإن اعتقدنا أن النفس باقية بعد موت البدن ، واعتقدنا أن لها سعادة وشقاوة بعد ذلك ، ثم احترزنا عن اللذات الجسمانية ، وأقبلنا على طلب الخيرات الروحانية ، فإن أصبنا في هذا الاعتقاد ، فقد تخلصنا من العذاب المؤبد ، ووصلنا إلى الثواب المؤبد . وإن أخطأنا في هذا الاعتقاد لم يفتنا إلا الفوز بهذه اللذات البدنية ، في هذه المدة القليلة ، المتوسطة بين الأزل والأبد . وأما إن اعتقدنا : أن النفس فانية وأنه ليس لها سعادة ولا شقاوة بعد الموت. فإن أصبنا في هذا الاعتقاد ، وصلنا إلى هذه اللذات القليلة المنغصة في هذه الأيام القليلة. وإن أخطأنا في هذا الاعتقاد ، وصلنا إلى العذاب المؤبد ، والشقاء المخلد . وإذا عرفت هذا ، فقد ظهر أن اعتقاد أن النفس باقية بعد موت البدن ، أقرب إلى الاحتياط ، فوجب المصير إليه .
The second proof: The method of precaution. We say: This body was non-existent from eternity until the day of birth, and it will remain non-existent from the day of death to eternity. And it is known that this time which lies between eternity and eternity is very, very little compared to the very, very much. So if we believe that the soul remains after the death of the body, and we believe that it has happiness and misery after that, then we abstain from bodily pleasures and turn to seeking spiritual goods, then if we are correct in this belief, we have been saved from eternal punishment and reached eternal reward. And if we err in this belief, we only miss out on attaining these bodily pleasures in this short period between eternity and eternity. But if we believe that the soul perishes and that it has no happiness nor misery after death, then if we are correct in this belief, we attain these few, bitter pleasures in these few days. And if we err in this belief, we reach eternal punishment and everlasting misery. And when you know this, it becomes clear that the belief that the soul remains after the death of the body is closer to precaution, so it is necessary to adopt it.
Source pages p. 227

Commentary
This passage presents the second proof, which is based on the principle of precaution or pragmatism (طرsigue الاحتياط). The author sets up a comparison between two possible beliefs regarding the soul: that it survives death and is subject to eternal reward or punishment, or that it perishes with the body. The argument begins by noting that the body is non-existent for an infinite time before birth and after death. The brief lifespan between these two eternities is negligible compared to the infinite expanse. Then the author evaluates the outcomes: If one believes in the soul's survival and lives accordingly (abstaining from bodily pleasures and pursuing spiritual goods), then if correct, one gains eternal salvation and reward. If mistaken, the only loss is missing out on the limited bodily pleasures of this short life. Conversely, if one believes the soul perishes and lives solely for worldly pleasures, then if correct, one only attains fleeting and tainted pleasures. If mistaken, one faces eternal torment. By simple comparison, the first belief is clearly the safer option (أقرب إلى الاحتياط). Therefore, reason dictates that one must adopt the belief in the soul's survival.

Key Terms
الاحتياط
النفس
البدن
الأزل
الأبد
السعادة
الشقاوة
اللذات الجسمانية
الخيرات الروحانية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This passage proves the soul necessarily survives death.
Correction: It is a prudential argument showing belief in survival is the safer option, not a demonstration of necessity.

Checkpoint
According to the precaution argument, what is the best outcome if one believes in the soul's survival and it turns out to be true?
A) One enjoys worldly pleasures
B) One is saved from eternal punishment and attains eternal reward
C) One loses nothing
D) One becomes a philosopher

Key Takeaway
The precaution argument shows that reason and self-interest favor believing in the soul's survival because the potential loss is infinite while the gain is eternal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believed that after death there is nothing, would you live today exactly as you do now? What would you change, and why haven't you changed it already?
?
The argument says you have a choice: believe in survival or annihilation. Have you ever honestly weighed what you stand to lose if you are wrong? What would it cost you to align your life with the safer belief starting this moment?
?
Look at your last week. Can you see evidence that you are living as if your soul will meet its Creator, or as if it will simply vanish? What is one action you can take today that would be different if you really believed in eternal accountability?

The Precautionary Argument for the Soul's Survival
The author presents the second proof for the soul's survival using a precautionary argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precautionary argument for the soul's survival after death and why it rationally necessitates belief in an afterlife.

Core Idea
Believing in the afterlife has infinite upside; disbelieving has infinite downside.

Imam al-Razi presents a second proof for the soul's survival based on asymmetric risk. He compares the possible outcomes of two beliefs: that the soul survives or that it perishes. The argument shows that the rational choice is to believe in the afterlife, as the potential loss from disbelief far outweighs any temporary gain.

Guided Path
1
Understand the premise
The body exists only briefly between two eternities of non-existence: before birth and after death. This sets the stage for comparing outcomes.
Note that the 'eternity' here refers to the temporal span before creation and after dissolution.
2
Compare the two beliefs
Consider belief (A) that the soul survives and there is reward/punishment, and belief (B) that the soul perishes. Then examine the outcomes if each belief turns out true or false.
Focus on the asymmetry: belief in survival yields minimal loss if wrong, but infinite gain if right; disbelief yields minimal gain if right but infinite loss if wrong.
3
Draw the conclusion
The rational choice is to adopt belief (A) because it is the precautionary option—it minimizes potential loss. The author supports this with a quote from Imam Ali.
This is not a probabilistic gamble but a logical necessity given the stakes.

الحجة الثانية : طريقة الاحتياط. فنقول : إن هذا البدن كان معدوماً من الأزل إلى يوم الولادة، وسيبقى معدوماً من يوم الموت إلى الأبد . ومن المعلوم : أن هذا الزمان الواقع فيما بين الأزل والأبد : قليل قليل ، بالنسبة إلى الكثير الكثير . فإن اعتقدنا أن النفس باقية بعد موت البدن ، واعتقدنا أن لها سعادة وشقاوة بعد ذلك ، ثم احترزنا عن اللذات الجسمانية ، وأقبلنا على طلب الخيرات الروحانية ، فإن أصبنا في هذا الاعتقاد ، فقد تخلصنا من العذاب المؤبد ، ووصلنا إلى الثواب المؤبد . وإن أخطأنا في هذا الاعتقاد لم يفتنا إلا الفوز بهذه اللذات البدنية ، في هذه المدة القليلة ، المتوسطة بين الأزل والأبد وأما إن اعتقدنا : أن النفس فانية وأنه ليس لها سعادة ولا شقاوة بعد الموت. فإن أصبنا في هذا الاعتقاد ، وصلنا إلى هذه اللذات القليلة المنغصة في هذه الأيام القليلة. وإن أخطأنا في هذا الاعتقاد ، وصلنا إلى العذاب المؤبد ، والشقاء المخلد
·
وإذا عرفت هذا ، فقد ظهر أن اعتقاد أن النفس باقية بعد موت البدن ، أقرب إلى الاحتياط ، فوجب المصير إليه
وهذا هو الذي قاله الإمام الأجل و علي بن أبي طالب : - رضي الله عنه وأرضاه :
قال المنجم والطبيب كلاهما لا تحشر الأموات . قلت : إليكما إن صح قولكما فلست بخاصر او صح قولي ، فالخسار عليكم
The second proof: The method of precaution. We say: This body was non-existent from eternity until the day of birth, and will remain non-existent from the day of death to eternity. And it is known that this time which occurs between eternity and eternity is very little, extremely little compared to the very much. So if we believe that the soul remains after the death of the body, and we believe that it has happiness and misery after that, and then we abstain from bodily pleasures and turn to the pursuit of spiritual goods, then if we are correct in this belief, we have escaped from eternal punishment and reached eternal reward. And if we err in this belief, we have only missed out on the attainment of these bodily pleasures in this short period between eternity and eternity. But if we believe that the soul is perishing and that it has no happiness or misery after death, then if we are correct in this belief, we attain these few, tainted pleasures in these few days. And if we err in this belief, we reach eternal punishment and perpetual misery. And when you understand this, it becomes clear that the belief that the soul remains after the death of the body is closer to precaution, so it is necessary to adopt it. And this is what the supreme Imam Ali ibn Abi Talib - may Allah be pleased with him and grant him satisfaction - said: The astrologer and the physician both said: The dead will not be resurrected. I said to them: If your statement is correct, I am not a loser; if my statement is correct, the loss is upon you.
Source pages p. 227

Commentary
This passage presents the second proof for the survival of the soul after death, which is an argument from precaution (طريقة الاحتياط). The author begins by stating the proof. He then explains the reasoning: The body exists for only a short time between two eternal periods of non-existence (from pre-eternity to birth and from death to eternity). Given this, we consider two possible beliefs. First, if we believe the soul survives and that there is reward and punishment, and we accordingly abstain from bodily pleasures and seek spiritual goods, then if our belief is true, we escape eternal punishment and gain eternal reward. If our belief is false, we only lose the temporary bodily pleasures of this brief life. Second, if we believe the soul perishes and there is no afterlife, then if we are correct we gain these few tainted pleasures, but if we are wrong we suffer eternal punishment. The author concludes that the first belief is clearly the more cautious and rational choice, so it must be adopted. He then supports this with a poetic statement from Imam Ali, which summarizes the same logic: the astrologer and physician deny resurrection, but the Imam points out that if they are wrong, they lose everything, while if he is wrong, he loses nothing.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The precautionary argument is merely a probabilistic gamble without logical force.
Correction: The argument is a logical necessity because the stakes are infinite and the two options are mutually exclusive; it is not a gamble but a rational choice under uncertainty.

Checkpoint
What is the core reasoning of the precautionary argument presented in this passage?
A) It relies on empirical evidence from the physical world.
B) It compares the risks and benefits of two beliefs about the afterlife.
C) It proves the soul is immaterial through philosophical deduction.
D) It uses scriptural authority from Imam Ali.

Key Takeaway
The precautionary argument shows that belief in the afterlife is the rational choice because the potential loss of disbelief outweighs any temporary gain.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believe that the afterlife is eternal and that your belief or disbelief has infinite consequences, why do you still find yourself prioritizing comfort and pleasure over spiritual growth? What does that reveal about the depth of your conviction?
?
The precautionary argument shows that belief is the rational choice even if you are uncertain. Yet many people act as if this life is all that matters. Where in your own life do you live as though the afterlife is not real, and what would it cost you to change that today?
?
Imam Ali's statement places the loss on those who deny. Imagine standing before Allah tomorrow and your belief is tested. Based on your current actions, would you be among the winners or the losers? What one change can you commit to this week to move toward the winning side?

The Third Proof: Universal Human Practice for the Soul's Survival
The author presents a third proof based on universal human practice of invoking the dead.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the persuasive argument from universal human practice that the soul survives after death.

Core Idea
The universal practice of invoking the dead proves their continued existence.

This lesson presents a third proof for the soul's survival, based not on philosophical deduction but on a universal human phenomenon. The author argues that the practice of praying for or cursing the dead, found across all cultures and times, implicitly affirms the soul's ongoing existence. This is a persuasive (إقناعي) argument that appeals to common sense and consensus.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its literal English translation. Notice the key terms and the structure of the argument.
Pay attention to the phrase 'أهل المشرق والمغرب' indicating universality.
2
Identify the two types of practices
The passage mentions two opposite ways people treat their dead: with prayer and mercy for the good, and with curses for the unjust. Understand that both imply the dead are capable of receiving benefit or harm.
The contrast shows the practice is not limited to positive or negative contexts.
3
Understand the implied premise
The author argues that these practices only make sense if the soul continues after death. Prayer and curses are requests to God to convey something to the deceased, which requires the deceased to still exist.
If the soul perishes, such acts would be meaningless.
4
Consider the conclusion
Denying this argument would imply that all humanity throughout history has agreed on falsehood, which is absurd. Therefore, the soul must survive.
This is a reductio ad absurdum argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثالثة من الوجوه الإقناعية في بقاء النفس بعد موت البدن : إنا نرى أهل المشرق والمغرب من الزمان الأقدم، إلى الآن : مطبقين على ذكر موتاهم . تارة بالصلاة والرحمة والخير، إن اعتقدوا فيهم الظلم والجور . ولو لم تكن النفس باقية بعد موت البدن ، لكان هذا الكلام باطلا . لأن الصلاة والدعاء والرحمة تضرع إلى إله العالم في إيصال خير إليه ، واللعن والشتم طلب من إله العالم إيصال شر وعذاب إليه . ولو كانت النفس تموت بموت البدن وتعدم عند عدمه، لكان إيصال الخير والشر إليه محالاً . فيلزم أن يقال : إن جميع أهل العالم من الزمان الأقدم، قد أطبقوا على الباطل [ ومعلوم : أن ذلك بعيد )
The third proof from persuasive aspects for the survival of the soul after the death of the body: We see the people of the East and West from the most ancient time until now are unanimous in mentioning their dead. Sometimes with prayer, mercy, and good, if they believe in them injustice and tyranny. And if the soul were not remaining after the death of the body, this speech would be invalid. Because prayer, supplication, and mercy are a humble petition to the deity of the world to convey good to him, and cursing and insulting is a request to the deity of the world to convey evil and punishment to him. And if the soul died with the death of the body and became non-existent with its non-existence, conveying good and evil to it would be impossible. Therefore, it must be said that all the people of the world from the most ancient time have agreed upon falsehood. And it is known that that is far-fetched.
Source pages p. 228

Commentary
This passage presents the third proof, which is a persuasive (إقناعي) argument for the soul's survival. The author observes a universal phenomenon: from the earliest times to the present, people across the world—East and West—are unanimous in mentioning their dead. They either pray for them, show mercy, and speak well of them, or they curse and insult them. This practice implies that people believe the dead still exist in some form. If the soul perished with the body, then such speech—prayer, supplication, mercy, curses—would be meaningless because it would be addressed to a non-existent being. The act of praying to God to convey good or evil to the deceased presupposes that the deceased is capable of receiving it. Since this practice is universal, rejecting it would mean claiming that all humanity throughout history has agreed upon falsehood, which is an absurd conclusion. Therefore, the soul must survive death.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This argument only applies to religious people or specific cultures.
Correction: The author asserts it is a universal human practice, regardless of religion or culture, from ancient times to the present.

Checkpoint

C) The physical continuity of the body after death.
D) The argument from design in nature.

Key Takeaway
The universal human practice of invoking the dead demonstrates an innate belief in the soul's survival, providing a persuasive argument for its immortality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you visit the graves of your loved ones or make du'a for them, do you truly believe they are present and can hear you? What would change in your heart if you fully internalized that your prayer reaches them because their soul survives? Commit to making one extra du'a for the deceased today with full conviction.
?
Have you ever cursed someone who wronged you, even after their death? That desire for justice—or revenge—reveals an innate belief that they still exist. Are you willing to leave judgment to Allah? Think of one person you've held a grudge against and consciously pray for their forgiveness instead.
?
The author says that all of humanity has always agreed on the soul's survival, and to deny it is to call them all foolish. Do you trust this universal human instinct? What fear or doubt prevents you from fully embracing the reality of your own soul's eternity? Write down one step you will take this week to live with that certainty.

Refutation of the Objection: Only Religious People Pray for the Dead
The author refutes the objection that only religious people pray for the dead by appealing to innate human nature.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author argues that praying for or cursing the dead is rooted in innate human nature, not merely in religious belief.

Core Idea
Prayer for the dead springs from innate human nature, not from religious ignorance.

The author has argued that prayer for the dead supports the survival of the soul. Now he anticipates an objection: only religious people engage in such behavior, not rational philosophers. In this lesson, we see how he turns the tables, showing that even the philosopher's innate nature would compel him to pray or curse when remembering past benefits or harms from the deceased.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Understand the claim that only religious people pray for the dead due to ignorance of realities. The objector says the wise philosopher never does this.
The objection is a common challenge to the argument from universal practice.
2
Identify the author's counter
Notice how the author redefines the 'wise philosopher' by setting aside his doubts and returning to his innate disposition (fitrah).
The author does not deny that philosophers exist but argues that their natural state overrides acquired views.
3
Connect to fitrah
See how the author argues that when the philosopher remembers past good or evil from the deceased, his innate nature inevitably impels him to pray or curse.
This shows the reaction is universal, not uniquely religious.
4
Consider the incomplete conclusion
The passage cuts off at 'الإقرار', but the direction is clear: innate human nature affirms something about the deceased, likely the continuation of their existence.
The missing conclusion would complete the argument for the soul's survival.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا : إن هذا الدعاء وهذا الشتم ، إنما يذكره أرباب الملل والنحل لجهلهم بحقائق الأشياء ، وأما الحكيم العاقل الكامل فإنه لا يفعله البتة . فتقول : إن ذلك الذي نسميه بالحكيم الفاضل الكامل إذا غفل عن شبهاته وضلالاته ، وعاد إلى أصل فطرته ، ومقتضى طبيعته الأصلية ، فإن كان قد ناله خير كثير من إنسان ، ومات ذلك الإنسان ، فإنه إذا تذكر ما وصل إليه من الخيرات، فلا بد وأن تحمله فطرته الأصلية على أن يذكره بالرحمة والدعاء. وإن كان قد ناله منه شر كثير ، فإذا تذكر تلك الأحوال ، فلا بد وأن يذكره باللعن والشتم . وذلك يدل على أن مقتضى الفطرة الأصلية هو الإقرار

And if he had received much evil from him, then when he remembers those circumstances, he inevitably mentions him with curse and insult. And that indicates that the requirement of the original innate disposition is the affirmation...

Commentary
This passage contains an objection to the previous proof and the beginning of its answer. The objection claims that only religious people (أرباب الملل والنحل) pray for or curse the dead, because they are ignorant of the true nature of things. The wise, rational, perfect philosopher (الحكيم العاقل الكامل) would never do such a thing. The author then responds by arguing that even the so-called wise philosopher, if he sets aside his acquired doubts and errors and returns to his original innate disposition (فطرة), will naturally feel inclined to pray for someone who did him good and curse someone who did him evil. This shows that the original human nature (الفطرة الأصلية) itself affirms something—presumably the survival of the soul—though the text cuts off before completing the conclusion. The argument is that such natural reactions are universal and not merely a product of religious indoctrination.

Correction: The author shows that even a philosopher, when returning to his innate nature, will naturally pray for or curse the dead based on past experiences, proving it is a universal human response.

The Fourth Proof: Dreams and Reports of the Dead
Fourth argument for survival of soul based on dreams and posthumous communications.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how anecdotal evidence from dreams and posthumous communications is used to argue for the persistence of the soul after death.

Core Idea
Dreams of the dead point to a soul that lives on.

This lesson presents the fourth proof for the survival of the soul. The author gathers stories from personal experience, poets, and philosophers to show that deceased individuals can interact with the living in dreams. Although not conclusive, these reports provide a strong presumption (ẓann ghālib) that the soul remains after bodily death.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to understand the flow of the argument.
Pay attention to the narrative style; the author is sharing personal and historical anecdotes.
2
Identify the three key anecdotes
Spot the three stories: the author's childhood dream, Ferdowsi's dream of Rostam, and Aristotle's disciples at his grave.
Each anecdote involves a deceased person providing useful information or assistance.
3
Examine the logical sub-argument
Within the first dream, the author's father gives a logical reasoning about motion. Notice how this is presented as an example of superior proof.
This sub-argument is about events with no beginning, not directly about soul survival, but it shows knowledge gained in dreams.
4
Analyze the conclusion
The author concludes that such experiences yield a strong presumption (ẓann ghālib) that souls persist. Ask yourself why this is not considered certainty.
The author acknowledges the limitations of anecdotal evidence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الرابعة : إن الإنسان قد يرى أباه وأمه في المنام ، ويسألهما عن أشياء ، وهما يذكران أجوبة صحيحة ، وربما أرشداه إلى دفين دفنه في موضع لا يعلمه أحد . وأقول (1) : ( إني حين كنت صبياً في أول التعلم ، وكنت أقرأ مسألة حوادث لا أول لها . فرأيت في المنام أبي ، فقال لي : أجود الدلائل : أن يقال : الحركة انتقال من حالة إلى حالة أخرى. فهي تقتضي بحسب ماهيتها : كونها مسبوقة بالغير . والأول ينافي كونه مسبوقاً بالغير . فوجب أن يكون الجمع بينهما محالاً . وأقول : « والظاهر : أن هذا الوجه، أحسن من كل ما قيل في هذه المسألة ) . وأيضاً : سمعت أن : الفردوسي ، الشاعر ، لما صنف كتابه المسمى بـ و شاهنامه : على اسم السلطان ( محمود بن سبكتكين ، وأنه ما قضى حقه كما يجب وما راعاه كما يليق بذلك الكتاب ، ضاق قلب و الفردوسي ، فرأى في المنام رستم ، فقال له : إنك قد مدحتني في هذا الكتاب كثيراً ، وأنا في زمرة الأموات، فلا أقدر على قضاء حقك. ولكن اذهب إلى الموضع الفلاني، واحفر فيه ، فإنك تجد فيه دفيناً ، فخذه . فكان ( الفردوسي و الشاعر يقول : إن رستم ، بعد موته أكثر كرماً من و محمود ، حال حياته . وأيضاً : سمعت أن أصحاب ( أرسطاطاليس ، كل ما أشكل عليهم بحث موضوع ، ذهبوا إلى قبره ، وبحثوا في تلك المسألة فكانت المسألة والإشكال يزول . وهذه الأحوال مما تفيد ظناً غالباً أن نفوس الأموات باقية بعد موت أبدانها .
The fourth proof: A person may see his father and mother in a dream and ask them about things, and they give correct answers, and perhaps they guide him to a treasure buried in a place no one knows. And I say (1): When I was a boy at the beginning of learning, and I was reading the issue of events with no beginning, I saw my father in a dream, and he said to me: The best proofs is to say: Motion is a transition from one state to another. By its essence, it requires being preceded by another. But the first (state) precludes being preceded by another. So it is necessary that combining them is impossible. And I say: It is apparent that this aspect is better than everything said on this issue. Also: I heard that Ferdowsi, the poet, when he composed his book called Shahnameh, dedicating it to Sultan Mahmud ibn Sebuktekin, and he did not fulfill his right as required nor regard it as befitting that book, Ferdowsi's heart became constricted, and he saw in a dream Rostam, who said to him: You have praised me much in this book, and I am among the dead, so I cannot fulfill your right. But go to such-and-such place, dig there, for you will find a treasure; take it. And Ferdowsi, the poet, used to say: Rostam, after his death, is more generous than Mahmud during his life. Also: I heard that the disciples of Aristotle, whenever a research topic was problematic for them, would go to his grave and research that issue, and the issue and the difficulty would disappear. These states provide a strong presumption that the souls of the dead persist after the death of their bodies.
Source pages p. 229

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth proof for the persistence of the soul after death. It is a collection of anecdotal empirical experiences: dreams of deceased parents providing correct information, the author's own childhood dream in which his father gave a logical argument about motion, the story of Ferdowsi receiving guidance from the deceased Rostam in a dream, and the practice of Aristotle's disciples seeking help at his grave. The author treats these as inductive evidence, concluding that they give a strong presumption (ẓann ghālib) that souls remain after death. The logical sub-proof within the dream—that motion requires being preceded by something else, and the first state cannot be preceded—is presented as an argument about events without beginning, but its connection to the soul's survival is not fully explained here; it is cited as part of the dream testimony.

الرؤى
المنام
الحوادث
الحركة
الظن الغالب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Dreams are entirely unreliable and cannot provide any evidence for the soul's survival.
Correction: The author acknowledges that dreams yield only a strong presumption (ẓann ghālib), not certainty, but argues that the consistency and utility of such reports give them evidential weight.

Checkpoint
What is the main conclusion of the fourth proof?
A) That the soul is non-material
B) That the souls of the dead persist after death, supported by dream experiences
C) That the soul is reincarnated
D) That dreams are always true

Key Takeaway
The fourth proof uses stories of dreams and posthumous communications to argue that the soul persists after death, though only as a strong presumption.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Did you attribute it to coincidence, or did you consider it a glimpse into a reality beyond this world? If you dismissed it, what would it take for you to treat such experiences as potential communications from the unseen?
?

?
The author’s father appeared in a dream to give him a logical argument. Have you ever received insight or clarity in a way that felt beyond your own intellect? How do you actively cultivate openness to such guidance, or do you ignore it? Will you begin to pay more attention to your dreams and seek Allah’s guidance through them?

The Fifth Proof: Induction from Empirical Observation
Fifth argument using inductive observation of lasting goodness to prove soul's continued existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Razi uses inductive observation of longevity to argue for the soul's survival after death.

Core Idea
Things connected to God last; things connected to matter perish.

In this passage, al-Razi presents his fifth proof for the soul's persistence. The argument is not a deductive syllogism but an inductive generalization drawn from striking empirical observations: religious buildings, the clothes of pious people, and even the reputation of scholars all far outlast their worldly counterparts. By tracing this pattern back to their connection with the Divine, al-Razi builds a powerful case for the soul's own permanence when it is illumined by knowledge of God.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice the examples and the inductive reasoning pattern.
The passage ends mid-sentence; don't worry about the incompleteness.
2
Identify the inductive pattern
Look for contrasts: religious vs. worldly buildings, pious vs. luxurious clothes, remembered vs. forgotten rulers. What common factor makes some things last?
3
Understand the derived principle
Al-Razi concludes that everything related to the Divine persists, while everything related to matter perishes. This is the core of the induction.
4
Apply the principle to the soul
If the rational soul is enlightened by knowledge of God, it acquires a strong relation to the Divine and must therefore persist eternally.
5
Reflect on the argument's nature
Recognize that this is an inductive generalization, not a strict logical proof. It offers strong presumption but not certainty.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الخامسة : إنا نشاهد أن كل بناء بني للخير والدين ، فإنه يبقى زماناً طويلاً مع ضعفه ورخاوته ، وكل بناء بني للدنيا مثل قصور الملوك والأكابر ، فإنه ينهدم في أسرع مدة مع غاية قوته [ وإنا نرى أهل الزهد والطاعة والعلم ، يبقى ذكرهم بعد موتهم دهراً داهراً وزمانا طويلا (۱) ] وأيضاً : نرى أن ثياب أهل الزهد والطاعة والعلم تبقى بعد موتهم دهراً داهراً وزماناً طويلاً وتبقى آثارهم وبلياتهم زماناً طويلاً (۲) ] مع أنها تكون في غاية الرخاوة والضعف . ولقد رأيت سجادة متخذة من الخرق الضعيفة ، بقيت بعد موت صاحبها مائة وعشرين سنة ، بل نقول : بقاء ثوب رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم ، بقي نيفاً وستمائة سنة . وأما ثياب الملوك والسلاطين فإنها مع غاية شرفها وصفاقتها ، تفنى في أقل مدة . وأيضاً : نرى الرجل العالم أو الزاهد مع فقره وغاية ضعفه التفات الناس إليه ، يبقى ذكره [ بعد موته مئات وألوفاً من السنين ، وأما الجبابرة والسلاطين فإنه لا يبقى ذكرهم (۳) ] في الدنيا إلا أياماً قلائل . ثم إنها تنقرض وتزول ، ولا يبقى منهم في الدنيا لا أثر ولا خبر وعدم إذا عرفت هذا فنقول : هذا الاستقراء يدل على أن كل ما له تعلق بحضرة الجلال ، فإنه يبقى بحسب قوة ذلك التعلق ، وكل ما له تعلق بعالم الجسم والهيولى (4) ، فإنه يكون سريع الدثور والفناء . إذا ثبت هذا فنقول : إن النفس الناطقة إذا أشرقت بنور معرفة واجب الوجود ، وتقوت بتلك القوة التي لا نهاية لها في العدة والشدة والمرة ، وجب أن تبقى بقاء مصوناً عن الفساد ، دائماً بدوام السبع الشداد. وتمام التقريب : إني لما رأيت نظرت تلك السجادة المركبة من تلك الخرق السخيفة ، وأنها بقيت مدة مائة وعشرين سنة ، قلت في نفسي : امتياز هذه الخرق السخيفة بهذا البقاء عن سائر الثياب الصفيقة النفيسة ، إنما حصل لأجل أنه اتفق أنه وقع عليها رجل ذلك الرجل الذي كان مدبر بدنه : نفساً أشرقت بنور معرفة الله تعالى فلاجل هذا التعلق البعيد ،
The fifth proof: We observe that every building built for goodness and religion remains a long time despite its weakness and flimsiness, while every building built for the world, like the palaces of kings and nobles, collapses in the shortest time despite its extreme strength. And we see the people of asceticism, obedience, and knowledge—their remembrance remains after their death for ages and a long time. Also: We see that the clothes of the people of asceticism, obedience, and knowledge remain after their death for ages and a long time, and their traces and trials remain a long time, even though they are extremely soft and weak. And I saw a prayer rug made of weak rags; it remained after the death of its owner for one hundred and twenty years. Indeed, we say: The survival of the garment of the Messenger of God (peace be upon him) remained for more than six hundred years. As for the clothes of kings and sultans, despite their utmost honor and thickness, they perish in the shortest time. Also: We see the scholar or ascetic, despite his poverty and extreme weakness, and people's attention to him, his remembrance remains after his death for hundreds and thousands of years. As for the tyrants and sultans, their remembrance does not remain in the world except for a few days, then they pass away and vanish, leaving no trace or news. If you have understood this, we say: This induction indicates that everything related to the presence of majesty remains according to the strength of that relation, and everything related to the world of body and matter is quickly effaced and perishes. If this is established, we say: When the rational soul is illumined by the light of knowledge of the Necessary Existent and strengthened with that power which has no limit in number, intensity, and force, it must survive a survival preserved from corruption, permanently with the permanence of the seven firmaments. And the complete approximation: When I saw and looked at that prayer rug composed of those mean rags, and that it remained for a duration of one hundred and twenty years, I said to myself: The distinction of these mean rags in this survival over other thick and valuable clothes is only because it happened that a man—who was the manager of his body—a soul illumined with the light of knowledge of God, Most High, came upon it. Hence, for this remote relation,
Source pages p. 230

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth proof for the persistence of the soul. It is an inductive (istiqrāʾ) argument based on empirical observation: things connected to goodness and religion—like buildings for religious purposes, the reputation and clothing of pious people, and the Prophet's garment—last longer than worldly things. The author extrapolates that this is because of their relation to the Divine majesty. He then applies this principle to the rational soul: if the soul is illuminated by knowledge of God, it will partake in that lastingness. The argument uses concrete examples (a prayer rug lasting 120 years, the Prophet's garment lasting 600 years) to ground the claim. The passage ends mid-sentence, but the argument's direction is clear: the remote connection of the rug to the saintly person caused its longevity, analogously the soul's direct knowledge of God ensures its persistence.

Correction: It is an inductive generalization that yields only a strong presumption, not logical certainty.

The Guest and the Mosquito: Strength from the Unseen
An anecdote illustrating how the unseen world strengthens the soul against bodily harm.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the light of the unseen world strengthens the soul to overcome bodily fears.

Core Idea
Connection to the unseen grants the soul strength beyond the body.

This lesson uses a story of a guest and a mosquito to illustrate a profound spiritual principle: the soul's fearlessness comes from its connection to the divine unseen world. The anecdote serves as a concrete example before drawing a general conclusion about the soul at death.

Guided Path
1
Read the anecdote carefully
Read the story of the guest and the mosquito, noting the contrast between the two nights.
Pay attention to the shift from fearlessness to distress.
2
Identify the principle
After the story, the author extracts a principle: the light of the unseen strengthens the spirit and gives it dominance over the body. Find this statement.
3
Apply to the soul at death
The author applies the principle to the moment of death. Understand why the soul becomes fearless of bodily destruction.
4
Reflect on the lesson
Consider how this teaching can affect your own understanding of the soul's relationship with the body and the unseen.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تلتفت في الليلة الماضية إلى السباع التي أحاطت بك ، وفي هذه الليلة أظهرت التوجع من هذه البعوضة . فقال : أما الليلة الماضية فقد جاءني ضيف كريم ، وسلطان عظيم [ من عالم الغيب (۱) ] فلأجل قوته ما كنت أبالي بتلك السباع . وأما في هذه الليلة فأنا وحدي مع نفسي ، فلا أطيق تحمل إيذاء هذه البعوضة . فثبت بما ذكرنا : أن ظهور نور عالم الغيب يقوي جوهر الروح ، ويفيد النفس استيلاء على عالم الجسم. وإذا ثبت هذا ، فجوهر النفس عند القرب من الموت ، لا بد وأن يلتجيء إلى الله تعالى ، فتحصل المكاشفة ، فيقوى جوهر النفس قوة لا تبالي بخراب كل العالم، فكيف تبالي بخراب البدن الضعيف
You turned last night to the wild beasts that surrounded you, and tonight you showed distress from this mosquito. He said: As for last night, a noble guest came to me, and a great sovereign from the world of the unseen, so because of his strength I did not care about those wild beasts. As for tonight, I am alone with myself, so I cannot bear the harm of this mosquito. So it is established by what we have mentioned: that the appearance of the light of the world of the unseen strengthens the substance of the spirit, and gives the soul dominance over the world of the body. And if this is established, then the substance of the soul at the approach of death must necessarily seek refuge in God Almighty, so unveiling occurs, and the substance of the soul becomes strong with a strength that does not care about the destruction of the entire world, so how would it care about the destruction of the weak body?
Source pages p. 233

Commentary
This passage is an illustrative anecdote followed by a derived principle. It begins with a question ('You turned last night to the wild beasts...') where a person is reminded of his fearlessness when a noble guest (representing the unseen world) was with him, contrasted with his distress from a mosquito when alone. The reply explains that the guest's strength from the unseen world made him disregard the beasts. The author then draws the conclusion: the appearance of the light of the unseen world strengthens the substance of the spirit and gives the soul dominance over the body. He applies this to the soul at death: the soul must seek refuge in God, obtaining unveiling (mukāshafa), and becomes so strong that it does not care about the destruction of the entire world, let alone the weak body. The passage thus uses a concrete example to argue that the soul, when connected to the divine, transcends bodily concerns.

The Ninth Proof: Soul Does Not Die with Body
A logical proof for the soul's immortality via reductio

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical proof that the soul cannot be more dependent than the body, and thus does not perish with it.

Core Idea
If the soul perished with the body, it would be more base—an impossibility.

This lesson presents a crisp reductio ad absurdum from the classical tradition. The author argues that assuming the soul dies with the body leads to a contradiction: the soul would be more base than the body, yet we know the body is base without the soul. This logical move is a cornerstone of the argument for the soul's immortality.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the conditional structure: 'if... then...'
2
Identify the two types of need
The passage distinguishes the soul's need for the body (for its essence) from the body's need for the soul (for its attributes). Compare them.
The body needs the soul for its temperamental qualities, not for its existence.
3
Understand the comparative logic
The argument states that greater need implies greater baseness. Ask yourself: why is that a reasonable premise?
This is a conceptual premise drawn from observations of dependence.
4
See the self-evident truth
The passage points out that the body with the soul is noble, and without the soul it is extremely base. This is presented as undeniable.
This fact contradicts the earlier conclusion that the soul would be more base.
5
Conclude the proof
Since the assumption (soul dies with body) leads to a false conclusion, the assumption is false. Therefore, the soul does not die with the body.
This is a classic reductio ad absurdum.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة التاسعة : لو فنيت النفس بفناء الجسد ، لكانت النفس محتاجة إلى البدن ، في بقاء ذاتها ، وأما الجسد فهو محتاج إلى النفس لا في بقاء ذاته ، بل في بقاء صفاته. وهي : الكيفيات المزاجية وعلى هذا التقدير فيكون احتياج النفس إلى البدن ، أكثر من احتياج البدن إلى النفس . وما كان أكثر حاجة ، كان أخر مما هو أقل حاجة . فلو ماتت النفس بموت البدن ، لزم كون النفس أخس من البدن . وهذا باطل . لأن الجسد مع النفس يكون شريفاً ، وبدون النفس يكون في غاية الخساسة . فثبت : أن النفس لا تموت بموت البدن
The ninth proof: If the soul perished with the perishing of the body, then the soul would be in need of the body for the survival of its essence, while the body is in need of the soul not for the survival of its essence, but for the survival of its attributes, namely the temperamental qualities. On this assumption, the soul's need for the body would be greater than the body's need for the soul. And that which has greater need is more base than that which has lesser need. So if the soul died with the death of the body, it would necessitate that the soul is more base than the body. But this is false, because the body with the soul is noble, and without the soul it is extremely base. So it is established: that the soul does not die with the death of the body.
Source pages p. 233

Commentary
This passage is a logical proof (ḥujja) for the soul's immortality. The author presents a conditional: if the soul perished with the body, then the soul would depend on the body for its own existence (since it would have no existence apart from it), whereas the body depends on the soul only for its attributes (the temperamental qualities). Thus the soul's need is greater. What has greater need is more base (akhas) than what has less need. Therefore the soul would be more base than the body. But experience shows the opposite: the body with the soul is noble, and without the soul it is utterly base. Hence the conclusion is false, so the soul does not die with the body. The argument uses a reductio ad absurdum: assuming the soul dies with the body leads to a self-evidently false conclusion, so the assumption is false.

Key Terms
النفس
الجسد
احتياج
الكيفيات المزاجية
أخس
شريف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the soul's need for the body is purely physical, like the body's need for the soul.
Correction: The argument focuses on the soul's need for the body in preserving its own essence, which is different from the body's need for the soul only for its attributes (temperamental qualities).

Checkpoint
What does the ninth proof conclude?
A) The soul is more base than the body
B) The soul dies with the death of the body
C) The soul does not die with the death of the body
D) The body does not need the soul

Key Takeaway
The soul does not perish with the body because assuming so leads to a self-evident absurdity: the soul would be more base than the body, which contradicts the observed nobility of the body with the soul.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believed your soul will never die, how would that change what you fear today? What would you start doing now that you might be putting off?
?
Your body will decay, but your soul will not. Which one are you currently feeding more—your body’s desires or your soul’s purpose? Be honest: where does your daily effort go?
?
The proof shows that without the soul, the body is extremely base. In what areas of your life is your body ‘ruling’ your soul instead of the other way around? What is one concrete step you will take tonight to reverse that?

Death: A Change of Qualities, Not Soul's Annihilation
A logical proof that death does not annihilate the soul

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical argument that death of the body is merely a change in bodily qualities, and why this does not entail the soul's destruction.

Core Idea
Death is a change in the body's qualities, not the end of the soul.

In this passage, the author presents the tenth proof for the soul's immortality. He defines death as the removal of temperamental qualities and balanced accidents from the body. Then he uses a clever comparison: these qualities are more strongly attached to the body than to the soul, yet their removal does not annihilate the body. So, a fortiori, their removal cannot annihilate the soul, which is only distantly connected to them. This argument strengthens the understanding that the soul survives bodily death.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage begins with 'The tenth proof' – it is part of a series.
2
Understand the definition of death
The author defines death of the body as a change in qualities, specifically the removal of temperamental quality and balanced accidents. This is not annihilation.
These qualities (الصفات) are accidents that subsist in the body.
3
Follow the a fortiori argument
Compare the attachments: the accidents are more strongly tied to the body than to the soul. Since their removal does not annihilate the body (closer connection), it should not annihilate the soul (distant connection).
This is a qiyas al-awla (all the more reason) argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة العاشرة : إن موت البدن عبارة عن تغير الصفات القائمة بتلك الأجسام ، فإن موت البدن لا معنى له إلا زوال تلك الكيفية المزاجية ، وزوال تلك الأعراض المعتدلة . فنقول : تعلق تلك الأعراض بتلك الأجسام ، أكمل وأقوى من تعلقها بالنفس المتصرفة في تلك الأجسام . ولما لم يكن زوال تلك الأعراض فناء تلك الأجسام (۲) مع ما بينها من التعلق القريب فلان لا يكون موجبا فناء النفس مع ما بينهما من التعلق البعيد ، كان ذلك أولى
The tenth proof: The death of the body is an expression of a change in the attributes subsisting in those bodies, for the death of the body has no meaning except the removal of that temperamental quality and the removal of those balanced accidents. We say: The attachment of those accidents to those bodies is more perfect and stronger than their attachment to the soul that governs in those bodies. And since the removal of those accidents is not the annihilation of those bodies, despite the close connection between them, then all the more reason that it should not cause the annihilation of the soul, given the distant connection between them. That is more appropriate.
Source pages p. 233

Commentary
This passage is another logical proof for the soul's immortality. The author defines death of the body as a mere change: the removal of temperamental qualities and balanced accidents. He then compares the relationship: these accidents are more strongly attached to the bodies themselves than to the soul (which only governs the body). Since the removal of these accidents does not annihilate the body (even though they are closely tied), a fortiori (all the more reason) their removal should not annihilate the soul, which is only distantly connected to those accidents. The argument uses a comparison of degrees of attachment: the closer something is, the more likely its removal affects the substrate; therefore, since the accidents are closer to the body and their removal does not destroy the body, the removal of these accidents should have even less effect on the soul, which is further removed.

Key Terms
موت البدن
الصفات
الكيفية المزاجية
الأعراض المعتدلة
تعلق
فناء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that death is the destruction of the entire person.
Correction: This proof shows death is only a change in bodily qualities, which does not affect the soul's existence.

Checkpoint

B) The body's death is the removal of qualities, which does not necessitate the soul's annihilation
C) The body and soul are equally affected by death
D) The soul's attachment to the body is stronger than the body's attachment to its own qualities

Key Takeaway
Death is a change in the body's qualities, and because those qualities are more strongly attached to the body than to the soul, their removal cannot annihilate the soul.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If death is only a change in the body's qualities and not your end, how does that reshape the way you face your own mortality? Will you live differently knowing that your soul will persist? What specific fear or attachment to this world does this truth expose in your heart?
?
The proof says the soul's attachment to the body's accidents is distant. Reflect on your own life: are you more attached to the changing qualities of your body (health, appearance, comfort) than to the soul that will outlast them? What concrete step can you take today to prioritize your soul's cultivation over your body's fleeting conditions?
?

The Soul's Prior Possibility and Its Matter
Explaining that the prior possibility of the soul's origination has the body as its matter.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the prior possibility of the soul's origination differs from the possibility after corruption, and why each requires a different matter.

Core Idea
The soul's prior possibility uses the body as matter; the possibility after corruption requires matter in the soul itself.

In this passage, the author responds to an objection that the soul's origination requires a prior possibility, which must have matter. He distinguishes two kinds of possibility: one before origination, which uses the body, and one after corruption, which needs a matter within the soul itself. This clarifies that the soul is composed of matter and form.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its translation. Identify the objection and the author's initial response.
The objection claims that if the soul is originated, its prior possibility needs matter.
2
Identify the two possibilities
The author distinguishes two types of possibility: prior possibility before origination and possibility after corruption. Note the different matters required for each.
Prior possibility uses the body; post-corruption possibility cannot use the body.
3
Understand why the body fails for post-corruption possibility
The body does not remain after corruption, so it cannot serve as matter for the soul's possibility of corruption. Hence, the soul itself must have a material substrate.
This shows the soul is composite (matter and form).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما السؤال الثالث : وهو قوله : ( إذا كانت النفس محدثة ، فالإمكان السابق على حدوثها ، وجب أن يفتقر إلى المادة ، فجوابه : إن ذلك الإمكان مفتقر إلى مادة ، ومادته هي البدن ، والبدن باق حال ذلك الحدوث ، فلم تحصل الحاجة إلى إثبات مادة الجوهر النفس . وأما الإمكان الحادث بعد الفساد، فلا بد له من محل ومادة . وذلك المحل والمادة ، يمتنع أن يكون هو البدن . لأنه غير باق ، بعد الفساد [ فوجب إثبات مادة لذات النفس ولجوهرها ، حتى يصير محلاً لإمكان الفساد ] فظهر الفرق .
As for the third question: and it is his statement: 'If the soul is originated (muhdatha), then the possibility prior to its origination must need matter.' So its answer: That possibility needs matter, and its matter is the body, and the body remains at the time of that origination, so there is no need to prove a matter for the substance of the soul. As for the possibility that occurs after corruption, it must have a locus and matter. And that locus and matter cannot be the body, because it does not remain after corruption. [So it is necessary to prove a matter for the essence of the soul and for its substance, so that it becomes a locus for the possibility of corruption.] So the difference appears.
Source pages p. 239

Commentary
This passage addresses the third objection: if the soul is originated, its prior possibility must need matter. The author answers that the prior possibility indeed needs matter, but that matter is the body, which exists at the time of origination, so no separate matter for the soul's substance is needed then. However, the possibility that arises after corruption (i.e., the possibility of the soul's own corruption) must have a locus and matter. That matter cannot be the body because the body is no longer present after corruption. Therefore, a matter for the soul's essence must be established, making it composed of matter and form. Thus, the difference between the two possibilities is clarified: the prior possibility uses the body as matter, but the post-corruption possibility requires a matter inherent in the soul itself.

Key Terms
إمكان
حدوث
مادة
بدن
نفس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that the soul does not require any matter for its possibility at all.
Correction: The passage shows that while the prior possibility of the soul's origination uses the body as matter, the possibility after corruption requires a matter inherent in the soul itself, indicating that the soul is composed of matter and form.

Checkpoint
Why, according to the answer, is the body not the matter for the possibility of corruption after the soul's corruption?
A) Because the body is eternal.
B) Because the body does not remain after corruption.
C) Because the soul does not need matter.
D) Because the possibility is prior to origination.

Key Takeaway
The prior possibility of the soul's origination is housed in the body, but the possibility of the soul's corruption requires a matter within the soul itself, proving the soul is composite.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
What would it mean for you if, after death, your soul still carries a 'matter' from your deeds and intentions? How does that affect how you live today?
?
The author argues that the body serves as matter for the soul's origination. In what ways are you using your body as a 'substrate' for your soul's development? Are you neglecting this responsibility?

Refuting the Circularity in the Soul's Attributes
Refuting a circular objection to the soul's attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the soul's attributes cannot be conditioned on the vanishing form at death, and how circular reasoning invalidates the objection.

Core Idea
If both sides depend on each other, neither stands — circularity collapses the argument.

The objection attempts to make the soul's attributes conditional on a physical form that vanishes at death. The author responds by revealing a hidden circularity: the soul's connection to the body is itself conditioned on those attributes. This lesson unpacks that logical trap.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
Read the objector's claim: why not allow the vanishing form as a condition for the soul's attributes? Note what the objector is trying to avoid.
The objector suggests that the soul's attributes come from the body's form.
2
Follow the author's answer
The author denies that the attributes can be a condition for the soul's connection (ta'alluq) to the body, because the connection itself presupposes the attributes.
3
Recognize the circularity
See how if attributes depend on connection and connection depends on attributes, we get a vicious circle. Understand why this is invalid in logic.
This is the core of the refutation.
4
Conclude the proof
The author concludes that the objection fails, and the original proof of the soul's incorruptibility stands.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما السؤال الرابع : وهو قوله : « لم لا يجوز أن تكون الصورة الزائلة عند موت البدن ، شرطاً لكون ذلك الجوهر القائم بالنفس موصوفاً بالحياة والعلم والقدرة ؟ ، فجوابه : إن كون النفس موصوفة بالحياة والعلم والقدرة ، لا يجوز أن يكون شرطاً لتعلقها بالبدن . وذلك لأن تعلقها مشروط بكونها في ذاتها حية عالمة قادرة . فلو كان انصافها بهذه الصفات ، مشروط بتعلق النفس بالبدن ، لزم افتقار كل واحد منهما إلى الآخر وذلك دور . وهو باطل . فهذا تمام الكلام في تقرير هذا الدليل [ والله أعلم ]
As for the fourth question: and it is his statement: 'Why is it not permissible that the form which vanishes at the death of the body be a condition for that substance subsisting in the soul to be described by life, knowledge, and power?' So its answer: The soul being described by life, knowledge, and power cannot be a condition for its connection (ta'alluq) to the body. Because its connection is conditioned by it being in its essence living, knowing, and powerful. So if its being qualified by these attributes were conditioned by the soul's connection to the body, then each of them would need the other, and that is a circle (dawr). And it is invalid. So this is the completion of the discussion in establishing this proof. [And God knows best.]
Source pages p. 239

Commentary
This passage answers a fourth objection: why not allow that the vanishing form at death is a condition for the soul's substance to be described by life, knowledge, and power? The author replies that the soul's being described by those attributes cannot be a condition for its connection to the body, because the connection itself is conditioned by the soul being in its essence living, knowing, and powerful. If the attributes were conditioned by the connection, then each would depend on the other—a circular dependency (دور). Since circular reasoning is invalid, the objection fails. This concludes the establishment of the proof of the soul's incorruptibility.

Key Terms
صورة زائلة
شرط
تعلق
دور
نفس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The soul's attributes (life, knowledge, power) are acquired after the soul connects to the body, and the vanishing body form is a condition for those attributes.
Correction: The soul's connection to the body is itself conditioned by the soul being inherently living, knowing, and powerful. Thus the attributes cannot be conditioned on the connection or the body's form.

Checkpoint
What logical fallacy does the author identify in the fourth objection?
A) Infinite regress
B) Circular reasoning
C) False analogy
D) Begging the question

Key Takeaway
The soul's life, knowledge, and power are essential and not conditional on the body's vanishing form.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
